THE RED PAGE.
COPYRIGHT
IF, contrary to intelligent expectation, the
House of Representatives should pro-
ceed to discussion of the Copyright
Bill before the measure has been
threshed thoroughly by a Select Committee,
it is respectfully suggested that the following
amendments should be made in the interest
of Australia.
Clause 5 to read :
. For the purpose of this Act publication, per-
formance, or delivery in the Commonwealth shall
be deemed to be simultaneous with publication,
performance, or delivery elsewhere, if the period
between the publications, performances, or de-
liveries do not exceed thirty days.
The Bill says fourteen days. It will not hurt us to con-
cede sixteen more.
Clause 13/2 to read :
Copyright shall subsist in every book which has
been printed and published or reprinted and repub-
lished in Australia before or simultaneously with
its first publication elsewhere, and has been regis-
tered within thirty days of such publication or
republication.
British copyright, according to Canadian ruling's, will
subsist independently. Nevertheless it is desirable to
establish and as far as possible to enforce a septi ate
Australian copyright status. '
There is a story of an Auier. rural magistrate, in the
happy days of barter, refusing matrimony to a pair of
backwoods candidates. “No, John!” he said; “no,
Melindy!—my fee is twenty pounds of beeswax. That
lump is five pounds short.” The downcast couple retired,
but at the door a bright thought struck the nymph, who
whispered to the swain, who returned sturdily to the
attack. “ Say, mister,” he said, “ can’t you marry us as
far as the wax will stick} ”
Though our legislation may not go all the way, we can
still enact it and let it go “ as far as the wax will stick. ”4'
J*
Add new clauses as follows, adapted from
the Canadian Act, 63 and 64 Vic., c. 25 :
If it is proved to the satis!action of the Minister
that the owner of the copyright of a book, pub-
lished in any part of the British dominions, has
lawfully granted a license to reproduce in Aus-
tralia, from movable or other types, or from
stereotype plates, or from electroplates, or from
lithograph stones, or by any process for facsimile
reproduction, an edition or editions of such book
designed for sale only in Australia, the Minister
may, notwithstanding anything in this or other
Acts, bv order under his hand, prohibit the im-
portation, except with the written consent of the
licensee, into Australia of any copies of such book
printed elsewhere ; provided that two such copies
may be specially imported for the bona fide use of
any public free library, or any university or
college library, or for the library of any duly in-
corporated institution or society for the use of the
members of such institution or society.
The Minister may at any time in like manner,
by order under his hand, suspend or revoke such
prohibition upon importation if it is proved to his
satisfaction that —
(a.) the license to reproduce in Australia has
terminated or expired ; or-
(b.) the reasonable demand for the boolc in
Australia is not sufficiently met without impor-
tation ; or
(c.) the book is not, having regard to the de-
mand therefor in Australia, being suitably printed
or published; or
(d.) any other state of things exists on account
of which it is not in the public interest to further
prohibit importation.
At any time after the importation of a book has
been so prohibited, any person resident or being-
in Australia may apply, either directly or through
a bookseller or other agent, to the person so
licensed to reproduce such book, for a copy of any
edition of such book then on sale and reasonably
obtainable in any part of the British dominions,
and it shall then be the duty of the person so
licensed, as soon as reasonably may be, to import
and sell such copy to the person so applying
therefor, at the ordinary selling price of such copy
in such part of the British dominions, with the
duty and reasonable forwarding charges added;
and the failure or neglect, without lawful excuse,
of the person so licensed to supply such copy
within a reasonable time, shall be a reason for
which the Minister may, if he sees fit, suspend or
revoke the prohibition upon importation.
Add a new clause adapted from the U.S.
Act of March 3, 1905
Whenever the author or proprietor of a book
which shall be published beyond Australia before
the day of publication in this country, or his
executors, administrators, or assigns, shall de-
posit with the Registrar one complete copy of
the same, including all maps and other illustra-
tions, within thirty days after the first publication
of such book beyond Australia, and shall insert in
such copy, and in all copies of such book sold or
distributed in Australia, on the title page or
the page immediately following, a notice of the
reservation of copyright in the name of the pro-
prietor, together with the true date of first publica-
tion of such book, in the following words:
“Published . nineteen hundred and
Privilege of copyright in Australia reserved under
the Act approved , nineteen hundred and
five, by .” and shall within twelve
months after the first publication of such book
beyond Australia, register such book and deposit
two copies of it that have been printed and
published or reprinted and republished in Aus-
tralia, then notwithstanding anything in this Act
copyright shall subsist in such book in the same
manner as if it had been printed and published
or reprinted and republished in Australia before
or simultaneously with its first publication else-
where ; but in no case shall such copyright continue
to exist after it has expired in the country of
origin.
Canadian law (Revised Statutes of 1880, c. 62, sec. 13)
makes similar provision for interim protection, hut' with
less liberal conditions Clause 65 of the Commonwealth
Act maybe amended to provide a Register of Interim
Copyrights. Clause 63 Should be expunged.
%
These amendmehts will involve the appli-
cation to Australia of the British Act of
1847, which was applied to Canada in 1868
as a part of the copyright law enacted irame-
diately after confederation, and has been in
force ever since. Following is an abstract
of tire Canadian Act (31 Vic", c. 56) :
. W hereas the British Act of 1847 provides that
in case the legislature of any British Possession
shall make provision for protecting the rights of
British authors the Crown may suspend the pro-
hibitions of the British Acts against the importa-
tion into and sale in such possession' of British
copyright books, therefore it is enacted :
ft shall he lawful to impose an ad valorem duty
not exceeding twenty per cent, (which may be
altered from time to time so that it does not
exceed this rate, and shall be collected as a duty of
Customs) upon foreign reprints of British copy-
right i woks.
Upon that a British Order in Council will
follow, similar to that made for Canada on
July 7, 1868, and still in force, suspending
the prohibitions of the British Acts.
An amendment of the Commonwealth
Customs Act is also involved, and may or
may not follow the Canadian Act 49 Vic.
c. 4, which declares as follows :
Dutiable Goods. — 33. Books, printed periodi-
cals and pamphlets, not elsewhere specified, not
being foreign reprints of British copyright works
nor blank account books, nor copy books, nor
books to be written or drawn upon, nor Bibles,
prayer-books, psalm and hymn-books, fifteen per
cent, ad valorem.
34. British copyright works, reprints of, fifteen
per cent, ad valorem, and in addition thereto
twelve and a-half per cent, ad valorem.
Free G00n5. —549. Books, bound, which have
been printed more than seven years at the date of
importation, except foreign reprints of English
copyrighted books, which shall remain subject to
the copyright duty ;
Prohibited Imports. —Bl4. Reprints of Cana-
' dian copyright works, and reprints of British
copyright works which have oeen also copy-
righted in Canada ;...
_ #
There is nothing in these amendments
which conflicts with British law. They
accord with British law, have been adopted
by Canada, and have been sanctioned by
Britain.
It is less easy to consider and pass these
amendments than to swallow the present
Bill holus-bolus, all in a lump. But there is
no hurry ; and the Commonwealth Parlia-
ment wants to make a good job. The
hard way leads to a law for the benefit of
Australia. The easy way leads to a law
for the benefit of Tom and Dick in London,
Hans in Leipzig, and Muckabuekajinjam in
Timbuctoo.
The Commonwealth Bill is clearly suscep-
tible of amendment in other ways which
there is no space to point out.
The Commonwealth Bill should be referred
to a Select Committee which, if it doubt the
efficacy of the Canadian plan, can collect
evidence by writing to Canada. Why not
take advantage of the elder federation’s 33
years’ more experience ?
We can very well wait a year for copy-
right legislation. Wby risk a bungle by
rushing it through a Parliament which does
not yet understand it ?
+.
A Health Reader , by J. S. Elkington, Tas.
public health officer, is published by Whitcombe
and Tombs (Melb. ; Is. (>c/.). The book is designed
for Australasian schools, and is written sensibly
and' well, Following a good rule, illustrations by
N. Lindsay enforce the text. The author deals
plainly with food and drink, air and baths and
clothing, tobacco and alcohol; and adds a few
cooking recipes and chapters on infectious
diseases and care of babies. Tjie lt3ons for
teachers, upon school hygiene, . would have
been placed more fitly in a separate book :
the subject deserves such a text-book written
for this country, with a criticism of school
buildings and a glance at the tropics. For
the children one would like some notes on the
hygiene of reading and writing, and the care of
the eyes. Games and gymnastics are worth a
chapter. Stress might be laid on the im-
portance of regular meals, “early to bed and
early to rise,” and sunlight for study; and the
remarks on the nutritive value of different foods,
and the childish use of tea, coffee, coooa, would
bear extension. As the book stands, however, it
is praiseworthy; and the printing is excellent,
though the details of binding can be improved.
Occasionally the author is inaccurate or dogmatic. The
victims of the Black Hole of Calcutta were not all
“ killed by the Carbonic Acid Gas from their own breath ’’
(16). The remarks about the necessity of scrubbing the
natural oil out of the skin (30) are doubtfully sound ; and
the advice to take warm baths should be safeguarded.—
One doubts whether it is worth while telling people to
drink boiled water in this country. It would probably
pay better, where there is not a good water supply, to
inculcate the Chinese habit of perpetual weak tea. Cocoa
might be commended as preferable.—The statements
about alcohol are probably expedient, but they are doubt-
fully true. —The advice to get vaccinated twice is bad in a
country where there is no smallpox. It would be just as
foolish to inoculate the whole population against plague.
Many Australian amateurs in photography will
desire to try their hands at the gum-bichromate
process. An excellent little handbook, written
by ,). C. Richards, has just lieen published by
fliffe & Sons (Loud. ; 2s. Gd. net). The advice is
illustrated liberally with plates of subjects in
several stages'. The writer closes :
To workers who, whilst endowed with the instincts and
enthusiasm of the trained artist, lack the technical skill
that would enable them to make, efficient use of the
pencil or brush, the gum process appeals as yielding the
ne plus ultra of artistic expression by mechanical means...
I do claim for photography that, ’in the future, if it is
aided by this sympathetic process, it can, and must, rise
to greater heights than those yet reached, and than could
ever have have been possible with a purely chemical and
arbitrary medium.
In Gidcstia and Other Australian Poems, by W.
H. Yarrington (Lond., Elliot Btock ; 3s. (id.), are
republished the moral and didactic verses of a
Sydney clergyman... and,it is necessary to say, as
courteously as possible, that this Pegasus has not
won the Cup.
N.S.W. PUBLIC LIBRARY
IT appears from a statement made in N.S.W.
Assembly on 7 Nov. by Premier Carruthers,
that in addition to the gift of his library col-
loction Mr. Mitchell proposes to make
N.S. Wales residuary legatee of his property,
“ which amounts to a very large sum. Indeed,
so large will be the benefit to the State that there
will be sufficient money provided from the annual
interest to maintain all the public libraries in
New South Wales.” This is Premier Carruthers’
statement, dependent on Mr. Mitchells state-
ment. The details are not given. The con-
ditions are not given. Apparently no deed
guarantees the gift or bequest. Mr. Mitchell
is apparently free to revoke either at any time.
Yet upon the Premier’s affirmation of a con-
tingent benefit N.S.W. Assembly voted to
approve the borrowing of 120,000/. in order to
house a collection which, until recently, was con-
tained entirely in Mr. Mitchell’s private house,
and for which apparently an adequate building
can be provided at a cost of from 10,000/. to
15,000/.
The procedure is not business-like. The ex-
penditure of such a large sum of money should not
be authorised with such scanty information, with
apparently so little real security. Nevertheless
the procedure may be “good business.” It may
be a case of doing the right thing in the wrong
way. One is quite prepared to believe that Mr.
Mitchell will justify the least word attributed
to him, and will give all that he has been
represented as willing to give.
J*
Yet this remains. When there was a straight
way of doing business the Government should not
have chosen the crooked way. When there was
a rational method of procedure the Government
should not have chosen the irrational method.
Parliament should not bo asked to rely upon the
hearsa; -; of Premier Carruthers. There should
have been placed before it a definite and recent
proposal in writing from Mr. Mitchell, embodying
all the intentions which Premier Carruthers attri-
butes to him, stating all the conditions and con-
tingencies, and formally guaranteeing the gift
when the conditions are complied with.
The sinister feature of the matter is the way in
which the admitted necessity of providing for the
Mitchell gift has been used to induce Parliament
to vote a sum of money greatly disproportionate
to that needed for complying with the ccaiditions
of the gift. Mr. Mitchell has been used as a
stalking-horse to secure an expensive new build-
ing for a public library, to be erected frpm plans
prepared without competition upon a site which
is doubtfully the best available. The tactic is
discreditable. "The public library supplies its
own justification, and can stand upon its own
merits. Though there be need of prompt action
to secure the Mitchell gift, there is no reason why
a new library should be built wituout full con-
sideration ; and the issues should have been Dut
fairly before Parliament and the public.
«**
H. C. Anderson, N.S.W. Public Librarian,
who has been reported recently as making several
foolish statements, was reported during the de-
bate a» making this one.:
“ I thought that as Sydney is as big- as many American
cities, it ought to have a library as big as theirs, and I
planned one accordingly.”
The saying may be added to other sayings and per-
formances which have raised considerable doubt as
to this gentleman’s fitness to occupy his present
position. The public value of a library does not con-
sist in the bigness of the building which contains it.
The building is a necessary adjunct. The books
are a necessary adjunct. But the value of a
library depends upon its practical utility, upon
its living inspiration ; and these things do not-
depend upon the piling of stones or the accumu-
lation of curiosities. While the education given
to children in N.S.W. schools is inferior because
the salaries paid are so mean that the best
men and women cannot be obtained as teachers ;
while the annual vote to N.S.W. public library is
so starved or misapplied that the trustees are un-
able to supply the deficiencies in the library and
to make adequate purchases of new books ; while
the-needs of the library or the capacities of the
librarian to supply theiri are so lightly held that
Premier Carruthers can transfer the librarian to
another and entirely different field of duties : it is
clear that the enthusiastic expenditure of borrowed
money on “big” buildings, without the fullest
consideration of the definite uses to which they
may be devoted, has nothing to recommend it.
- - - ♦- -
The Parliament of Beasts and Other Verses, by
“Rip Van Winkle” (Bond.; R. A. Thompson ;
2s. Gd. net.), entitles a little book hailing from
Capetown, and criticising men and things in light
satiric measures, not always well handled. The
topics are too local for Australian amusement;
yet occasionally the writer (D. Colvin, of The
Gape Times) exhibits a fresh and pretty senti-
ment that may be earnest of better things. The
sestet of a dedicatory sonnet is noticeable ;
Some happy olden story would 1 tell,
Or sad romaunt wrought of a poet’s sighs.
Then list for your soul’s answer, as one lies
Upon the brink of a deep, silent well
Wherein long moments since a pebble fell,
And hearken as the sounding echoes >~ise,
So tears would ripple up into your eyes
Or laughter ringing like a golden bell.
A golden bell ; but swung from what sad deeps
Even so a trembling water lily makes
' Through glimmering floods he r wavering pilgrimage,
Until at last, a golden crown she takes
And, samite robes, but still her glad heart keeps
The memory of her silent a nchorage.
A. E. Housman (of A Shropshire Lad) edits the
classics with quite an Eighteenth Century twang. In a
just-published recension of Juvenal he writes of one hated
opponent and his detested descendant: “ I imagine that
Mr. Buecheler, when he first perused Mr. Sudhaus’ edition
of the ‘ Aetna,’ must have felt something like Sin when
she gave birth to Death.”
MEMORANDA
Minister Groom, introducing the Copyright
Bill to the Representatives, said that the Infperi 1
Foreign Reprints Act.of 1847 “was not successful
in its operation, as it failed to confer any real
benefit upon authors.” The statement is inaccurate 1
The Act was so far successful that it has neve-
been repealed and remains a basis o| existing
Canadian law, while the principle which it
established was recommended by the British
Commission whose ideas have been adopted*
to some, extent in the Commonwealth Bill
Possibly the Act “ failed to confer anv
real benefit” upon British authors, but British
authors are not the only pebb 1 -on the beach. The
Act did benefit Canadian readers; and Canada
has always affirmedt what Britain has always
admitted, that the interest of Canadian readers is
entitled to consideration no less than that of
British authors. Copyright legislation m us t;
compromise between authors and readers ; and it
is natural to expect that the Commonwealth
Parliament will put Australian interests first.
“ We have not developed a literature entirely
distinct from the great parent stock,” says an
American critic, “but we believe that our writing
has been inspired by definite American ideals.”
And in this country’s literature we want—definite
Australian ideals!
“Anglo-Indian,” in the N.A. Review, declares
that Kitchener’s triumph over Curzon is due to
the new conception of India as a military bulwark
of Britain.
“India, under the ®gis of Lord Kitchener, is destined
to fight the battles from which the British conscript
shrinks. It is an Imperial idea, but it is the Imperialism
of a decadent democracy. Lord Curzon, who knows well
the secrets of Indian finance, and knows well and sympa-
thises with the crying needs of Indian administration,
sees how the revenues will be spent; he sees the money
required for education, irrigation, railways, and police
clutched by a confident Commander-in-chief. He does
well to resign.”
The French poet Heredia; recently here referred-
to, died on 2nd October. Obituary notices point
out that his native Cubans are proud not merely
of the French Heredia, but of their own ; for the
deceased writer left a- cousin with exactly the
same name, who is considered the greatest poet of
Spanish America. There is a nephew, too:
Emilio do Heredia, a brilliant painter; and one
of Heredia’s daughters, Mrae. Henri de Regnier,
is a distinguished writer of prose. Another
daughter is married to Pierre Louys, and the
third to Maurice Maindron.
It is encouraging to find W. Cunningham, of
the Economic Science Section of the British
Association, declaring in his presidential address,
1905, that “in the world of political and economic
activities we never find the same conditions
repeating themselves. The-fundamental inquiry
must always be, How far were the conditions of
some growing community in the past similar to
those of some growing community to-day? How
far are they on all fours, so that we can argue
from one to another directly?”—This utterance is
commended to the gentlemen who argue about
Freetrade for Australia from the foreign experi-
ence of 50 or 100 years ago.
‘ ‘ The day one really requires a holiday most is
the day after.” —“Winifred” in Melb. Argus.
J. L. Ford, in The Reader, gives a good ex-
planation of the inferiority of the cheaper Eng.
magazines :
To go to the very root of the matter, we find that the
great bulk of current magazine literature is prepared by a
class of writers who are quite different from those who
furnish similar material in America. Every year there are
graduated from the English universities a vast number of
young men who are finely educated, capable of writing
the purest English, and possessed of an income almost
large enough to maintain them in the style befitting their
birth. I am speaking now of the younger sons of good
families, the scions of the minor branches of aristocratic
-oots, and of others who go to make up the great army of
well-bred, well-dressed, well-connected, well-fed, and
well-educated young men, who know that if they can add
to the allowance or inheritance that is theirs already the
sum of one or two hundred pounds a year, they can live
comfortably in London and enjoy the best that the town
has to offer to its well-placed idlers.'
The only problem, then, that confronts them is how to
earn this one or two hundred pounds with the least labour
and social degradation : and, naturally enough, nine-
tenths of them decide upon the profession of letters. As
they are veritable ■Chinamen in their willingness to
underbid legitimate craftsmen, as well as in their habits
of not spending any of their own money, they dispose of
their services for much less than the regular market
price,- and there are even some of them who offer one or
two social introductions as asort of bonus to those editors
or publishers who are willing to purchase their wares.
Realising as they do the necessity for reaching a class
that they regard as beneath their own, they attempt to
“ write down " to their readers, and they are apt to do
this with an insolence and a condescension that are a
positive insult to those whom they address.
The result of all .this is that the pages of the sixpenny
English magazines are filled with matter that, although
admirably written, is not only wholly unsuited to the
tastes, of those who are expected to read it, but also in
many instances reeking with impertinent,. boorish affa-
bility.
Robert Bridges indulges us with a new volume,
his sixth of “poetical works.” His concurrent Yeats
indulges himself rather with six revisions-of a volume.
But Bridges used to be a-mighty oarsman,; and ' probably
has a big heart to drive Him to -effort. Gather ye-muscle
while ye may !
E. G. Bourne, Yale Professor of History, in a
recent volume ascribes the actual invention of the name
America to Martin Waldseemiiller, Professor of Geo-
graphy at the college of St.. Die : who suggestediin ISO/
that “since‘Americas discovered the new part of - the
globe, Anierigen, quasi Amend terrain,, sive Americaw
nuncupare licet." Said Americawas not always the land
of freedom. In 1671 Governor Berkeley, of Virginia,
wrote : “ I thank God there are no free schools or print-
ing, and I hope we shall not have these hundred years,
for learning has brought disobedience and heresy and
sects into the world, and printing has divulged them and
libels against the best government. God keep -us-from
both ! ”

A HARROWING TALE OF SUFFERING AND A
THANKFUL TRIBUTE FROM ULTIMO.
The Case of Mr. V. BORWICK,
(•By a Sydney Keporter.)
Mr. Victor Borwick is a storekeeper
who has his business premises and. re-
sidence at 286 and 288 Crown-road,
Ultimo, and although he could have
been found at this address any time
during- the past eleven years,it was not
till lately that a reporter had occasion
to pay his establishment a visit, ‘the
reason for calling,” as the scribe ex-
plained, “being to get some informa-
tion respecting a widely-known recov-
ery that happened to come within
hearing.”
“Ah ! That is six years ago now
since I got all right again, Mr. Bor-
wick remarked, “and I think it was
about up to me to have a change of
luck, considering that I had been suf-
fering for fully three years. The pains
that ran along my gums and reached
up to the top of mv head were some-
thing terrible, and half my time I did
not know what I was doing. My ad-
versities set in when I was ‘running
the plank,' as they call it, on a boat
discharging blue metal, and I can as-
sure you that 1 was so broken down
in health that I had to put another man
on to do my work about every other
week. Talk about neuralgia ! I’m sure
nobody could have it worse than I
had, and as I found such a grand cure
for it the least I can do is to let hu-
manity know all about it, for I dare-
say my experience will prove very
helpful. Well, out of all the medicines
I tried to cure neuralgia the only one
that was of any lasting benefit was
Clements Tonic, and when I say that
after keeping going with that remedy
for a few weeks the fearful pains all left
me and have never returned all these
years, it is the honest truth I am tell-
ing you.”
“Is that the only ailment you have
had ?”
“Indeed, no. All the time the neural-
fic tortures were at me I had other
roubles to deal with, for my diges-
tion was just as bad as it could be.
Both from want of appetite, amd also
owing to the pains about my jaws,
eating was almost out of the ques-
tion ; but what was still more unfortu-
nate was the fact that the little I did
force myselt to take to keep body and
soul together had the unhappy habit
of disagreeing with me. And you must
remember that I was getting no sleep.
Neither night nor day—for weeks at a
stretch sometimes —could I get any
rest, and, as you may well imagine,
the result was disastrous. I got so
terribly weak that ultimately I had to
give up my work altogether, and m ,< '
body was so denuded of flesh that my
clothes hung about me like a sack.
Indeed, i was just going to have some
of them altered about the time that I
started on Clements Tonic, and bv
some means or other the. idea was
shelved for a while, for which I was
afterwards vc v glad, as in few
months I was as stout as ever. Yes i;
it was quite a matter for regular com-
ment how well I was looking, and,
thank God ! I felt well, too. For three
years previous to using Clements To-
nic I was nothing but a total wreck,
for every part of my body was ad-
versely affected in some way or other.
It was my first experience of indiges-
tion, and T sincerely trust it will be
my last, for I would rather be dead
and out of the world altogether than
go through such misery again. T got to
feel as shaky and uncomfortable as if
everything in my inside was going to
pieces, and was so disturbed and un-
settled mentally that the least thino-
made me irritable. My eyes always
felt heavy and swollen to such an ex-
tent that 1 could scarcely see to read,
and I felt so discontented and restless
that T could not stay indoors. Conse-
quently 1 often wandered about out-
side hardly knowing what to do with
myself, feeling as though death would
be a thousand times more welcome
than the wretched life I was leading,
for I was in agony everywhere. The
pains- in my chest were really cruel,
and I thought I had strained myself
or broken something between my
shoulders owing to the pain that was
there. It was like getting hold of a
piece ot meat, and tearing it in two —
that was the kind of feeling 1 had be-
tween the shoulders, and my loins
ached badly into the bargain."
“And have you never had a return
of any of those symptoms during the
past six years ?’’
“JNo, upon my honour 5 and I have
not had that nasty, bulged-out feeling
in the stomach either after eating my
food. JNo ; Clements Tonic removed
everything, even to the bitter taste of
a morning and the fur that used to
cover my tongue, besides which it gave
me permanent relief from the many at-
tacks of giddiness that I had pre-
viously been subjected to. How much
my nerves accounted for the misery I
had endured is hard to say definitely,
but they undoubtedly had their fair
share of responsibility, for I must tell
you that 1 was so highly nervous that
I could not stand the least excitement
without getting all of a tremble, and I
had also contracted an uncontrollable
practice of measuring the future with
nothing but trouble and worry in
store. All 1 could see before me was a
continuance of my suffering only to be
added to by misfortunes of other de-
scriptions, and now that you under-
stand the gloomy frame of mind I waf
in you will readily conceive. why 1
speak so highly of Clements Vonic for
lifting me from that sorry _ state to
the happy and healthy condition I ami
now blessed with. It was. through
reading somebody’s testimonial that i
decided to give Clements Tonic a trial,
and my earnest hope is that other
sufferers, for their sako, will be so in-
fluenced when they read mine, and
then l shall consider I have performed
a valuable service to the community.
1 can give Clements Tonic a special
word in respect to its invigorating in-
fluence over my nervous system. It
cleared every trace of nervousness
right away, and as an appetiser it
was simply marvellous. Cat ! why,
when once I got going I hardly knew
when to stop, so no wonder I soon
filled my clothes out again. But it
was all good, sound fle-h I put on,
plenty of strength and vigour behind
it. and after using several bottles of
Clements Tonic, there was hardly an
ache anywhere to mar the wonderful
cure 1 had made. What little discom-
fort there was left after using my first
lot of Clements Tonic was soon dis-
persed by my continuance of the same
remedy, and if anybody doubts the
completeness of my recovery all they
have to do is to drop in and see me,
and I will give them my experience
verbally from end to end.’
“And in the meantime may I circu-
late vour story?’’ ...
“■You are at liberty to publish it in
any conceivable style you may wish*

Various Verses.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
An Hour’s Respite.
Here, in the deep, cool shadows of the widely-
spreading tree,
I will rest me for an hour with my head upon
your knee.
Quite near to us our people drudge—to slavery
resigned;
But for one fleeting hour at least, my dear, I will
not mind.
Forget them all, my girlie. Ah ! I bided there,
and fought
The battles they are fighting, in the wider realms
of thought.
And I suffered ; but I’ve left them at an angel’s
sweet behest —
For I’m tired —oh, so tired ! —and I crave a little
rest.
Yet I wonder (ah ! forget them? —it is little use
to try !)
And I wonder if they’re sadder folk or wearier
than I.
Do they dream the dreams of childhood ? And,
I’m yearning still to know,
Have they ever one to cheer them when they
extra weary grow ?
Forget them ! Ah, my girlie, in the city’s dull
confines,
My brother scribes are writing—aye, they’re
grinding out the lines ;
Shaping leaders, pars, and poems, stories grave,
and humor gay ;
And I know that they are weary, and I pity them
to-day.
But forget them... I’ll forget them, with my
head upon your knee,
As we listen to the breezes’ strange, old, soothing
melody;
Where the maid is half an angel, and the man is
half a beast,
We’ll forget our deepest sorrows for one trifling
hour at least.
For an hour and I’ve forgotten—all the city’s
strife is gone—
Receded, faded, vanished into deep oblivion.
For one hour ! And could we ever cease to falter
and to fret,
And be blind to all our troubles, or forgive them—
and forget!
And you whisper words of comfort with my head
upon your knee :
This is rest, and peace, and rapture—’tis angelic
sympathy...
Forgetting... still forgetting... and a word, a kiss,
a smile—
Our lives are long, and, oh ! an hour is such a little
while!
N.S.W.
GILROONEY.
Melbourne—A Silhouette.
Lamps a-glitter in the street,
Rain and laughter, hasty feet,
And the pale moon soaring high
In the sparkling winter sky.
Violin and harp and lute,
Gaslight gilding piles of fruit,
Tram-cars shooting through the night,
Clanging as they rush from sight.
Singers shrill, and blind man’s drone,
Shutters closing one by one,
Empty streets and cabs in flight,
Night and silence —silence, night.
vio.
DOROTHY FRANCES McCRAE.
Music.
What is this message that thou hast for us ?
What tale to tell that never yet was told
Of yearning needs, of pain diaphanous,
Of misty memories long aeons old ?
Thy voice is as the voice of far-off spheres
Bathed in translucent ether, shining blue,
Heard in dim dreams, but closed to mortal ears,
Of bliss ineffable vast ages through.
Thy voice is infinite as is the sea
Crooning its love-song to the sun-lit sand ;
Luring the dancing boat to wander free,
Full of caresses for its love—the land.
Thou bring’st before us with a Dryad’s touch
All that we ever knew of hopes and fears,
Of truth and love and tenderness, of such
As moves the heart to laughter and to tears.
The blaze of sunlight over growing cane,
The singing in the quarters after work,
The curlew’s cry of never-ending pain,
The clink of horse-hoofs homing through the
murk.
The smell of burning pine on autumn eves,
The rocking of the cradle in the hut,
The morning sunshine through the tender leaves,
The fragrance of the cedar logs fresh cut.
The passion and the pain of primal love
When man sees woman first through opened
eyes—
And these thou tell’st of from thy shrine above,
Winging thy message through the starry skies.
Thou bring’st the primal wildernesses back,
The savage virtuous vice of earliest man,
The darkling fear, the freedom, and the wrack
Of jungle battles when the world began.
What strange Erse legends of the pathless times
With wondrous Celtic glamor thou dost tell,
Of chivalrous true knights in minstrel rhymes—
Of holy quests, and murders dark and fell.
Thou art the echo of what e’er could be—
What e’er has been of darkness and of light,
The beating heart of all humanity—
Thou far-off murmur of the Infinite.
N.S.W.
C.G.S.

Jl ow to A ddrens Betters.
Correspondents to Tiie Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. 'To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not. be there; the official
always is.

NEWSPAPER CONSPIRACY.
The newspaper press is supposed to be a great
agency for good, and while it is so in many
respects, it is also a great conspiracy for evil.
This is the day of the press syndicate, and the
combine, and the big proprietor, who supply the
political opinion of half-a-dozen or a dozen or a
score of papers, and who make a personal preju-
dice, or a personal job, or the grinding of a per-
sonal axe seem like a spontaneous outbreak of
public indignation rising all over the land to pro-
test against a national injury. And the power of
the press syndicate is so vast that it can make
black appear as white, it can make war and make
peace, and though its lie and steal can’t endure for
ever, still they can be a very robust curse while
they last. To take the first of a few recent
instances —it was a sordid press combine which
made the South African war, and with the
blood of thousands of human beings on its head
it still goes on and prospers exceedingly. I he
Transvaal was, of all the countries in the world,
the one which offered the most cordial welcome
and the greatest freedom to the British alien.
The Transvaal allowed him to own practically all
its mineral wealth, while Japan kicks the British
would-be mine-owner out and tells him he is un-
clean and unfit. The Transvaal allowed the alien
to own land, while Japan says he is unfit to own
land. The Transvaal didn’t object when the
alien mopped up nearly all the riches of the coun-
try, and it didn’t hang even one of him when he
rose in rebellion ; but it refused to let him own
its Parliament or to import Chinese slaves, and it
always stood by the white British miner against
his blastiferous German Jew boss. So a huge
German Jew syndicate, which controlled almost
the whole press of South Africa, set to work
to persuade England and the world generally
that the foolishly liberal Transvaal Govern-
ment, which had allowed the alien to possess
almost everything in its country, was some-
thing unique in the way of oppression, and
the Transvaal State went down in blood. These
brutish floggers and torturers of Chinese serfs —
the floggings at one mine have averaged 40 per
day —actually dragged the alleged cause of
humanity into the case, and set up a charge of
“ cruelty to the natives” as one reason why the
Boer, in a political sense, must pass away. It was
a wonderful instance of what newspaper con-
spiracy can effect, but it was far from being a
solitary instance.
* * * *
The same newspaper conspiracy struck Aus-
tralia next. The Australians in South Africa
were a troublesome race with ideas about a white
man’s privileges, with leanings towards trades-
unionism, and with a large abhorrence of China-
men, and of Chinese (or any other kind) of slavery.
They probably said more things about the Chinese
policy of the great German Jew swindlers than
most of the outsiders in South Africa. The Aus-
tralian press and some Australian Legislatures
put in unpleasant protests also against the Chinese
slave system. Then the White Australia policy
gives the German Jew gang exactly the same
reason for hating this country as it had
for hating the Kruger Government in the
Transvaal. The present campaign of lies and
slander against Australia began in the South
African papers ; the same papers which ran exactly
the same campaign against Paul Kruger. It was
carried on in England by the papers which the
South African gang could control or influence. It
spread to the rest of the press which became con-
vinced that where there was so much smoke there
must be a great deal of fire. In Australia a
servile gang of Tories and Tory papers took
up the Stinking Fish policy in the hope
that the storm of obloquy that beat on this
country from all sides would scare the democratic
party, and bring the old retrograde gang
of Tories, Freetraders, land-monopolists, bank-
swindlers, and advocates of nigger and Chinese
labor back into office. Another class of Tories,
who were not malevolent, but only weak-kneed
and foolish, began to be convinced, through hear-
ing the lie so often, that their country had really
done something dreadful by reason of its demo-
cratic policy, and joined the howl of execration,
after the manner of Editor Sowden, of S.A.
Sowden, apparently, had no idea what he had said
about his country till he saw his statements in all
their naked horror in an American paper, and
then he started in a frantic fashion to explain them
away. In Johannesburg, when one Englishman
killed another under horribly brutal circumstances,
and then tried to kill a Welsh policeman who
sought to arrest him, and was shot by the
policeman when the latter found that he had
no other chance of saving his own life, the
newspaper conspiracy called it “the brutal and
unprovoked murder by a Boer of a British
subject named Edgar,” and the storm of obloquy
grew so terrible that the Boer Government was
driven to put the policeman on his trial for mur-
der. In Australia, when the Petriana’s colored
seamen were carefully rescued and sent back
safely to their homes, the newspaper conspiracy
called it something very like murder, and de-
scribed it as a national disgrace. The Melbourne
Argus and the Sydney Telegraph and Herald,
and half a dozen other papers, more or less played
the part in Australia that the floggers and torturers
of Chinese used to do in South Africa. Now that
the newspaper conspiracy has turned against
Australia, this country can partly understand what
kind of an unspeakable fool and an unspeak-
able criminal it was when it helped to butcher
the Boers in a war brought about by exactly
the same process of lying. If Australia had been
a weak foreign State, instead of part of the em-
pire, British troops would probably have been burn-
ing Melbourne and Sydney before now in order
to avenge the allegedly-murdered Petriana niggers,
and to secure 400,000 Chinese coolies to work the
mines of Bendigo, Ballarat, Kalgoorlie, Mount
Morgan, Broken Hill and Charters Towers in the
interests of foreign syndicates.
* * * *
In New South Wales to-day, the Australian
Government is struggling against such a news-
paper conspiracy as the German Jew press ran
against the Kruger Government in the Trans-
vaal, and such as the German Jew press in South
Africa and a section of the English press is now
running against Australia. It is practically a
conspiracy within a conspiracy; the parochial
movement in N.S.W. is part and parcel of the
Stinking Fish movement in and out of Australia.
At its head is the degraded Sydney Telegraph, the
old Freetrade party with its frenzied desire to
destroy Australian industries in the interests of the
importers, and the Carruthers Government—a
little mob of sour and uncivil nonentities which
can only keep itself before the public by sticking
out its tongue at its intellectual superiors, and
by posing as the strong crowd which is saving
the State from some stupendous imaginary
danger. The policy of this conspiracy is exactly
that of the German Jew press against the Kruger
Government, and of a section of the British press
against Australia —everything the Australian
Government does is wrong, and if the Australian
Government reverses its policy, and does the other
thing, that is wrong also. Its theory, put in brief,
is that the people of N.S.W. are quite unfit to
govern themselves if they do it through a Barton
or Watson or Deakin Ministry, and that they
can only rise to be politically something above the
beasts of the field when they govern themselves
wholly through Joe Carruthers, and do it
wholly at Sydney, where the Telegraph can
dictate to the Ministry. And its weapon
is simply that persistent lying which, if
pursued long enough, always convinces a section
of the unthinking. When the N.S.W. Parlia-
ment offered the Dalgety site (among others) as a
possible capital, and withdrew it with foolish
insult as soon as it was accepted, the anti-
Australian newspaper conspiracy really persuaded
a large number of people that in some way a
grievous wrong had been done to N.S.W. By
steady falsehood the Telegraph and S.M.
Herald and other papers have almost persuaded
the public that there is somewhere in the consti-
tution a pledge that the capital is to be not much
over 100 miles from Sydney, that it is to consist
of not much over 100 square miles, that the
N.S.W. State Parliament is to be consulted about
the site—which is much on a par with the story or
the German Jew press in Johannesburg about the
murder of a British subject, and about its desire
for the destruction of the Boer Government
solely that the white Briton might be
free and prosperous and have a vote.
The cries that N.S.W. is being swindled in the
distribution of the surplus, and that it is contrary
to the Constitution to get stamps printed at Ade-
laide, are further features of the same campaign
of fraud, and rank with the alleged drowning of
the Petriana niggers, and the London Statist s
new allegation that Australia is breaking laitli
with its creditors. Nowadays, it is hardly neces-
sary for anyone outside Australia to bear a hand
in the Stinking Fish conspiracy ._ The Sydney
Telegraph, and a few papers which support it,
and the Carruthers Government, and the old
brigade in N.S. W. Parliament, with their constant
assurances that all the States except N.S.W. are
thieves and highway robbers, that all the people
of N.S.W. except those that denounce Federation
are in league with these thieves and robbers, and
that the people of N.S.W. are quite unfit to
govern themselves except through the medium of
the State Parliament bossed by J oseph Car-
ruthers, keep the campaign of slander going
about as well as Australia’s worst enemy could
desire.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Value of a Politician’s Pledge.
Divisions on the initial stages of the National
Library Bill before N.S.W. Assembly show that,
compared with the solemn pledge of some members
of Parliament, a dicer’s oath is quite a respectable
warranty, and the word of honor of a spieler a
trustworthy security. At the last N.S.W. general
elections The Bulletin conducted a campaign for
honest finance, and asked candidates to pledge
themselves to the following platform :
Absolute cessation of borrowing, except for renewal
purposes — i.e., no increase of public debt.
Establishment of a sinking fund for the repayment of
existing debts.
The setting aside of an adequate sum out of revenue
for the maintenance of public works and the construction
of necessary new works. (For new works it is suggested
that the net land revenue of about £1,500,000 per annum
would be a fair provision.)
Some scores did pledge themselves, and of those
who gave this solemn assurance of honesty the
following were returned to the Assembly:—
John Gillies J. R. Dacey
John Hurley Eden George
O. W. Oakes J. B. Nicholson
P. H. Sullivan Hugh Macdonald
J. J. Cohen A. Kelly
G. S. Briner E. Richards
S. J. Kearney F. J. Thomas
A. Millard Tiios. Jessep
E. C. V. Broughton Dr. Arthur
David Fell
In addition, all Labor candidates were required by
the platform of their party (which was reluctant
to go the Whole Honest Hog as proposed by The
Bulletin, but was prepared to go a very long way
in that direction) to pledge themselves against any
further borrowing except for reproductive works
which would return full interest on t'he cost of
construction and also a 1 per cent, contribution to
sinking fund. The proposal of the Carruthers
Government to build a library out of loan money
was as definite an issue as could be raised to test
the fidelity of members to these pledges. A Public
Library building is not a permanent and repro-
ductive work ; it will return no interest and no
contribution to a sinking fund. Arthur Griffith
first challenged the House on this point, and his
direct proposal to build the library out of revenue
was negatived on the following division :
Ayes 13.

Thus showing that Arthur, Briner, Cohen,
George, Gillies, and Nicholson are pre-
pared to definitely pledge themselves to a
certain principle, to appeal for support on
that principle, and to accept material help
from the advocates of that principle, and then
rat shamelessly when the question comes to a
test; and further, that some dozen other members,
having pledged themselves to a principle, did not
take the trouble to attend to vote on its behalf.
But these had another chance to retrieve them-
selves, for, next day, Mr. Perry (the Richmond)
moved again that the library be built out of
revenue, and the division on this occasion was :

Wilks Hitting Rome.
According to a public announcement made re-
cently in Melbourne by Wilks, Lee, Simian
Phraser and other waste products, their party—
the Foreign-Orange party—proposes to fight the
next Federal election on the religious ticket, with
the Walls of Derry and the Flames of Smithfield
as its principal issues. The same people tried to
fight the last election on the same aged and remote
joke; and when the election after next arrives
they will doubtless be found dragging their dis-
used European shriek over the face of Australia
once more. As regards next election, the plea is
that the Federal Parliament itself dug up the con-
troversy by passing a motion in favor of Home
Rule for Ireland—the same as there is in Canada
and Australia —but as that was a purely political
question, and as Wilks and Phraser bellowed just
as hard about the Walls of Derry at last election
when Parliament had passed no Home Rule motion,
the lie looks a very poor one. There are a dreadful
number of things waiting to be done in Australia,
and it is a pathetically childish business that,
every time an election comes round to decide who
will hustle along the live urgent business of the
country for the next three years, a considerable
section of the people should drop everything and
elect members chiefly on account of their opinions
about a scramble of men with muskets that hap-
pened at a dirty little city on the other side of the
world before the grandfather of anybody now
alive was born. If the man who gets in on the
Walls of Derry or the Flames of Smithfield issue
was of real value for any other purpose, it would
matter very little how he got in ; but, as a rule,
he is a dull ass who arrives by screaming about
the Flames of Smithfield, because the Flames of
Smithfield are just about his size, and he is good
for nothing else. Or if, having got in by
crying about the dangers of Rome, these people
had any programme for lessening or abolishing
the dangers of Rome, then the position would be
at least comprehensible. But they neither pro-
pose to abolish Roman Catholic churches and
schools, nor to disfranchise Roman Catholic
voters, nor to shut out Roman Catholics from
Parliament and the Civil Service, nor to do any-
thing at all that will depress Rome. They do,
certainly, demand that customs duties should be
abolished so that the mostly-Protestant Austra-
lian workman may lose his job, and the goods he
used to make be replaced to a great extent by the
products of the Roman Catholic of Belgium,
France, Austria, Italy, and Southern Germany,
but that isn’t supposed to be a religious issue.
Apart from the customs question and the desire
to import Roman Catholic products, it seems that
the only reason why we should hate Rome is to
get Wilks a job, and when Wilks has got the
job he does nothing to suppress Rome. If
the country must have an Orange vote it
is time, and more than time, that Wilks
was sent to earn an honest living in the retail
wood and coal business, and his place taken by a
live Orangeman, who will really hit Rome in the
eye, and rush the Scarlet Woman off the premises
as an undesirable emigrant. What has Wilks
done to Rome in his 11 years of political life to
justify his re-election ? Is there a single crack in
Rome that Wilks has made? The census of
1871, and again those of ’Bl and ’9l, showed that
Rome was a diminishing quantity in N.S.W. It
represented first 293 people out of every 1000
inhabitants; then 276 ; then (in 1891) only 255.
After that, in 1894, N.S.W. elected Wilks to give
Rome a shove that would finally send her over the
precipice, and the census of 1901 showed that
Rome represented 260 out of e\ ery 1000 Cornstalk
citizens. It began to increase and multiply as
soon as Wilks arrived. The inference, therefore,
is that either Wilks is wholly inefficient, and
that Rome is too big a contract for him, or else
that he is a traitor who is secretly propping up
Rome and repairing its walls in the night.
Either way, Orangeism plainly wants somebody
who can do things as well as talk. If Rome
doesn’t show signs of decadence before next year
the whole of the present Orange push should lose
their seats in the Australian Parliament and make
room for a crowd that can leave a visible dent on
Rome when they hit it.
The Eldest Sons of Britain.
Two cablegrams from England, both bearing on
the unemployed question, appeared almost side
by side the other day :
Replying to a deputation representing the London
unemployed, the Prime Minister gave his reasons for
refusing State aid and for not summoning a winter session
of Parliament. One of Mr. Balfour’s arguments was that
State aid was calculated to destroy the enterprise of
capitalists and the energy and fibre of the workmen.
Speaking at Basingstoke, Mr. H. H. Asquith, M.P.,
attributed the existing misery and pauperism in England
to the Government’s profligate extravagance.
There is something almost pathetic about the
anxiety of people like Balfour not to destroy the
“energy and fibre” of the poor by saving them
from starvation, and about the way he goes forth
languidly to play “ goff,” in full reliance that the
unemployed will pull through by their own bull-
dog pluck. And, on the other hand, there is
Asquith, who only sees in the popular hunger
and misery a reason why his crowd should get
Balfour’s job, and only recognises in thedead body
of the seamstress, who died because she couldn’t
live on lOd. a day, something to step on as he
climbs into office. Britain is a tolerably rich
country. Its wealth is estimated at £3OO per
inhabitant, or £I2OO for the average family. Yet
there are more deaths from hunger in London m
an average year than in France, Denmark and
Prussia combined. Except in Russia, Europe has
no poverty to show like that of England. Its
whole political system aims an keeping a small
body of men immensely rich, whereby an im-
mensely large body must be horribly and incre-
dibly poor. Its law of property and the traditions
of its propertied families hand down the estate to
the Eldest Son, so that the big pile may not be
divided, and subdivided, and thus cause a more
general diffusion of wealth. The British law
believes, as far as possible, in keeping the heap of
wealth together, in disinheriting as many of the
relations as possible, in retaining the land m the
fewest possible hands, and in promoting monopoly.
Wherever there is an estate and ten descendants,
British law aims at making one so rich that he
will probably become a disease a person who is
constantly seeking for new pleasures to buy with
liis money, and finding very little pleasure after
all—and at making the others so poor that rAm*
descendants, in a few generations, will probab y


have “gone under.” If Britain had a law for the
subdivision of estates and fortunes among the
children of the deceased owner, a very tew
generations would probably see such a dirrusion
of wealth set in that the pall of misery would be
perceptibly lifted. But it believes with a pathetic
and horrible belief in having a few enormously
bloated Eldest Sons, some of whom hardly know
how to get rid of their mountains of gold, and at
the other end of the scale millions of starving
wretches for whom there is no hope, no
country, no patriotism, no God and no pros-
pect here or hereafter. Now and then a
deputation of this accumulated woe manages to
get the ear of the ruling coterie of Eldest Sons,
and asks blindly why these horrors should be.
And the Eldest Sons, if they are of the crowd in
office, say that it is unaccountable, that it is a dis-
pensation of Providence, and that the bulldog
pluck of the British race must pull them through
—which is rough on the people who, through
living eight in a room and trying to exist on 2d. a
day, have no pluck left, and who would gladly eat a
bulldog if they could steal one. And the Eldest
Sons, if they happen to be out of office, generally
reckon that the trouble arises through the other
crowd of Eldest Sons "being in office. Neither
crowd can be expected to suggest that the disease
has its principal root in the policy of Eldest Sons.
Finally, having cleared the matter up, the Eldest
Sons generally go back to their ancestral castles
and play “goff,” or go shooting over the wilder-
nesses which were once pasture and corn land
before the Eldest Sons turned the people out to
starve, and began growing wild animals where the
country used to grow British citizens. Some day,
perhaps, when the outer world grows more
crowded, and emigration no longer supplies a
partial outlet for the surplus misery, Britain will
acquire sense enough to have a revolution, to
break up its big estates among hundreds of thou-
sands of small farmers, each farming his own land,
and to hang its Eldest Sons.

Woollens and Wages.
The decision of the Australian Woollen Co., of
Footscray (Victoria), to close up—a decision to
which it has been driven by constantly-increasing
losses—should be something for the Freetrade
section of the Labor party to think over very
hard. The Australian Woollen Co. started under
the old Victorian tariff of 25 per cent, on tweeds
and from 15 to 35 per cent, on other woollens.
Under the Federal tariff which cut down the duty
on tweeds to 15 per cent, and made heavy reduc-
tions in all other lines (on flannels, which formed
a large part of the Victorian industry, the duty
was cut down one half) it did very badly, like all
the other Victorian mills. To this fact the Free-
trader replies with a short sharp yelp to the effect
that the N.S.W. woollen mills are getting along
moderately well on the whole under the same
tariff (though they also showed a slight falling off
last year), and it is the Victorians’ own fault if
they can’t do just as well (even if that well
isn’t nearly so good as it might be). But
it isn’t their fault —at least not wholly so ; it is to
a great extent a question of wages. Woollen mills
employ about as many women as men, the figures,
at the time of the latest* issue of Coghlan’s
“Statistical Account of Australia and Maoriland”
(1904) being, for the Commonwealth, 961 males and
896 females. (Maoriland, which charges duties of
20 per cent, and upwards, against the Common-
wealth’s 15 per cent., has a woollen industry
employing 727 males and 965 females.) And the
average wages according to Cogiilan were :
Average of
Low Tariff States. Wages per Week, one man and
Men. Women, one woman.
N.S.W. .. £l3 7 £0 12 11 £0 18 3
Victoria .. 136 0 17 8 107
High Tariff State.
Maoriland .. 1 15 9 0 16 11 1 6 4
Therein lies at least part of the explanation.
N.S.W. keeps its woollen industry going under
the low tariff by paying the lowest rate of wages,
though the reduction of its mills from five to three
suggests that, in N.S.W. also, the wages have
been pushed up quite as high as the ill-pro-
tected industry can stand. Victoria pays higher
wages it got into that habit when it
had its own tariff and before a Federal
Labor party, at the instance of the Foreign-
trade section, turned dog on its own class
—and it is doing worse than N.S.W. The extra
wage turns the scale and makes it impossible to
compete, at the decent hours and conditions which
prevail in Australia, with the foreign importer.
Maoriland, with a higher tariff, is not only able to
support a woollen industry almost as large as that
of all Australia and Tasmania put together, but
to pay better wages than the best Australian
average. Victoria is apparently almost “up
against” this alternative: in the woollen indus-
try, if the present tariff lasts, the employees must
either take less wages, or else lose their employ-
ment and take no wages at all. The ‘ ‘ bloated
manufacturer ” is already getting, in a good many
cases, nothing per cent., so he can’t well be cut
any further. It is for the Labor party to
consider the position. It sank the fiscal issue
at the instance of a small section of
Laborites, mostly from N.S.W. By so doing it is
crippling industries, reducing wages, destroying
employment, and doing the dirty work of the rich
Tory importer, with his underpaid and mostly
non-union employees. If the Labor party con-
tinues to do that sort of thing it can hardly die too
soon, for a Labor party which regards the finding
of regular and profitable work for the people as no
part of its business has taken the wrong name.
It has been said that the question of high Pro-
tection for Australian industries can’t be raised
without splitting the party, but if that is so the
party can’t split too quickly. Then the really Aus-
tralian section of it may do some good work,
while the section which is foreign and anti-Aus-
tralian at heart can gravitate quite naturally to
its old friend Joe Cook. It is better that half of
the party should remain alive than that the whole
of it should remain dead, and on the most urgent
question in Australian politics the Labor party is
now not only dead—it is buried.

Another Parson on Black Australia.
Again the parson—a Westralian parson this
time—as reported in the S.M. Herald :
Perth (W.A.), Wednesday.
Speaking ai. the general assembly of the Presbyterian
Church of Western Australia, last night, the Moderator,
the Rev. G. Brewstf.r, made an attack on the White Aus-
tralia policy. “ What about the heathenish, selfish cry
for a White Australia ? ” he asked. He did not care if he
had all Australia or all the world against him. He would
stand by God and His Word, and say that no man could
remain a Christian and look down on his black brethren.
The day that a man thought less of his brother, be he
black, white, yellow or brown, than himself, that day he
would become disloyal to the Cross of Christ. Could
they for a moment think that God, who had made of one
flesh all nations of the world to dwell together, would
allow a handful of feeble godless creatures to keep this
glorious continent locked up for themselves, when they
could neither use it nor populate it ?
Rev. Brewster is at least a downright parson, and
probably thinks he is sincere. It is utterly false
to say that Australians can’t populate Australia ;
things are not so good as they should be, but still
the people of Australia are continuously increas-
ing, and this country has grown almost as much
population in its first 130 years as Canada did in
its first 300 years; but the poor ignorant
preacher can’t be expected to know that.
It is also shamefully false that Australians
don’t use their country; things are far from
being what they should be, and the rich
landlord blocks settlement, and the rich
importer blocks manufactures, but when a new
country like this exports nearly twice as much as
China, and supplies the United Kingdom with
nearly twice as much goods as India, it is doing
something. Still a mere excited sin-shifter who
doesn’t study his subject, but simply yells, can’t be
expected to understand anythinglikethat. And the
Rev. Brewster obviously thinks he is sincere. No
doubt, seeing that he holds that Europeans, China-
men, Indians and the like are all perfectly equal,
he invites Chinamen and Brahmins and negroes to
dine at his table, to show that his Christianity is
real and not mere advertisement and hypocrisy ;
and he asks the Jap and the Hindu into his draw-
ing room; and if Sambo or Ah Sin, as a perfect
equal and a complete brother, asked for his
daughter or his sister in marriage he would throw
no obstacles in the way. If he did he would
obviously be “disloyal to the cross of Christ.”
Probably he even lives in the Chinese quarter of his
town to show that his faith in the equality of
races is genuine, and that the Asiatic is our equal
even in cleanliness. If he doesn’t live in the
Chinese quarter it is an oversight. And seeing
that he holds the Hindu and the other colored
races of the empire as being quite equal to the
whites, he has probably said dreadful things in
public about the policy by which the whole
control of the vast British empire is reserved to a
comparative handful of whites, about 55,000,000 in
all, while some 330,000,000 colored people have no
more self-government, and no more share in the
control of the empire of which they form the
greatest part, than the cows in English paddocks.
And in denouncing this sin he has assuredly
been driven to call Edward YII. a godless
ruffian, and the whole British Parliament a
disgrace to Christianity. Probably he has
even denounced missions to the so-called
“heathen,” as an arrogant proceeding, by
which the white man pretends to be better
and wiser than a colored brother who is really
quite as good as he is, and quite as likely to be
right in his beliefs, and whose Mumb'o Jumbo may
be every bit as true as Parson Brewster’s Pres-
byterianism. The Bulletin hopes that Parson
Brewster has given these proofs of sincerity.
For if he hasn’t there may arise a tendency to
regard him as one of the virulent crawling
things that go on all fours after the rich,
and who only maintain the “rights” of the
colored man when it is a question of letting
him into Australia to supply the Tory employer
with cheap labor and to starve the white workman
out of a job. People may even look up the dusty,
neglected Scripture in search of a forgotten
passage in Luke, which denounces woe on those
who “lade men with burdens grievous to
be borne, and ye yourselves touch not the
burdens with one of your fingers,” and which
seems to imply that the hell, which the parson
talks so much about, is yawning for that kind of
character by reason of his hypocrisy, his time-
serving, and his capacity for finding out that
God is always on the side of the affluent. The
poorer class of worker has to live alongside
the Chinaman, to compete with him for
employment, and to have his daughters exposed
to the blandishments of the heathen’s opium den.
And the Rev. Brewster, with his cheerful
willingness that someone else should hump these
burdens while his own job is safe from cheap
Asiatic competition, is apt to be mixed up by the
unthinking with that Divine curse in the Gospel
of Luke, unless he gives very solid proof to the
contrary.

Australia’s Back Door.
S. Australia’s attitude with regard to the
Northern Territory is becoming one of sheer
despair and hopelessness. It has practically given
up all idea of making the settlement pay its way.
For the current year the estimate of expenditure,
just tabled in the S. Australian Assembly, is
£180,538, and only £57,286 of revenue is expected,
leaving a deficit ,of £123,252. Already the total
debt of the Territory is £2,904,676, or over £BOO
per head of the population ! S. Australia’s atti-
tude is, in effect —“The debt is so vast; what
does it matter if it is a little vaster?” Of course,
S. Australia is responsible for this debt. But it
knows that its responsibility is only nominal. It
never expects that it will itself have to make the
debt good. It knows that, sooner or later,
the Commonwealth will—must—take the Ter-
ritory over. South Australia is thus in the
position of a person provided with another man’s
promissory notes in blank ; and it is filling them
in at the rate of £125,000 a year. From any and
every point of. view this is folly which ought to
stop. It is absurd that the authority which
eventually will have to deal with the piper should
pass on to somebody else the calling of the tune.
The Commonwealth is as much on the spot as S.
Australia is ; in fact, until the transcontinental
line is built, the Territory could be better con-
trolled from either Melbourne, Sydney, or Bris-
bane than from Adelaide. But, even apart from
that, there can be no earthly sense in the Common-
wealth letting a trouble, which it will have to face
some day, grow in the hands of the State authority
which has really given the business up as a bad
job, and now simply lets things drift.

A Long-Lived Freetrade Lie.
N.S.W. Department of Labor report for 1904
remarks :
During the hearing of a dispute before the Arbitration
Court about the middle of the year, a statement was
made by one of the legal practitioners that, in the opinion
of the police, the Eariy Closing Act was the cause of girls
being drawn into habits of loitering about the streets.
Inquiries having been made by the Inspector-General of
Police, he reported that the police gave an absolute denial
to the statement, and regarded the same as a gross libel
on the vast number of shop girls concerned. The de-
partmental inspectors were in a position to deny the
truth of these uncalled-for remarks, and it was also
proved that the number of female students attending the
Technical College classes since this Act came into opera-
tion had increased over 35 per cent.
Curious the vitality of a lie ! This yarn—that if
the working classes were given more leisure
for enjoyment they would certainly make beasts
of themselves; and that, therefore, long hours
of labor must be maintained in the interests of
morality—was one of the stock arguments of the
old Freetrade “ economists ” in England. Now,
it is revived in N.S. Wales in the 20th century !
The Bulletin is game to wager, in the dark, that
“the legal practitioner” responsible for its re-
vival is a stout pillar of the Freetrade cause in
Sydney.
Morgan Takes a Risk.
Premier Morgan, of Queensland, has swallowed
his scruples, and agreed to a syndicate rail-
way to open up the alleged mineral wealth
of the Gulf. A syndicate railway is a hard
thing to swallow at any time; but for Morgan
and his allies it can be said, anyway, that they
were between a devil with a very sharp tail and a
sea with a bottom that Queensland can barely
touch. The Gulf mineral country cannot be
developed without a railway; and the Govern-
ment certainly wouldn’t be justified in building a
railway. If the unproved mines petered out, the
railway would be merely two streaks of steel
running through poorish pastoral country, with
never a hope of closer settlement. At the
best, since it runs up to the Gulf, it empties
itself into a port where lightering will be
necessary: and that would be an extra handi-
cap on agriculture, even if agriculture were
otherwise possible. Further, though it is a syn-
dicate railway, the original mining syndicate—
the Queensland S.L. Mining Co. —and the tram-
way syndicate are practically one and the same.
In N.S.W. a mining co. could take up a line-track
on lease and build a line without asking for Par-
liamentary authority; and the solidly-established
principle that the people must own the ways of
communication isn’t thereby seriously impaired.
The Queensland case has points of resemblance to
this. Almost the last Bill that a mining-railway-
syndicate did put through in N.S.W. was by the
Tarrawingee Co. The line cost something over
£lOO,OOO, and, inside ten years, the State was im-
plored to take it over for about £15,000. There is
no mine for it to serve now—a train runs over it
once a week or so, and occasionally has a
bale of wool to carry. If Morgan were justified
in building a similar N.Q. line, he would be also
j ustified in pushing the State into mine-speculation.
To do it on borrowed money would be unthink-
able ; and with a House of Fat to fight at every
step, and with the heaviest interest bill per head
on earth to struggle with, Morgan has too much
difficulty in getting revenue for the solid things,
to think seriously of taking any of it for the
gambles. So Morgan and his allies may be
spared the dead cat of scorn and the brick of
punishment that Australia does well to save up
for the railway-syndicate-monger. It is possible
that Queensland may yet have to throw the dead
cat at itself over the business —it depends upon
how it turns out. All that it can do meantime,
having tried a dangerous experiment, is to see
how it pans out before trying any more of the
same kind.
Victorian Laborites.
Victorian Laborites, if not inside, certainly out-
side Parliament, seem to be suffering from decay
of energy. A conference of delegates has been
sitting for some weeks at the Trades Hall consider-
ing the problem of lack of employment. There
are over 100 representatives, of whom about 30
attend on an average ; one or two societies have
already withdrawn their delegates in disgust.
Various abstruse and entirely theoretical propo-
sals have been mooted and tentatively adopted,
but the practical work of the conference has been
nil. Certainly Tom Mann has animadverted on
the sufficiency of a six-hours day, while one or two
delegates have gone several better and assured
their hearers that a two-hours day would suffice to
keep the worker up to his present standard of living.
At a recent meeting, however, one delegate got
tired of these didactic, speculative vaporings and
remarked that progressive legislation to protect
wage-earners and to cope with the unemployed
difficulty was an absolute farce unless it were pre-
ceded by the imposition of a Protective tariff.
Thereupon several delegates fell on him with great
tonnage, and asked whether he would vote for a
Freetrader who was supported by the Labor
party or a Protectionist who wasn’t. The
answer was straight— ‘ ‘ For the Protectionist every
time; ” and this delegate narrowly escaped
being thrown out on his ear. This con-
ference is supposed to be considering the
problem of unemployment, yet apparently dele-
gates can’t understand that the chief cause
of unemployment is want of work, and that
while Australia imports some £40,000,000 worth
of goods annually, nearly all of which might be
made here, there is certain to be a lack of em-
ployment. Also, they don’t seem to grasp the fact
that, were these goods made here, the country
would not only have the goods, but also the money
paid for producing and manufacturing them, and
would therefore be so much the richer at the end
of each year. When the money is in the country
there is some chance for the party which
aims at a fair division of wealth, but if there
isn’t money to divide it can’t be divided.
No paper recognises more than The Bulletin
that it is a huge calamity for 'Victoria’s fertile
Western District to be monopolised by a knot of
huge land-grabbers, but that' is better than haying
no fertile Western District at all. While it is
there, the people will recover it some day, and if
it wasn’t there they wouldn’t. The same rule
applies to many other forms of wealth.
The Marriage Shop Scandal.
From the N.S.W. Parliamentary records of last
week :
Mr. Sullivan asked the Colonial Secretary: Has his
attention been called to the actions of the people con-
ducting' Marriage Establishments in the city, and will he
consider the advisability of taking their licenses from
them ?
Mr. Hogue answered : The fact that certain people are
conducting places for the solemnisation of marriages is,
of course, known, but as the law stands, there is a diffi-
culty in dealing with the persons associated with these
institutions.
This professes to be a “good” Government, a
Government of Josephites, but it can answer
nothing but a weak non possum when asked if it
will put an end to one of the grave social
scandals of the city. In some of the marriage
shops of Sydney often conducted by some
broken-down and disreputable clergyman —the
most serious contract of life can be entered into at
a moment’s notice, and by practically anybody who
has the small fee to pay. Cases have come before
the courts of drunken persons being married in
these shops, of others being forced into wedlock,
of others being persuaded into bigamous alliances.
Apart from these cases, there can be no doubt that
some harm results to the community from the
degrading of the marriage ceremony by its
celebration in such places, by couples who
in some instances seem to adjourn to the
Domain for their honeymoon. Yet the remedy
is so obvious that there is no need for the
evil to exist another month if the Government
is willing to take action. Let the law insist that
the marriage ceremony shall in every case
be a civil one before a duly authorised
officer of the State—as is the law in most Euro-
pean countries. The Law should make the civil
marriage alone binding, whilst leaving to lovers
the option of taking part in any subsequent or
preceding church ceremonies. The idea is not to
abolish the church rite, but to make the civil rite
the essential one. Such a law would sweep away
the evils of the marriage shop—the drunken wed-
ding, the casual wedding arranged at the street
corner, the forced wedding in which one or other
party is a victim to fraud or fear—for all unions
would have to be sanctioned by the State official,
who would have no fee inducement to make him
willing to marry anybody anyhow.
