Even in the N.S.W. Railway Commissioners’ re-
ports, which consist mostly of large slabs of
figures, there is some pathos. One return shows
regularly “ the removals of employees from the
service ” for the preceding quarter; and two-
thirds of the “removals” in the mechanical engi-
neer’s department run like this : “ Brown, John,
apprentice, ss. per day ; apprenticeship expired.”
In the last three months 15 engineering appren-
tices were thus passed out; the State had taught
them their business, and then couldn’t or
wouldn’t find any further use for them—and mean-
time it was sending to foreign countries for
engines, on the ground, among others’, that
it could - not get them built here fast
enough. In the same quarter, though it
couldn’t or wouldn’t employ the men it had
trained, it engaged 19 apprentices in the same
department, putting them on at lOd. per day;
and in five years time it looks as though this 19,
and the 19 or so who are taken on every quarter,
will be passed out, too. Then when those trained
mechanics ask for work, we shall probably make
some remarks concerning our great natural
resources, and tell them to go on the land, where
they won’t know barley from a Scotch thistle ;
and the Labor party will manfully “sink the fiscal
issue” and let the work that the country has
trained these young Australians to do come in
from Belgium or Brummagen or somewhere. If
this country puts up one sign, “No factories
wanted here,” in fairness to these youngsters it
ought also to put up another one—“ No appren-
tices wanted here.” They ought to be warned
from the start that their purpose in life is to
wrestle with chick-weed and cultivate the
acquaintance of the cow.
Speaking of the scenes in the House of Representa-
tives when Attorney-General Isaacs was fighting
the stonewall, Sydney Telegraph threw in this
bit of description (the parentheses are not the
Telegraph’s) :
The demoralisation soon became worse. The Speaker
retired, and Dr. Salmon, the Chairman of Committees,
took his place. Some members brought in blankets and
pillows, and lay prone on the benches. The Deputy-
Speaker ruled one of Mr. Fuller’s (of N.S.W.) arguments
out of order. Mr. Conroy (of N.S.W.) proposed to dissent
from the ruling. Some disorder followed. Mr. Conroy
(of N.S.W.) was named for standing and shouting out
something. He expressed regret, and the naming was
withdrawn. Then Mr. Fuller (of N.S.W.) moved dissent
from the previous ruling. Mr. Conroy (of N.S.W.) ob-
jected to members bringing in bed-clothes and sprawling
about the benches, tucked up in blankets and lying- on
pillow’s. While this was being discussed great disorder
prevailed. The Deputy-Speaker seemed temporarily to
lose all control of the Chamber. He named Mr. Joseph
Cook (of N.S. W.). There was a scene of great excitement.
Order, after great difficulty, w’as restored. Mr. Cook (of
N.S.W.) withdrew some remarks that had offended the
Deputy-Speaker. . . . Mr. Deakin never saw any of these
scenes. He was probably dreaming of that Federation
which w’as to be sacred as a shrine, or ruminating on the
splendid results which, under his direction, it was
achieving.
But when Deakin advocated Federation, how was
he to know that this small minority of shrieking
lunatics from N.S.W. was going to turn the legis-
lative hall into a bear-garden every time it wasn’t
allowed to have exactly its own way in deciding
what business was to be done ?

The agitation that has recently been going on
among the wharf laborers of Sydney against the
award of the Arbitration Court is a matter that
every friend of the Arbitration principle must
deeply regret. The whole purpose of Arbitration
is to promote industrial peace, and to prevent
strikes, or anything that even colorably resembles
strikes. Of course, either party may rebel against
an Arbitration award, and when that happens the
law has extensive pow’ers to compel obedience. But
every time one or other of the parties talks
rebellion it is a misfortune ; and when any party
discusses possible ways and means whereby it
may refuse to obey an award, and yet avoid the
penalty of disobedience on some technical point,
it simply seeks to prove that Arbitration is a
failure. If the wharf laborers succeeded in evading
the award of the court, they would thereby
have proved that, in this particular case, Arbitra-
tion had been a failure, which would provide
the Tory party with a more plausible argument
than any it has yet possessed for demanding the
abolition of the law. Of course, the fact will
remain that the Tory himself, when the award of
an ordinary civil court goes against him, resists it
to the bitter end, and appeals over and over again,
and tries to dodge the award on every possible
side issue, and leaves no stone unturned in his
efforts to bring the law into contempt and to
prove the whole system of justice is a failure, and
that he flatly disobeys the courts as far as
lie dares. But that won’t prevent him declaring
Arbitration a discredited thing if the employees
A Ballad of Stinking Fish.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Lo ! a song is sung with a blatant tongue as shrill
as the Fiend could wish ;
Take round the hat for the sake of Fat and his
Ballad of Stinking Fish !
Let others sing of arms that cling, and build the
wanton rhyme;
Fat’s only theme is ancient bream and ’couta past
its prime.
Some bards prefer to chant of myrrh and Love’s
white-odored breast;
Fat trills one song of perfume strong and trills the
same with zest.
’Tis never a lay of music gay, or of dancers’ skirts
a-swish —
Fat fills the street with his chanson sweet—his
Ballad of Stinking Fish !
And around the world that song is hurled by each
itinerant Fat
From all hotels that Tory swells are wont to tarry
at.
Oh, he chants that tune in the first saloon at
Naples and Bombay;
Yea, he sings that song at far Hong-Kong, at
Paris and Mandalay.
From Cheop’s crest ’tis blown with zest across
the turbid Nile;
In ’Frisco-town that ballad brown is reeled off by
the mile.
There’s never a spot where that human blot doesn’t
bang his loud tin-dish —
Where he does not sing that fearsome thing—his
Ballad of Stinking Fish ;

At Last a Move.
Prime Minister Deakin suddenly shed his affa-
bility last week. N.S.W. Premier Joseph
Carruthers was shocked to get from him a firm,
dignified letter, in which all the little shifts and
evasions and quibbles of that clamorous insect
on the Federal capital site question were merci-
lessly reviewed. Short of publicly spanking
Joseph, the Prime Minister could not have more
effectively rebuked and shamed him. Then, turning
to the Federal Opposition, the other insect which
has been holding up Federal business, Deakin
made to it also some firm remarks. The
political larrikins who misrepresent the bulk of
New South Wales in the Australian Parliament
were so shocked by the Prime Minister’s intima-
tion that tom-foolery must cease and business
begin, that they converted Parliamentary proceed-
ings into a riot, and for long weary stretches bad
language, bad air and bad manners filled the halls of
legislature. For 57i hours last week the riot of
tongues continued, and this week opened with
Babel still in full blast and the Government still
firmly facing the howling pack of the Opposition.
It was a repetition of the Foreign-trade party’s
tactics, which nearly killed C. C. Kingston and
made a wreck of George Turner’s health, and
succeeded in completely wrecking many an Aus-
tralian home by whittling away the tariff; only
that it raged more furiously since it was met
with more firmness than Barton was able to
summon up. The end was a victory for
decency and good government. On Monday an
exhausted Opposition agreed that if ordinary
sittings were resumed, the debate on the closure
resolutions would finish on Thursday. The
Bulletin trusts that, having won after an arduous
struggle, Prime Minister Deakin will now make
no concession to the beaten Opposition, but will
enter on next week’s business armed with full
powers to suppress any disorderly debate. The
men who stand against him deserve no con-
sideration and no courtesy. They follow
wild-beast tactics in their political warfare,
and when beaten into submission should be
trusted as much as wild beasts —as much and no
more. A feature of this new development of
firmness in politics has been the frenzied efforts of
the Foreign-trade press to mislead the public into
the belief that, in proposing to give the Federal
House of Representatives power to closure useless
and disorderly debate, Deakin was bringing
forward a novel and tyrannous scheme. As a
matter of fact, every civilised Parliament has
such power. The House of Commons has it;
every State legislature of Australia has it; G. H.
Reid, when holding office under the whip of
the Labor party in the N.S.W. State Parliament,
used it mercilessly to push through the Foreign-
trade tariff with which he sent the workers from the
factories to the sand-hill relief works. The Aus-
tralian Parliament was, so far as The Bulletin
knows, the only Parliament in the world without
a closure Standing Order, and that was because
it was practically without any Standing Orders at
all, the work of framing rules of debate having
been neglected by the first Ministry. The absence
of law has given the Foreign-trade Opposition a
chance of showing its love for anarchy and rowdy-
ness. For the future there is to be some curb—it
is to be hoped an effectual curb.

A Remarkable Judge.
According to the news columns of Sydney Tele-
graph (21/11/’O5), N.S.W. Judge Rogers made
some quaint remarks in the case of a man named
Norris. This is the D.T. report:—
John Norris, described as a mechanical engineer, was
indicted for aiding and abetting a man named Dreager,
now in gaol, who had been found by night with house-
breaking implements in his possession. Dreager and
Norris were acquaintances of some months standing, and
Norris had given the police the information which had
secured Dreager’s arrest. Dreager- declared that
Norris had given him the tools, and otherwise
assisted him in a contemplated burglary, while Norris’s
story was that Dreager told him that he intended
committing a robbery, and he informed the police. . . .
The jury found Norris guilty, and it was then sought to
have him released on probation. His employer came for-
ward and declared that he was one of the best men he
had ever had. He was honest, and could do work of a
class which had never before been done in the State, but
had to be sent away to England. His shop was open to
the man whenever he could return. Norris’s wife, lead-
ing their little daughter by the hand, also made a
piteous appeal to his Honor on her husband’s behalf.
He was her only means of support. . . . His Honor said
prisoner was apparently one of the very tradesmen wanted
in the country, if they were not kept out by “ this dog-
in-the-manger Federal legislation. We want the best
people out here, not the second best,” added his Honor.
“ The whole thing is terribly disastrous to the country, I
think, speaking as a New South Welshman.”
From the same issue :
His Honor, refusing an application made on behalf of
the prisoner that he should be released on probation
under the First Offenders Act, sentenced Norris to a
term of six months’ imprisonment with hard labor.
It is surely time that someone in authority asked
this silly judge what he was thinking about. No
Federal law or dog-in-the-manger policy had kept
the valuable Nohris out of Australia. He was
obviously and plainly there. Even a drunk could
have seen that he was there, and his presence
gives a flat contradiction to Rogers’ amazing
remarks about such valuable people as he not
being admitted. And then, after declaring that
Norris was just the sort of man the country
wanted, and almost weeping over his preciousness,
Rogers hadn’t the moral courage to back his
opinion by releasing him under the First Offen-
ders Act, so that the country could get the benefit
of him. He apparently believed Norris was
innocent. He was fully empowered by law to set
him free under the F.O. Act. He was impressed
with the appeal of the wife and child, with
probably hunger before them if he
sent Norris to gaol. And then he
covered his retreat with irrelevant
and useless denunciation of a law
that had nothing to do with the
case, and after his foolish attempt to
bring the law into contempt he sent
the bread-winner to gaol. It was
desperately cold comfort for the
wife and child who can’t feed on his
Honor’s weeps about Federal legis-
lation ; the most callous person who
ever sat on the bench couldn’t
have given them anything much
colder than what they got from
his sympathetic and deeply-moved
Honor. Apparently either Judge
Rogers believes that “the very
tradesmen wanted in this country ”
are criminals and accomplices of
house-breakers, or he believed that
Norris wasn’t a criminal, yet hadn’t
the courage to set him free as the
law permitted.
Sec. Niel Nielsen, of N.S.W. Parliamentary
Labor party, writes :
Bulletin (Nov. 16) says that only three members of the
Labor party, Griffith, Meehan, and Miller., voted
against building the new Public Library with borrowed
money. As a matter of fact the motion for which only
three members of the Labor party voted was to put the
library in Government House. Mr. Griffith’s motion,
moved later on, that the library be built out of revenue,
was voted for by 13 members of the Labor party.
Fact is that, when Solemn Lee proposed that
the work be done, Griffith asked whether the
money was to come out of loans. Lee
replied that it was to be got that way. At once
Griffith said (D.T. report, Nov. 8) that “this
was another proposal to invest loan money in a
non-revenue-producing undertaking, and the
Labor party, to which he belonged, was opposed
to that.” He therefore proposed that the work
be referred back to the Works Committee, with a
view to inquire as to using Government House
for a library, and thus preventing the expendi-
ture of loan money on a non-productive work.
And, though the Minister had announced that if
his proposal was carried it meant using loan
money for this unproductive work, only two
members voted with Griffith to send the pro-
posal back to the Committee in order to avoid
smashing one of the Labor party’s planks. Sub-
sequently, Griffith having failed to save the
party that way, a direct motion that the work be
paid for out of revenue was submitted ; and the
13 members of the Labor party who voted for it
deserve the credit a man should have for
doing what he has pledged himself to do. What
ought to happen to the others of the party who
didn’t trouble to do even that much is another
matter.

Sydney Telegraph in its alleged “reports” of the
Federal Parliament, lies with a venom and perti-
nacity that passes all bounds even of political party
journalism. For instance, Nov. 16 :
Only a month or two ago, when proceedings did not
please some of the Labor supporters of the Government,
Mr. Deakin rushed down to the House in a fume and
tabled some extraordinary proposals to allow members
playing billiards to be counted as present and doing their
Parliamentary work. This fantastic scheme he abandoned
almost as soon as he submitted it.
Those “extraordinary proposals” did not amount
to what the Telegraph says. Let that pass:
where there is the slightest opening for misrepre-
sentation or vilification the Telegraph may take
license. But the statement that Mr. Deakin aban-
doned the proposed new Standing Order (that, on
the attention of the Speaker being called to the
state of the House, he need not “count the House”
if he were satisfied of a quorum being within its
precincts ready to do business) is no twisting or
mere misrepresentation. It’s a straight-out false-
hood, and its falseness must be within the know-
ledge of that journal, unless it is prepared to plead
guilty to a shocking degree of ignorance of Parlia-
mentary procedure. The proposed new Standing
Order is still among the draft Standing Orders,
awaiting the adoption of the House.

The Sydney afternoon-tea gossips, who call them-
selves the “ Australian Women’s League,” got a
mild shock at a recent foregathering. It was
moved and seconded that colored laborers should
be admitted to tropical Australia for the “de-
velopment of its natural resources,” and the
meeting received the idea enthusiastically, and
said “how nice,” or words to that effect. Then
a Miss Breciienridge, a visiting ex-president of
the largest women’s club in San Antonio, Texas
U.S.A.), rose and remarked that: —
History showed that all nations which had admitted
black agricultural laborers to the heart of a country had
acknowledged their mistake. Every servile race brought
in for this purpose had a disastrous effect upon the
country they were brought into to develop. The trouble
would come after the country was developed, and every
woman who assisted to introduce those lower types
would feel her heart rent in twain at the mischief done.
With race trouble came race hatred. She strongly
advised the women of Australia not to let this evil he
done to their country. She knew something of the lower
African blacks.
This unexpected language, from one who comes
from a country where they understand these
matters, so upset the meeting that it was quite a
time before it was calm enough to signify in the
usual manner that it was in favor of the motion.
Those anti-Peace demonstrations of the deluded
jingo Jap have ended in a great wail of distress
from the north end of Monkey Island. The
roaring “patriots”—they mostly stayed around
Tokio whilst the bloodshed was proceeding—are
at last in the secret as to why Russia was able to
make peace on practically its own terms. The
local Government knew what was coming. A
considerable part of its swollen-headed country
is famine-stricken. This season’s rice crop was a
failure, and the unhappy agriculturists are said by
the cable to be eating baked roots, likewise horse-
THE JAP FAMINE.
In certain districts in Japan children are being sold as
servants at from Is. 7d. to 4s. each. —Daily Paper.
flesli (which is terrible tucker for men reared on
rice) and they are selling their children for about
two-and-sixpence each to be slaves of the
comparatively well-to-do citizens who screamed
for more berlud. The cable in this case may he
accepted as a truthful thing, for it sounded a
warning note re the rice crop when peace negotia-
tions were first talked about. The Monkey has
had his glorious war. The famine troubles are
only beginning. Vast sums of money that might
have been devoted to the succour of the starving
crowd were blown into smoke during the recent
campaign, and the poor little brown Empire finds
itself burdened with a legion of damaged con-
valescent soldiers, needing to be fed up with some-
thing more strengthening than root cakes. When
Russia went to that famous negotiation it also
took a spectre with it. It was about as close on
revolution as Japan was on famine. But the
Russians hid their spectre better than the Japs
did, and put up the bigger bluff of the two.

Princes Court Sensational Novelties. *
After an interval of sixteen years, so far
as Sydney is concerned, “The Sorcerer” was
staged by the Repertoire Co. at the Criterion on
Saturday night. The audience took the piece in
a most cordial and friendly spirit, and seemed to
enjoy it all the better for having sampled its
pleasant melodies years ago, and laughed at its
grotesqueries and arabesques. A large quan-
tity of the audience present on Saturday was
born about the time “The Sorcerer” last in-
canted in Sydney, and the section agreed,
as the lyric comedy progressed, that life was
not quite a blank with the old man and
the old woman, seeing that they lived in an age
when Gilbert was young and fresh and Sullivan
tore music out of the sweet young lyre of a great
intellect. Howard Vincent plays John Welling-
ton Wells, the role formerly taken by the late
William Elton. Vernon can stand up in coils, so
to speak, and can waggle like an eel dancing on
its tail. His incantation scene with the teapot is
a notable bit of burlesque. The Bulletin wonders
if the Witch that King Saul consulted cut such
capers before the spirit, of the hairy old
Samuel consented to appear. If so, the
monarch fell down with mirth, not fear, and died
next day at Mount Gilboa because he was
laughing too hard to be able to spear a Philistine.
“ The Sorcerer ” is followed by “ Trial By Jury,”
in which Miss Vinia de Loitte distinguishes
herself in the character of the plaintiff. On
Saturday night next the fairy opera of “ lolan the ”
will be staged.
** * *
“The Battle and the Breeze,” by Aus-
tralian actor-manager George Darrell, was staged
at Sydney Royal on Saturday and showed
that that old actor-manager knows the recipe
for a successful melodrama. There is plenty
of breeze, battle, bloodshed, bushranging,
abduction and so on ; and quite the only
bar to the complete gallerj enjoyment of this
smolcing-hot sensationalism is that “ absolutely no
smoking is allowed in the theatre.” Yet the bill of
fare would blend well with a strong tobacco flavor.
Seriously the success of this piece, of Pratt’s
“ Thunderbolt,” and the like, show that there is
no need to go outside of Australia for the manu-
facture of robust melodrama. Australians can
write quite badly enough for its produc-
tion on a large scale, and the “local
color ” with which they are able to flavor
sensationalism makes it a little more palatable to
those of the audience who want something more
than plain blood. Darrell’s work is full of diggers,
Chinamen, Maoris, and other “ features”, and rui s
briskly from beginning to end.
The cast of Anderson’s co. has been
strengthened by the return of Miss Duggan and
the engagement of Harry Diver, than whom there
are few better in Australia at melodramatic work.
(This is saying nothing of his capacity for other
work ; his lago was among the finest this country
has seen, j In “ The Battle and the Breeze” he has
a light comedy part, and carries it through well.
George Cross, Edmund Duggan and Bert Bailey
also stand out from the ruck; Edmund Duggan
acts with a vigor that is convincing.

Just as might have been expected,
“ Merely Mary Ann” smelt Christmas coming,
and suddenly gave notice that her mother was ill,
and she was very sorry, but she couldn't stay any
longer. Anyway, she left Her Majesty’s on
Tuesday ; and as this paper is passing through
the mill, Miss Tittell Brune is playing “ Sunday”
by night and working at “ The Second Mrs.
Tanqueray ”by day. The latter, in which Miss
Brune has not been seen in Sydney, will be first
staged on Saturday night. Meantime those who
haven’t seen “ Sunday ” are offered the hint that
it is Miss Brune’s size exactly, and that she does
at least as well in it as in anything else, even if
she doesn’t do better.

At Sydney Palace Miss Lily Dampier has
been tearing (be careful how that word is pro-
nounced) in the role of Lady Isabel in “East
Lynne,” but she was announced to be a princess
m “ The Postmistress of the Czar” on Wednesday
night. As this issue was then in the press, it is
impossible to say till next week whether the Czar
got the letter with an insufficient stamp on it or
not. The name of the drama is full of possi-
bilities of battle, murder, and sudden bomb.
•* * *
The Sydney Amateur Orchestral Society
closed its season with a river of melody.
Lady Northcote’s presentation piece, Dvorak’s
“ Nature,” proved to be a glorified and amplified
spring song, and Mr. James Fairfax’s present, a
Tschaikowsky Symphony, kept it excellent com-
pany.
** * *
Sydney Tivoli has, taking it all round, the
strongest bill it has had for years ; and the Tivoli
public recognises it, so that only the early bird
catches the seat. To the other attractions which,
a week ago, were anointed with the oil of good
words, has been added the remarkable musical
performance of the Jackson family. Sydney has
heard the same family before, but it hasn’t had
the same performance, and isn’t likely to soon have
another like it.
** * *
The beauties of “Veronique” promise to
grow and ripen in Melbourne estimation like the
apples whereof Florence Young sings in the second
act of that pleasant French opera. “ Veronique”
is without question the most charming musical
novelty presented by the Williamson co. since
“Robin Hood,” and it has the lightness and
gaiety which De Koverfi s work lacked. Also the
story harmonises with the music, reminding one
of the English “ Dorothy ” in this respect,
whereas “ Robin Hood ” had a serio-comic
libretto, mostly out of touch with the
score. The advt. describes “Veronique” as
“ the forerunner of another golden age of comic
opera,” but it might be more aptly proclaimed the
first of a new series of humorous sentimental
operas. The humor has the quality of mercy. It
is not strained. There is no irrelevant funny
business for George Lauri, no cheap parody of
somebody’s performance from a rival show, no
buffoonery that isn’t quite legitimate and accept-
able. Lauri, all the same, has never been so
admirably grotesque as in the character of
Mons. Coquenard, the warlike florist. A
subtle comedian could do it in a more
French and frivolous spirit, but Lauri’s
genial method is very serviceable. As much
may be said for Bantock in the smaller part of a
bailiff attached to the French court, and for Miss
Clifton as an incredible member of the Old
Nobility. Their humor is good humor, the more
effective for being more subdued than usual. The
singing principals are considerably handicapped
by circumstances. Lyrics written for French
words are not easily translated into Eng-
lish, and the clear, slow enunciation of both
Miss Thomas and Miss Young, which gives
full value to a Gold Country ballad, is a
drawback when they come to the dainty
melodies of Messager. The substitution (last
Saturday night) of Haigh Jackson for Doran has
put more go into the performance, and the new
hero strengthens the picture, seeing that he
hasn’t the air of a footman who has forgotten to
bring along a letter on a tray. But there is still
the same want of fluency and sweetness in the
ladies’ warbling. Their voices seem to be mis-
placed. Perhaps the most applauded number in
the opera is the “ Swing ” duet, by Miss Thomas
and the baritone, although it is thm and common-
place in comparison with the Apple song afore-
said, which gets most of its charm from the
chorus. And neither of these good things is so
well done as the chorus to the groom’s song in the
first act. W. S. Percy is a plump and lively little
groom who subsequently gets married to Miss
Connie Milne. She likewise is a festive little
item in a capital entertainment.

Nancy and Dorgan with soft music, such as is set
apart for ghosts and villains. But the descriptive
accompaniment is now dispensed with. Not a
sound is heard, not a sinister note. The criminal
proceedings are as silent as the Burial of Sir John
Moore.
** * *
Bland Holt is about to close “ The Betting
Book ”at Melbourne Royal. Melbourne for the
present has forgotten the ill effects of the gambling
evil, and lost its burning interest in the tote. A
revival of “ With Flying Colors” will restore our
rolling nautical brethren of the bulldog breed to
the Holtian stage on Saturday night.
* * * *■
The final week of Wirths’ Circus was
inaugurated last Saturday with a burst of exciting
behavior on the part of the menagerie. Whilst a
number of animals were rehearsing a Happy
Family act in the arena, a blind jaguar disturbed
the domestic peace by putting his teeth into
the neck of a deerhound, and although
the dog escaped with his life, the shock
to his system was so great that a sym-
pathetic deer dropped dead of heart failure,
which caused some dismay to the neighbor-
ing elephants. Later in the morning the other
deer, finding itself mateless, broke from captivity
and started to swim up the Yarra to Richmond.
The fugitive was eventually lassooed from the
river by Jack Kelly, and led back in triumph to
the tent, where it took a fit. If the elephants go
mad during the week, Wirths will get a tre-
mendous farewell advt. for a prosperous season.
** * *
The Circus at Fitzgeralds’ Building con-
tinues to meet a declining demand for educated
gee-gee and dummy clown, and the tumult of its
invincible brass band mingles with the distant
blast of the band at Prince’s Court. Stephenson’s
co. has retired from the Bijou, but the Gaiety
keeps open house as usual, and the Athenaeum
Hall is preparing to receive the Corricks, whose
trade mark is Marvellous.
& 3* &
Payen and Lucie, a highly-finished pair of
gymnasts, are the latest arrivals at Melbourne
Opera House. Fortunately they are not grave
orthodox gymnasts, but dissemblers whose clever-
ness is intended to be funny and achieves its
purpose. The billiard-ball Brunins are doing as
before, likewise Bluett and the Grahams, and other
people, including the comely coon-songstress with
the irresistible smile. When she desires the
congregation to stand up and sing hallelujah,
there is gladness in the air at Rickards’.
** * *
Charles Holloway has for the moment
abandoned the Horse Drama at Adelaide Royal,
and is now engaged in showing “Why Woman
Sins ” —a subject on which the steepled city pro-
fesses a vast ignorance ; the cheaper parts of the
house are, however, showing a cheerful readiness
to learn. The Jackson family and the automatic
Frenchman have moved on from the Tivoli, and
the programme is now mostly song, dance, and
dialogue. Miss Ada Delroy has a good gauzy
dance—not new, but well put on—lighted by
flames from below the stage. Bostock and Womb-
well have a menagerie-circus on a popular pitch
near the Central Market. The colored Ally from
Tokio gives a neat and plucky selection of balanc-
ing feats, and the larger animals are also skilful
performers.
AT POVERTY POINT.
The Melbourne police-court case o'f Grant Hervey, Walter
Baker and the Revolver took a humorous turn last week
after the tall prisoner—on bail—had been granted a
further remand. Baker protested “ strenuously ” against
the' bailing of his adversary on the ground that
he was vaguely suspected of still carrying his his-
torical firearm, for the purpose of shooting at some
more Baker. Then the magistrate, to settle all doubt as
to whether Hervey had any deadly weapons concealed
under his cheerful demeanor, ordered the young man to
feel in his pocket, so he turned his pockets inside out and
produced a pair of nail-scissors. Whereupon the Court
breathed again. It was clearly impossible for even the
most terrified Baker to get shot with a pair of scissors.
Frank M. Clark presented Melbourne Gaiety audiences
with a biograph version of “ The Jewish Persecutions in
Russia ” a few nights after the massacres had commenced,
and advertised that, “in producing this picture, depicting
as it does the terrible persecutions of a law-abiding people,
he has secured the same at Enormous Cost.” Evidently
the great expense was caused through having the biograph
films cabled from Russia. Then, of course, there will be
the cost of keeping a photographer in every spot on earth
where serious bloodshed is lilc ly to happen, to say nothing
of the loss when the Cossack spears the camera or the
Chunchuses gallop on it.
“ Colander ” : Bland Holt locates his tote, in “ The
Betting Book,” at the rear of a tobacconist’s shop. To
attain complete realism he should place it behind a tea-
shop, and make a small addition to this effect Enter
from street door suspiciously' tall man—might be a
“ John ”in plain clothes. He advances to the counter,
and attempts to lift the flap and pass towards the devious
passages leading to the tote-yard. Tote Myrmidon behind
the counter (springing forward and stopping him):
“ Well, what’s for you?” Would-be Punter: “ Oh, you
know ! ” T.M. : “ No, I don’t know. What d’yer want? ”
W.P. : “Oh, you know all right. I want to get through
to the tote.”' T.M. (virtuously indignant): “Strike me
dizzy! Tote ! Look ’ere ; don’t you come ’ere talkin’
about no tote. D’yer want to spoil a man’s business ? If
yer want ter buy any tea ” (pointing to an open box of
drought-stricken leaf and a few dummy packets with the
dust of years on them) “ well, buy it. If yer don’t want
it, git, or yer’ll dam soon be made ter.” The would-be
punter (who in reality has no connection with “the
force ”) realises that his size is against him, and departs,
betless and tealess, meeting on his way out twenty or
thirty “ safe ” punters, '•>ho are straightway allowed to
pass thesacred flap, presumably to sample some Pekoe in
the parlor.

Nellie Stewart is putting her foot down with a firm
hand on the autograph nuisance. She advertised in the
Melbourne papers, the other day, that in future she will
forward her autograph only when the collector encloses
with his or her request half-a-crown for the Dis-
tressed Actors’ Fund. Melbourne Princess’s corres-
pondence - bag may 7 now be expected to weigh
half -a - hundredweight per day less than formerly.
But why 7 doesn’t Sweet Nell make a larger and sterner
effort to save her suffering country, and only 7 send her
signature to the applicant who encloses 10s. in reduction
of the Public Debt? Miss Stewart might yet get
Australia out of pawn with her unaided autograph.
“ Alone she did it.”
“Edy'son”: “The Bishop’s Qarriage,” secured by Mr.
Musgrove at such enormous expense that the advertise-
ment is fairly staggered at the thought of it, is remark-
able at least in presenting the most preposterous tail-tag
I have ever struck in serious drama. The play' would not
be an extremely bad play, as plays go, if it knew its own
finish, which happens harmoniously at the end of the
penultimate act. How a manager could have_ failed to see
that that wasteful, ridiculous, and excessive last act
must dull all the good impressions and send people away
soured and abusive is incomprehensible to me. Play's are
judged by final impressions, and tedious last acts have
destroyed many' a drama. Then the author gives his
heroine an epilog-ue—a long, dull epilogue in an age that
can’t sit with patience under the best epilog-ue of which
Shakspeare was capable, and that settles it ; The people
are too polite to scream, but they 7 feel the injury 7 all the
same.

Mabel Morg-an, who scored as Princess Soo-Soo in the
recent Melbourne revival of “ A Chinese Hone.vmoon,” is
a Richmond (Melb.) girl. She is comparatively new to

“Hamer”: Holloway as Matt Gabbett in Dampier’s
“ Term,” at Sydney Criterion, was not as horrible as
many who have enacted the gentle cannibal creature.
There are some players of such parts who love to get all
the gore they can out of them. A good old actor with
this taste shocked Sydney at one time by his version of
Bill Sikes. The version consisted mostly of a bludgeon
topped off with a perforated india-rubber ball, inside
which was a blood-soaked sponge. With this weapon he
whacked Nancy on the head in the presence of the audi-
ence until that lady’s visage was pleasing to his artistic
soul. Then people started to faint.
The absurdity of the average professional mummer’s
pretensions can be measured by trie comparative excel-
lence of a really clever amateur. Lawrence Hardinge,
who played Captain Coddington in the amateur produc-
tion of “ In Town,” at Melbourne Princess’s, is, thusfar,
a more convincing actor than many a competent pro.
who has had years of stage experience. Granting that
Hardinge was an excellent amateur, it is quite as certain
that he was a novice even as amateurs go—one quite un-
versed in the theatrical “art” whereof the old ac-tor
talks so impressively. The other week he rattled through
the Coddington part in a perfectly natural style, and a
week later he walked on as a member of the Musgrove
Co. and took a minor character in a melodrama with a
distinctive, easy virility. It will be interesting to see
whether he loses some of his naturalness in acquiring
“ professional polish.”

zenana comprised two French girls, three of Welsh

When Wirths’ circus was at Ballarat a while ago the
rain fell in torrents, and some of it came through the
canvas roof upon the “ Death Trap Loop ” track. But
the tent was packed, and Ohefalo took his chance down
the damp incline. He said afterwards that it was the
riskiest trip he had ever made. His language before he
started must have been very choice Italian.
“ 0.K.” : Ellen Terry must have been very young—little
more than a child actress—when she was billed as
“ Nellie,” as alleged in a recent Bulletin par. At the
age of 16-17 she married G. F. Watts, the painter, and
retired from the stage. A brief and stormy period of
cohabitation with her unsuitable husband ended in his
signing a deed of separation, by which he agreed to allow
her £3OO a year, to be reduced to £2OO if she went back
to the stage. For the next few years the golden-lnured
charmer lived in seclusion at the little village of Har-
penden, 20 miles from London. During that time things
happened whereof the world heard only rumors, and
Watts, who lived apart from the world in his London
studio, heard absolutely nothing at all. Mrs. Watts was
perhaps a woman of four-and-twenty, or thereabouts,
when a London manageress (Mrs. John Wood) made a
mysterious announcement that “a charming actress
would shortly return to the stage ”at Mrs. J. W.’s theatre.
The great unknown proved to be Miss Ellen Terry.
The notices of her re-appearance made no reference to her
having been previously known as “ Nellie,” nor was ever
a word said about her intervening marrriage, as far as I
can remember. G. F. Watts heard of her re-appearance,
but he didn’t instruct his lawyers to reduce the annual
allowance to £2OO. His nominal condition of wedlock
never troubled him at all, seemingly. But one day Tom
Taylor, the dramatist, who had been best man at the
wedding, took the liberty of telling Watts that it
was time he got a divorce and spared himself
the foolishness of paying the lady £3OO a year. So
Watts applied for a divorce, and got it as a matter of
course, Ellen Terry being then 27. The suit was unde-
fended ; the co-respondent was the late Godwin, an archi-
tect with a great reputation as an archasologist. But the
respondent and the co. had ceased to play speaks for a
long time before Miss Terry got her liberty. A while
later she married Charles Kelly—and parted from him.
Dan Fitzgerald, or rather his manager, Erskine Scott
(for Dan himself is unhappily too sick to look for novelties
just now), nearly succeeded in securing a red-hot attrac-
tion for the show at the Fitz circus building. An offer
was made to William Ferrier, the heroic Warrnambool
fisherman who went forth in his little dingy for the rescue
of two men from the wrecked Bella. It was a tempting
offer, and Ferrier wired his acceptance, so the next thing
to consider was what to do with the hero when he joined
the circus. There’s a big tank below the arena at Fitz’s,
and Scott struck the idea of refilling the tank and using
it for a Rescue act—Ferrier to paddle to the far end in a
very small boat and save a couple of drowning circus
mariners, whilst tlie band play'ed “ A Life on the Ocean
Wave.” The details of the “turn” were in course of
incubation ; the glowing ad. was drafted ; the manage-
ment had shaken hands with itself—and then came
another telegram from Ferrier cancelling his promise. He
preferred to keep his heroism unspotted from the circus.

“ L.” : As to Gilbert’s “ Comedy and Tragedy ” having
been written for Mary Anderson" (“ S.T.S.”, B. 2/11/’O5),
that usually accurate stage historian, W. Davenport
Adams, after recording the first production of the play,
with Mary Anderson as Clarice, at London Lyceum in
1884, sa.vs : —“ The piece had, some years previously 7, been
accepted by Miss Litton for the Court Theatre, but was
not produced there.' The author told the story of the
drama in the form of a prose contribution to the Stage
Door (1880).” So The Bulletin writer who called
“Comedy and Tragedy” “one of Gilbert’s early efforts in
dramatic work ” may have been pretty near the mark, as
there is a possibility of its having been in manuscript for
years before Miss Litton’s acceptance of the play or
the publication of the Stage Door story.
Testimony showing that Nell Gwynne must have
frequently 7 carried a frilly, bundle to the Drury-lane
laundry comes from ’way back in the centuries. Some
French Court dames who came to London “ had heard of
the luxurious fineness of Miss Nelly’s underclothing, and
asked if they could judge of it for themselves. Without
more ado she let them raise each petticoat, one by one,
and before all in the room examine them on her.” French
ambassador Courtin, who was present at this interesting
exhibition, adds : “ I never in my life saw such thorough
cleanness, neatness, and sumptuosity.”
The virtues of linen mesh undergarments have
lately been forced upon Australian notice by Ball and
Welch, the Melbourne drapers and outfitters, not because
B. and W. are philanthropically concerned about the pub-
lic health, but because they had bought up a manu-
facturer’s stock. Medical advocates of cellular linen
underwear maintain, with sound argument, that it must
inevitably take the place of “ warm flannel next to the
skin ” when the world gives intelligent thought to the
question. A garment is thoroughly healthy for just as
long as it imparts warmth and admits the air freely, hence
the old prescription of a piece of neiv flannel for a sore
throat, weak chest, &c. The woollen material is
unimpeachable if it lets plenty of air through it, and pre-
sents a frank, open appearance when held up to
the light. But after each visit to the wash-tub it
returns in a more and more contracted form. The
original porousness is lost. A man who takes
violent exercise, or perspires without exercise, in a
matured woollen shirt finds that he grows cold. The
garment, almost as dense as a piece of felt, keeps him air-
tight more or less, and he acquires rheumatics through
the very precaution he has taken in always wearing
flannel next his figure. A few years ago, a famous
London physician, a lawgiver on the treatment of rheu-
matism, solemnly retracted his previous woollen doctrine,
and declared himself a high-priest of the new linen-mesh
religion. Testimony from bronchitis and rheumatism
sufferers was abundantly offered, and the big advt. -drum
was banged with the enthusiasm due to a good cause, but
the old faith in flannel is not easily killed. Even the cir-
cular issued with the aforesaid lot of linen improvements
is afraid to hit the woollen fetish too hard. It recom-
mends them as “ particularly well-suited for summer
wear, cool yet protective.” In good sooth —as the knights
of old would have said —in good sooth these garments of
unshrinkable linen-mesh are the best for all seasons, and
may be warranted to cure rheumatism in winter-time
when all pills and old-fashioned precautions have failed
miserably.

Joe Cook didn’t quite blush when, in the
midst of his furious denunciations of the closure
system, some one read to him his own speech of
a few months ago demanding it, but he wore the
confused look of a teetotaller who had just put a
whisky capsule in the collection plate.

A letter awaits “ Brunelys ” —whose
original marriage advt. was printed in this paper
11/10/’os—at The Bulletin office.
George Reid has been complaining bitterly
about the proposal by the Deakin Government
(kept in office by the aid of the Labor party) to
introduce the closure, and chewed quite a pound
of soap to make a froth over it. Therefore it is
interesting to remember that when George Reid
(then N.S.W. Premier, kept in office by the aid
of the Labor party) introduced his Foreign-trade
tariff he carried the whole blessed thing in one
sitting by the application of the closure. He
started with the closure, and kept it going all the
time. When Reid is more thaiiusually shocked
at the other fellow doing something, it is always
safe to assume that he habitually did it himself.
If he is desperately shocked, then he probably
invented the thing.
The Federal Government did not quite
adopt the suggestion in last week’s D.C.L. par—
to offer the opposition the right brand as a soother
for their unruly passions. They have held out the
olive branch of the closure instead. But there is
yet time for the frivolous stonewallers to mix
D.C.L. whisky with their mental masonry and
grow wise. “Ah ! What subtle virtue lies in the
Whusky of the Wise ” sang the greatest of Scotch
uoets.*

“No, darling, he thought you were my father."
Bendigo was able to go across the road the
other day and watch the decay of its own leper.
The leper had been in the Chinese camp for years,
and had been steward on the premises of one of
the Chinese societies, and had cooked its “loast
duck ” ; yet the other Chinese simply turned him
out and said ‘Whaffor?” as soon as a row was
made about the matter ; so the patient being thus
published, as it were, inspection was made easy.
Years and years ago they knew, so the Chinamen
said, that he had something that looked like
leprosy; but then he seemed to put on flesh again
—the loast duck that he cooked for the club
apparently knocked the leprosy out in that round.
Consequently they didn’t say anything, and the
leper remained a welcome member of the Chinese
camp till his fingers began to drop off, and then
they let him lie untended in a hovel. Apparently
the vanished hand didn’t paw over the vegetables,
or finger the ha’penny whistles that white mothers
buy for their youngsters to put in their mouths ;
but, all the same, for years it was in contact with
others that did the pawing. And the touch of a
vanished hand, when viewed in that light, is
unpleasant.
* * * *
“ One Who Has Of times Risked Contagion
Without Knowing It ” writes :
Bendigo Chinese Camp at Easter is a principal place of
call. The Chows stir up their Dragon on the Sunday
afternoon for the Fair on Monday, and the camp is over-
run with visitors —crowds of children and girls. The
camp itself, except for one or two weather-board struc-
tures, is a collection of hovels, composed of old tins
fastened together, and positively reeking with odor of
joss and cooked fowl. The Chows are mostly old men,
and are dying out fast. One time I found in a hut an
old man who had slept on sheets of tin so long that they
had taken his shape. As he lay at rest he looked like a
tinned Chow gone bad through exposure to the air.

“ Squidge ” on a military matter :
I went to the review in Albert Park (Melbourne) on
King’s Birthday. Thought I’d like to see how Australia
soldiered ; but I didn’t. The review was all gold lace and
“ Gor’ Save.” “ Gor’ Save ” was played on every possible
opportunity but one, and there must have been some mis-
take about that one. On thirteen separate occasions the
bands played “ Gor’ Save.” They played it when Fan-
shawe came. They played it when Talbottle came. They
played it when Strickland came. They played it when
Northcote came. They played it three times while they
fired a feu de joie. They advanced in review order,
saluted, and played it. They saluted the colors and
played it. They played it when Northcote with his jockey
bovs and flunkeys drove away. They played it when
Talbottle drove away. They played it when Fanshawe
drove away. They played it when Strickland drove away.
The time they didn’t play it was when Pursey Ohirnside,
in frock coat and top-hat, strolled over from the crowd of
civilians into the centre of the parade ground and was
handed something by Northcote to let him be called
C.M.G. for the future. Surely something better can be
found for grown men to do than to stand like gilded
popinjays and wave silly swords 13 times in an afternoon
to the accompaniment of “Gor’Save the King.” Are
there not rifle-ranges in Melbourne where the men may
learn to shoot ? Are there not miles of country owned
by people like Pursey Ohirnside and his ilk where
they can learn the meaning of cover and be taught to
behave like soldiers? Why then do 2000 men waste a
glorious day in “ Gor’ Save "?
• * • •
The incompetence of the average Shire
councillor is such that Vic. State Entomologist
French has reported, lately, that, unless St.
John’s wort and other abominations are to speedily
overrun the whole State, the administration of the
Noxious Weeds Act must be removed from the
hands of local bodies and placed in those of the
Government. St. John’s saintly weed was first
introduced to Australia by an old dame of Bright
(Vic.) for medicinal purposes. Like all imported
curses, it spread apace, and now it has got such a
hold of a lot of Victorian mountain country and
of stretches of South Gippsland that it would cost
more than the land is worth to clear it. One of
these precious shires—Upper Yarra— by the way,
has just discovered that it is in debt to a bank
£231 over the amount allowed by law, that its
arrears of irrecoverable rates amount to £4OO, and
that to pay its way honestly it would have to
strike a rate of 4s. in the £. Lots of English
towns and counties pay far more than this, but
Upper Yarra isn’t taking any 4s. rate; instead, it
is moaning piteously on the Treasury door-mat,
and beseeching Bent to soften his heart. Mean-
while, it is too agitated to look for St. John’s wort.

the imposing resi-

and a deputation in

road when the great
one was passing.
Also would I please
teach them “ Gor
Save” and something
extra in the way of
a bow and bob-curtsey.
Now, it was in the days of payment by results,
the inspector was about due, and the kids were
mostly of the cow-intellect species. I couldn’t
spare a day from the pursuit of results, so refused
with some shortness and disrespect. The thunder-
struck tenantry retreated in disorder, and from
that day till I left I was regarded as a Red
Anarchist, a Fenian, and a Dangerous Person of
No Manners.

ment intends to fittingly celebrate the jubilee ” of
that bit of gummy paper at the end of the year !
That’s about bedrock of the celebration foolery,
so far.

Sydney is apparently getting over its
spasm of virtue. Since the Birth Rate Commis-
sion sat the monthly birth average has been an
improvement on that 4 of former years; but for
October there was a sudden decrease and the
figures are on the same old level. The scarce and
necessary infant wants another advertisement
evidently.
* * * *
N.S.W. Bench and Bar were seldom so
destitute of wit and humor as at present. Want
is seriously ill, Wise is away, Julian Salomons is
practically out of the ring, and Pilcher, always
grave, has returned to his early pedagogic manner.
The younger men are all struggling for seats on
the Bench, District Court or Supreme Court, and
a belief has spread among them that heaviness to
the point of stolidity is a recommendation for the
post. The lightest Bar joke oi the last three
months weighed over a ton.

“ Albert D.” writes :
Re the Dentists’ Amendment Bill and its urgent need,
Around both Sydney and Melbourne various Hindus,
named Taj Mahal or Gosh Himalaya or the like, have
practised for years as certificated surgeons and stump
extractors. Having a tooth that troubled like the knob
of Hades, I consulted Gosh Himalaya in his cheap and
greasy cavern. Thrusting his black fist inside my mouth,
he gripped the aching molar with finger and thumb, and
waved my head to and fro briskly. “ Very sore,” he said,
gravely. “ You wait a leetal, en I pull heem out with
the forceepa.” I turned curiously, and saw about a dozen
rusted, blood-stained instruments that seemed to have
been hauling out bad teeth since the time of Job.
Vacating the dental chair hurriedly, I told him that I
didn’t like the gory, unwashed appearance of his
“forceepas.” Admitting that he might know some-
thing about sharks’ liver, frogs’ bones, and blowflies, I
felt squeamish anent his ability to grub an agonised jaw.
He became violently abusive. “I show you me dee-
plomis,” he shouted. “Me 18 deeplomis.” Then he flew
round the equator, and spoke learnedly about the Bull
Camel University of Timbuctoo, and of the unimpeach-
able integrity of Professor Sambo Chickasee, who signed
all diplomas. He shook a tooth certificate from the
Sultan of Borneo in my face, referred to himself as a
British subject, and called me a “ bletty pleece spy.” I
discovered afterwards that most of his victims left his
quackery in a pitiable state of blood and mutilation. In
the poorer quarters of the city the colored dental im-
postor waves his filthy instrument without fear of gaol or
exposure. His turbaned arrogance and his glass and
metal gauds attract the ignorant and inexperienced. A
sudden outburst of shrapnel in his direction would save
the community’s teeth a lot of pain and trouble.

The Broken Hill magistrate, in a betting
prosecution, has just decided that the passage of an
hotel isn’t a place within the meaning of the Act.
“The question was whether the defendant used
the passage sufficiently to make it a place.” The
next time this S.M. has before him a person who
has used a tomahawk to drive it into somebody’s
skull, the lawyers will no doubt argue that, as the
tomahawk was Only driven in once, it wasn’t used
often enough to constitute it an axe; also that,
as it only struck the man’s head once, the head
wasn’t struck often enough to make it legally a
cranium as provided by the Act. Whereupon the
S.M. will dismiss the case with some moral re-
flections.

Shops and factories legislation now before
F _ .. *=>
Victorian Parliament proposes that butchers
shops must on certain days close at 5 p.m., but
“ sausages may be sold up to 6 p.m.” This special
license to the sausage seems to The Bulletin to
carry reverence for the Unknown to the point of
superstition. We all know the special claims of
sausage to veneration; the profound mystery of
its origin, the antiquity of its component parts,
the fact that it is often the mausoleum of the dear,
dumb friend of man. Sausage, we admit, calls
for awe ; but is it to the public advantage that
that awe should be deepened by a deliberate legis-
lative enactment by which sausage, alone of
butchers’ goods, should hold the boards during
the twilight hour from sto 6 ? Besides, it gives
the show away. The plain meat will keep till
next day, but the sausage, already old and hover-
ing on the brink, can’t be trusted lest it commit
suicide in the night.

Bent’s roads, as described in the account
ol the Melbourne-Sydney motor contest: —
After leaving Seymour tbe roads are abominable —mere
bush tracks, full of holes, ruts, creeks, and glue pots, and
studded with trees and stumps.... M’Kenzie had a sensa-
tional experience. He was putting up very fast time, but
when about 20 miles from Euroa he got into a bad hole
and fractured his front axle The press car had a
most exciting experience, as the last 10 miles over a vile
bush track had to be done in the dark and without lights.
S. Dalryinple threaded bis way through holes and bush
with wonderful skill and nerve.... The official directions
state that after Tallarook, 55 miles from Melbourne, it is
nearly all bad road, and that bridges and culverts on it
must be treated with suspicion. One man said “No one
but millionaires or fools would race over this road.” A
good many who are not millionaires raced over it to-day,
but —it is not a credit to Victoria—no one was hurt....
There is every promise of fine weather but a bad road for
to-morrow’s stage to Albury.
The greatest and most important road in Aus-
tralia has become a mere bush rut, because the
greedy landlords who run the shire councils of
Victoria won’t pay enough rates to keep their
highways in repair. And until there is a law
under which shire councillors can be fined a mini-
mum of £lOO each for their neglect, there seems
to be no hope of improvement.

Ballarat’s > quaint old inhabitant, James
Oddie, the amusing gentleman who once donated
a number of pictures to the local School of Arts,
and lately, in a huff, threatened to take them
away to the lunatic asylum, broke out afresh over
the question of the Golden City’s jubilee, which
transpires next month. Oddie said he had given
the subject much thought, and had been delivered
of two ideas ; first, that a banquet should be given
to 5000 citizens, and, second, that the Local
Government Act should be amended so that the
Mayor should be converted into a Lord Mayor—
allee same Melbourne —and “there should be the
usual attendant train of aldermen, whose portly
figures gave tone to the municipal status of the
councils of Melbourne and Geelong.” The reason
of the existence of aldermen is now explained.
They are mostly portly and give tone and waist-
coat to the municipal status.
Concerning some things Australia does die
from, and some it doesn’t: —
Appendicitis has become a fashionable enough disease
to have a line to itself now in Victorian vital statis-
tics, though until Edward took it to his lower bosom, so
to speak, not one in a million ever thought of dying of it.
It’s a marvellous thing how changeful fashion is in the
matter of diseases—or doctors. It rarely occurs to any-
body now to die of quinsy or croup or thrush or chicken-
pox or erysipelas or rose rash or mumps or a dozen other
quaint, old-fashioned diseases; in fact, though the Vic.
Health Department keeps rose rash and mumps in the
returns from force of habit, only one doctor has been bold
enough to put either down on a certificate for over five
years. If it hadn’t been for new diseases, it looks as
though there would be a pretty good chance of immor-
tality, or thereabouts. Even as it is, with cancer killing
twice as many people (in proportion to population) as it
did 25 years” ago, and influenza five times as many, the
Victorian death-rate has come down from 25 - S2 per 1000
people in 1875 to 12’99 per 1000 in 1904. Which is pretty
good proof of the decay of the neglected old diseases.
Running back over 25 years of Victorian medical
history, these are the principal features : —(1) The gradual
reduction of the death-rate from phthisis. It is still the
most sweeping of all diseases ; but it sweeps off 135 per
100,000 now as against 239 per 100,000 in 1880. (2) The
stamping out of typhoid. In 1878 it killed 119 per 100,000
of the population! In the past four years the proportion
has never got beyond 14 per 100,000. Thank the
Health Department and Socialism for nearly all of
this. (3) The rarity of deaths from scarlet fever. In
the two years 1875 and 1876 that simple disease
killed 1298 people ; the average for many years past has
been under a dozen. Generally speaking, the 3’oungster
who is born to-day hasn’t more than half the chances
of promptly dying that he had 30 years ago. In 1874, IS6
and a fraction out of every 1000 infants that were born died
within a year ; in 1904, all but 92 in every 1000 lived. The
youngster doesn’t get:born quite as often as he did ; but
when he comes, he is more liable to stay. (4) The worst
feature of almost all Australian-death-and-life statistics
is headed “ Cancer.” Gradually year by year, and almost
without a break in its deadly progress, this disease gets
faster into the heart of Australia, and, at the present rate
of progression, in less than 15 years will have more Aus-
tralian victims than phthisis—the “great white plague”
that gets five times more public attention.

The Waverley (Sydney) sanitary inspector
lately rooted out a pretty example of the insani-
tary woman. He deposed in court that the de-
fendant’s yard was littered with heaps of rotting
matter, that her house and premises were in a dis-
graceful state, and that at the time of his visit
12 cats and three mangy dogs wandered about the
hall. When he added that hens were sitting on
eggs in the dining-room and kitchen, the court
felt that it would like a whiff of fresh air.
* * * *
The course of the law, so far as it has got,
in the case of Wadey v. Mort’s Dock Co. :
(1) Wadey sued the Co. for damages, and was non-
suited.
(2) The N.S.W. Full Court set aside the non-suit, and
ordered a new trial.
(3) The co. appealed to the Privy Council against a new
trial (P.C. appeal No. 1).
(4) The case came before the Full Court on demurrer.
There was also a replication and sundries. Plaintiff won,
but there doesn’t seem to have been any money in if.
The co. appealed to the Privy Council (P.C. appeal No. 2).
(5) New trial, lasting 30 days. Plaintiff got verdict
against the co. for 1111,130. Appeal to the Full Court.
(6) The Full Court set aside the verdict. Plaintiff
appealed to the Privy Council (P.C. appeal No. 3). Tuns
the co. had two appeals before the P.C.. and Wadey had
one.
(7) The Privy Council dismissed all three appeals.
And the end of all these things is Costs.
“ Squidge ” is an alarmist :
Waranga Basin (Vic.), though a splendid scheme in
theory, is going to turn out an enormous failure in prac-
tice. The Basin, when filled, will supply irrigation water
for all the ’pootras within 50 miles of Kyabram. But it’s
almost a certainty that the ’pootras some wet night will
be all washed out of their beds. The big earthwork that
dams the water up is some miles long ; but, owing to
a nice sense of economy in building it, it is about 10ft.
too narrow for absolute safety, and the first big storm
when the Basin is full will crumble the bank away and
inundate the country for miles and miles. The country
is infested with rabbits, and they will soon lower the
safety limit of the bank to vanishing point. Already one
serious break-away has occurred ; the bank of the princi-
pal inlet channel gave way in the night, and about two
chains of embankment were swept away. The water was,
however, turned off, and not much damage was done.
A lake several miles in area and 30ft. deep can’t be turned
off, however, and it will be Heaven help the country when
the Waranga water breaks loose.

“R.” : Ajyropos “O’Fipp” and names of
places off Little Bourke-street, Melbourne (8.,
26/10/’O5). In the fifties there were Romeo Lane
and Juliet Terrace, both off Bourke-street East,
north side. In the late seventies the terrace had
still its old name, but the lane was changed to
Crossley-street. In the early days both Romeo
and Juliet were of a moral habit that was simply
shocking considering their names, and their yells
at night were calculated to murder sleep, like
Macbeth.

THE VERSATILE DRUNK.
MRS. B IFFRM: “ Club, indeed ! Nothing I loathe
more than bare-faced lies.”
MR. B IFFRM: “ Kin ave ’em wi’ whiskers on if
y’r like, me dear (hic).”
Knox, M.P., in his capacity of “president
of the general council of the Chambers of Com-
merce of the Commonwealth,” had a most enjoy-
able evening last week. He gave a dinner in
honor of Australia’s revered G.-G. and most Noble
visitors—Lord Northcote and Lord Jersey. With
an Earl on either side of him Bill Knox was about
the proudest groveller that ever sat at meat. The
reports in the Melbourne dailies gave no idea of
Knox’s language in proposing the healths of his
exalted guests. He introduced the word “Noble-
man” at every possible opportunity, and he laid
such a reverential stress upon it that all the plain,
unvarnished Australians present seemed ignoble
creatures by comparison. And he was especially
grovelsome as regards Jersey—“the Honorable
Noble Lord.” Which suggests that Knox is look-
ing to the Noble Jersey to push his claim when
the next list of “ distinctions ” is made out.
* * * *
The much-cursed S’African swaddie, who
fought and burned and thieved for his bleeding
country, continues to have the long finger of scorn
pointed at him. A clerical big gun has just remarked
to a Maoriland synod that there had been a lot
of drinking among young men since the war. The
parable was taken up by others, and the inference
derivable was that the man in kharki Was fonder
of the pint-pot than he was of work. And it is
eminently true ; his thirst is chronic, and it takes
a veritable beer reservoir to accommodate him.
When not in gaol, many of these ’eroes spend the
days looking out for beer, and the nights fishing
for more beer.

the unmarried state for touring purposes, and was
not taken seriously in that capacity except by his
adoring barmaids, who don’t know any better.
It’s an easy matter to pass for a lovesick bachelor
at a bar, anyhow. Hebe, who ought to be the
most sceptical of creatures, is as gullible in one
respect as the boarding-school miss. She sees a
possible husband in every old bounder who talks
“ pretty ” to her, and is never convinced of his
marriage until his angry missis has dropped in one
evening and led him away by the ear.

- —a great man, in-

distributed through the ocean.
Four hundred writers, the cream of
Jewish talent in America, Britain, Ger-
many and elsewhere, have been employed on the
“ Cyclopedia.” There is in it all the crystal
lustre of intellect, with scarcely a trace of pre-
judice.

This is solemnly set forth in Hazell’s
Annual, an English work of general reference :
Ecclesiastical Law.
A moveable marble communion table is an illegal orna-
ment ; but a wooden table, to the three exposed sides of
which moveable slate and slabs covered with marble
mosaics are attached, is a legal ornament (Rector, etc., of
St. Luke’s, Chelsea, U. Wheeler).
Well, well! What a thing it is to be religious !

Justice Cohen, in the N.S.W. Divorce
Court, put to the door of “the cursed drink”
most of the cases coming before him. It is a fair
wager that the “ cursed drapery ” is about equal to
drink as a divorce producer. Husbands, cer-
tainly, often come into court as defendants
through their vinous wickedness. Wives, as
often, because one man’s salary wasn’t sufficient to
satisfy an extravagant and unnatural craving for
clothes.
THE LADY ACROBAT.
T OMMR : “Now, then, auntie, over you go !”
N.S.W. insanity figures for 1904 are not
encouraging. There is a steady increase in the
lunacy average, which now reaches one in 28G of
the population. Still recoveries are frequent,
showing that the asylums are well conducted. In
1904 they averaged 43 per cent. Among the
causes of insanity drink comes first, with 149
cases to its discredit; business worry and impe-
cuniosity sent 61 to put straws in their hair, while
religion was responsible for 54 cases.
TO MARGOT.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I dearly loved a lady fair,
Whose name was Margot;
A steamer bore her far from me,
A precious cargo.
I met another charmer sweet,
When I lost Margot;
She scooted with a motor man,
I saw their car go.
The next one was a taller girl
Than darling Margot ;
She’s married now to one called Smith,
Who lives at Bargo.
And many more have caught my eye,
But none like Margot ;
Some age has placed upon the shelf—
A srreat embargo.
And none of them with her compare.
I shall afar go
To seek my long lost love again,
My own dear Margot.
We’ll love each other very much,
Myself and Margot,
And faithful prove till our two souls
Shall to a star go.
N.S.W.
J. G. M'EWES.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Treasurer Peake, of S. Australia, introduced a
Bill the other day to provide for the renewal of the
State’s most imminent P.N. and its most immediate
IO U. Some members were quite surprised to discover
that before October, 1910, £6,034,700 of troubles in the
shape of bonds and another £1,737,500 in the shape of
Treasury bills will be coming home to roost ; they
probably haven’t the ghost of an idea that in 1916 a vast
trouble of £9,486,045 will throw itself on the doorstep in
one heap and demand to be wrestled with. When, a while
ago, S. Australia had. to argue with a small enemy in the
shape of £850,000 of 3£ per cent, bills, it only got out of
the trouble by renewing them at 4 per cent., which
meant another £4250 of annual interest; and Premier
Price apparently remembered the little incident when he
deplored that several States would have to go on the
market at about the same time. But that is going to
happen as almost a regular thing now that the curse has
started to come home —it isn’t a mere coincidence of the
next two or three years. Take the list, compiled from
Coghlan, of the debts of four States falling due for some
years ah^ad: —
N.S.W. Vic. Queensland. S. Aus.
1907 ..£5,276,128 £5,050,000 £1,333,075
1908 .. 3,090,800 2,025,000 2,208,150
1909 .. 384,000 25,000 3,384,025
1910 .. 3,418,879 25,000 - 60,300
1911 .. 2,132,000 68,300
1912 .. 9,366,227 88,000 £530,000 85,000
1913 ... 4,746,759 2,066,500 46,300

But the States, being now able to borrow locally, are
doing it mostly on short-dated Treasury bills; so that
the figures for the last six or eight years in the foregoing
table will certainly be worse by the time they have to be
argued with. There are, even now, only two years in the
next 12 in which at least two States won’t be interested
in the argument; and in one of those years (1914) Tas-
mania (whose sins are not shown in the foregoing because
one column can’t hold the sins of a whole Oommonwealth)
will also be struggling with one of its P.N.’s ; and when
the period covered by the table is completed, Westralia
will also start to come into the controversy. And Aus-
tralia isn’t facing the situation with a sinking fund, out
of which.it could do something, supposing the argument
was gping against it ; while about all the advice it is
getting from its financiers is that no self-respecting State
that wants to go on being drunk all its life should really
drink more ttian £1,000,000 in any one year.
The Japanese “Financial and Economic
Annual, 1905,” issued by the Department of Finance,
shows the amazing j umps the Monkey has been making.
In 10 years the revenue has gone from £9,817,003 to
£29,350,094, the banking deposits from £19,113,541 to
£80,145,446. But the rice crop has only increased from
199 to 257 million bushels, and the barley and wheat crop
from 97 to 98 million bushels. So, though Japan has
made marvellous progress, it hasn’t been by “ developing
its great natural resources ’’—which the Australian Tory
always understands to mean growing wheat and wool.
On the other hand, the Jap’s coal output has increased
from 3,346,158 tons to 10,138,707 tons, an increase of 203
per cent. ; and that explains the situation. Japan, with
the help of a tariff running up to 200 per cent., has estab-
lished factories and industries ; and, though its agricul-
tural production isn’t as large per head of population as
it was 10 years ago, yet its revenue has increased just
upon 200 per cent., and its banking deposits by 319 per
cent.
* * * *
The Australian Woollen Co., one of the woollen
manufacturers recently referred to as being in difficulties
through the lowering of the tariff, has decided that it is
useless to carry on the struggle any longer ; and at a
meeting of shareholders in Melbourne last Friday it
was decided to hold a special meeting to deal with the
assets. Tenders meantime are to be invited for the
works. The co., whose mill is at Footscray, lost £l6OO
last year, and, like almost every other woollen mill in
Australia, has drifted into debt since the tariff was
reduced; its burden at present is about £BOOO.
The Freetrade crowd of course says that the trouble
is the result chiefly of “interference with labor
by the Wages Board.” To a great extent, too,
that is true. Even without any tariff at all Australia
could probably make a success of its manufactures if it
were willing to be a cheap labor country, and were pre-
pared to sacrifice its eight hours system, and its objections
to child labor. What it can’t have with the present tariff
are manufactures and fair Wages, hours, and conditions
of labor. And the Labor party goes on sinking the fiscal
question, as, though it weren’t concerned about such
things as wages, hours, and conditions of labor.
* * * *
The growth of life-assurance funds supplies one
reason why Australia should be even more ashamed of
itself for getting into debt to the foreigner now than 10
years ago. At the end of 1894 the accumulated funds of
Australian offices amounted to £19,240,944. The October
number of the N.S.W. Statistical Bulletin shows that
total assets at the end of 1904 were £34,241,309. There
has thus been in the last 10 years an accumulation of
£15,000,36.5, practically the whole of which has been
available for investment in those securities which Bull
Oohen likes best—mortgages and public stocks. There-
fore there are 15,000,365 more reasons now for doing
without Bull Cohen than there were 10 years ago, and
there are 15,000,365 more reasons for scoffing at the’ man
who implores Australia not to do anything sensible lest it
should drive foreign capital out of the country. On top
of the assurance-office accumulation there has been
another accumulation of over £5,000,000 in the savings
banks of N.S. Wales alone ; and that extra money has
also been available for the same sorts of investment. If
foreign -capital isn’t-being driven out of the country, it is
quite time somebody started to drive it with enthusiasm
or some other weapon.
* * * *
The accounts of N.S.W. Government (Post-
office) Savings Bank for the past year show a loss of
£20,817, which reduced the reserves to £37,838. Total
cost of management, with incidentals, was £26,998, of
which £9975 Was paid in commission to the Australian
Government; and the interest paid or credited to deposi-
tors was £251,474, or £3 ss. 2d. per cent, on the average
amount of deposits. The year opened with deposits of
£7,475,058 and closed with £7,952,885. Except £133,412
held in cash, the whole of the funds are represented by
N.S.W. public stocks or Treasury receipts. This
aciounts for the small earnings, and explains the
deficit. Since all its deposits carry interest the
Government Savings Bank pays, on an average
much higher interest than any other bank; while’
all the assets are in a form of security which’, if the
safest, also gives about the smallest return of a’ll those
dealt in by ordinary banks. Why the Savings Banks
shouldn’t take some of the more profitable business—such
as advances to farmers and others on freehold security
is incomprehensible. As usual, the State takes the work
that hasn’t any fat on it; and then private enterprise
carefully points at. the result and says the State doesn’t
know how to run things.
* *. * •
So far as getting cash for its goods is concerned,
Australia has. hardly a worse customer than Eno-land’
Victorian Statist McLean has just issued some'’trade
figures which show that in the past couple of years
though that State sent the United Kingdom £ll 233 211
worth of goods, it received £13,244,186 worth of mostly-
manufactured articles in return. In other words
Victoria didn’t receive any money at all from its export
trade to the United Kingdom, bnt had to hand over the
whole of that trade and £2,010,975 besides in order to pay
for British goods that mostly should have been made in
Australia. India and Ceylon are better customers—thev
paid Victoria £6,397,665 and Victoria paid them
£1,286,459, leaving a balance of £5,111,206 for the ex-
tension of Australian industry or for the repayment
of Australia’s foreign debt. S’Africa is a customer of
the same sort. Other European countries show up far
better than Britain does. Victorian trade with Belgium
in the two years left Vic. with a balance of £694,073 on the
right side ; with Germany, £323,121 ; while France paid
the State in hard cash no less than £2,046,372. Victoria
relieved itself of a little of its foreign public debt and a
great deal of its foreign private debt last year ; but it
wouldn’t do it if all the trade were on the British basis.
It was simply because it had some foreign customers who
didn’t expect us to buy 21s. of goods for every 20s. they
bought from us that the State was able to push off a
pound or two of its crushing burdens. Yet there are still
people who wonder what ever Australia would do but for
its commercial relations with a dear benevolent old
Mother Country.
* * * *
Melbourne Tramway Co. is not going the right
way about securing the extension of its lease, which
expires in 11 years. Instead of being conciliatory it is
arrogant, and has lately been making demands, not
only for a monopoly of the right to run trams in
the streets at present used, but also for the sole
right over the whole of the municipalities served by
its system. When Premier Bent proposed to connect
St. Kilda railway station with Brighton by means of a
State electric tram, the company fought all it knew, and
brought to bear all manner of influences to prevent a
quarter of a mile of State line passing through the
municipality of St. Kilda at all. It was not pro-
posed to run the line down the streets in which the
company already ran its cars, but to connect through a
different set of thoroughfares altogether, yet the company
bluffed at first and whined afterwards about the injustice
of State interference. That the co. was never intended
to have a monopoly of the suburbs is indisputably proved
by a number of legal points in the original Act, the most
striking being a provision that it must afford facilities
for the lines of any other tramway to cross its own.
This matter having been decided, the co. went
back growling to its kennel, but has now come
forward with an even more cheeky proposal to prevent
Bent’s State motor-cars from plying for hire in any
street in which tramways exist, the claim amounting
to one of ownership of the thoroughfare from wall to wall.
The logical conclusion of this contention would be the issue
of tickets at 2s. a dozen permitting pedestrians to walk
along the footpaths, the prosecution of any person whose
vehicle dares to set wheel on holy ground, and the collec-
tion of blackmail from bicycles, private vehicles, and
delivery vans. And the monstrous demands of the mono-
poly were defeated in the Leg. Council by only 14 to 12
votes. “If the Tramway Co. has its way when we have
fll'ing machines,” said old Martin Oussen, one of the few
moderately democratic men in the House, “ it will not
permit us to wing our way down Bourke-street.”
Compare the profits of three of the leading soft-
goods-importing firms (as given in Melbourne Trustees’
and Investors’ Review) with the results of the efforts of
three Australian softgoods manufacturing concerns :
Importing Companies.
1903. 1904. 1905.
Profits. Profits. Profits.
