R. Reid & Co 26,376 27,893 41,038
Patterson, L., & Bruce .. 23,833 48,502 46,500
Total profit .. .. £90,358 £138,937 £156,955
Manufacturing Companies.
1903. 1904. 1905.
Loss. Loss. Loss.
Queensland Woollen C 0... £1386 £2BBO £2795
Profit. Profit. Profit.
Castlemaine „ „ .. 2214 2155 1320
Ballarat „ „ 4484 4599 3162
Total profit .. '.. £5312 £3874 £1687
The three concerns that make their profits almost wholly
by importing the goods that have been made by foreign
workmen with the help of foreign capital in foreign
factories, have in three years netted profits totalling
£386,250. Speaking generally, all the Australian labor
that these co.’s have employed was a few badly-paid
men dragging the goods out of the ships and a few badly-
paid people—clerks and others—trying to sell the goods
to Australian people. And in the same three years
the three Australian manufacturing co.’s, employing
Australian labor and Australian capital and Australian
material, have made £10,873 altogether; and then
nothing is written off in most cases for wear and tear of
plant. While the profits of the importing concerns have
moved from £90,358 in 1903 to £156,955 in 1905, those of
the Australian concerns have fallen from a poor £5312 in
1903 to an utterly miserable £1687 in 1905. The two lots
of figures, taken together, supply evidence that ought to
be strong enough'.to convince anybody that the present
tariff is insufficient to keep Australian trade for Aus-
tralian workmen. Year by year the foreigner has
grabbed more and more of it. At first only the
capitalist suffered ; his profits came down and his
dividends disappeared. But now that profit has
vanished, the workman suffers—the factories are
either discharging hands or are closing up altogether.
Also the assets are in most cases mortgaged ; and the
bank or mortgage co. is finding itself either left, or very
liable to be left, with a quantity of machinery which it
won’t pay to work under the present tariff, and which it
won’t pay to shift. But the workman who has lost his
job, and the landlord whose house he lives in, and who
can’t get rent for the house, and the bank with the doubt-
ful asset, can all have the satisfaction of knowing that
the mostly - foreign importing concerns are doing
splendidly.
* * * *
The philanthropists of the Stock Exchanges
have beaten the White Knight of Melbourne Board of
Works, and latter, at the eleventh hour, had to offer
i per cent, commission on the £500,000 loan conversion.
Even then the conversion was only a very moderate
success, and the time for receiving applications had to be
extended. The Exchange, anxious to prove its influence
is vigorously whipping in the interests of the scheme’
but, after all, a third of the debentures (4A per cent.) will
probably have to be provided for by a hew loan. Con-
sidering the price, and the supposed plenty of money
just now, and the cost, and the very partial success of the
conversion notwithstanding the cost, the whole trans-
action is enough to give at least two Australian State
Treasurers the creeps when they think of the debts they
have falling due.

crushed out of existence. The small amount shown in

least, an extra 1 per cent, has been added to the bonus);
and, as it still leaves 51 per cent, of the net profits to go
to reserves, no fault can be found with the increase,
especially when it is made in this tentative way.

This co. owns the East Greta and also the Stanford-
Merthyr colliery, where the explosion occurred the other
day : the accounts don’t, of course, show the consequences
of that disaster. From all that can be learned, though,
the fire has been controlled ; the biggest hole in the co.’s
profits will probably be through loss, not only of current
trade, but of customers. There is keen competition in
the coal trade these times, and one co.’s disaster makes
another co.’s chance. The East Greta, though it does
something more than the average coal co. in writing
down its.assets, including freehold, nevertheless pays
dividends to almost the last available shilling. It has
recently increased its capital ; the last balance-sheet
shows that £13,739 was raised during the half-year. But
mortgages and bills on Stanford - Merthyr purchase
account have only been reduced by £4600, though there
was enough cash in hand to do a good deal more in the
same way if the co. felt inclined. Surplus of liquid assets
over liabilities is now £5889 ; six months ago, before this
£13,739 was got in, the deficit was £2582. "The mortgage
has been reduced to £BOOO ; practically the whole of the
other liabilities are now “ sundry creditors ” —the balance
on the Stanford-Merthyr account is only £702. Of the
new issue of 25,000 shares (making 150,000) 21,937 were
allotted ; and, £13,739 having been paid on these, there is
£Bl9B still to be received on account of this issue.

has had to rely upon overdraft for funds, and the business
isn’t profitable enough to stand that. Yet there seems
a difficulty about getting in other capital. The big
investor doesn’t favor the building society, and the small
investor, who wants building-society help, pretty often
nowadays turns to the Starr-Bowkett societies, which
offer him something in the way of a gamble as well as an
investment. Yet, in favor of this City of Brisbane
Society, it can be said that apparently it has no proper-
ties whatever in possession or payments in arrears, and,
though for seven years’work the figures are small, still
this other record is highly creditable—it counts, in fact,
for a good deal more than big figures.

The aggregate amount of the 1905 dividend is an estimate ;
the exact sum will not be known till the next balance-
sheet is issued. But it is possible to get pretty close to
the real thing; for 1904 the total was estimated at
£2300, and it worked out at £9 more. The further
increase of dividend looks an unwarranted pro-
ceeding in the face of the figures. profits
are certainly sufficient to pay the dividend ; in the last 12
months they amounted to £3475. But, then, £1350 was
written off securities. If these are old losses, which
should have been written off in the past, then the in-
crease of dividend can be understood ; but if they are
merely current losses or depreciation, then it isn’t under-
standable. It wasn’t in that way that this society
escaped reconstruction when other societies were tum-
bling to pieces all around it. The deposits total £85,240,
as against £86,400 a year ago ; shareholders’ funds total
£61,713 against £63,735. Twelve months ago the society
had enough cash in hand to pay the dividend and have
£386 over ; this year it has almost everything—deposits,,
capital and reserves—out in advances, &c., and has not
enough cash or bank balances by £824 to pay even the
dividend with. What would happen if the society had to
face a sudden call from depositors can’t be too pleasant
to think about. It is certainly cutting things very fine..

The Westralian Producers’ Co-operative
Union, Ltd., whose work is bigger than its figures::—

The union, which now has something like 1000 members,
was formed for the purpose of enabling Westralian farmers
and fruitgrowers to co-operatively do without a host of
middlemen. Agriculture and fruit-growing in the West
hardly ranked as industries. Things just happened, and
were allowed to happen. There were no grain sheds
along the railways, and, of course, none at Fremantle,
there being' no export trade. In the fruit business it was
the same ; there was an utter lack of organisation. So
far this union has spent most of its energies (and commis-
sion) repairing these defects. It claims to have made
a vast difference in the prices at which the farmer can
get his fertilisers, wheat-sacks, and other requirements ;
and now it is helping him find a market. In the accounts
issued the union claims reserves of £325, but that is
arrived at by reckoning in as an asset £268 which has
been spent apparently in extending- business, and isn’t an
asset which an auditor can either touch, smell, or taste.
In the table it has therefore been disregarded.

Westralian gold yield for October was 160,6220 z.
The total for year so far is 1,629,9730 z, against 1,654,2250 z.
for same period of 1904. This means a shortage of
24,2520 z., which is more likely to be increased than
decreased before December 31st.

Ex-manager Harper has resigned his director-
ship of Golden Pole, Davyhurst (W.A.). Percy Morgans,
late manager of Westralia Mt. Morgans, has been asked
to examine the property ; and, whether his report is
favorable or not, it is certain the bottom level is dis-
appointing. The Is. 6d. a share dividend foreshadowed
for December can hardly be forthcoming, the last three
months’ profits being £3700, £3BOO and £4700, a total of
£12,200. After paying off debts this would leave about
£6OOO. November’s profits may amount to £5OOO, and
Is. div. requires £lO,OOO. This much may be paid, if only
for appearance sake.
» * * *
Karridale, Burtville (W.A.), continues to blaze.
Latest return is 1120 oz. from 156 tons. Up to date mine
has produced 33100 z. from 365 tons. Burtville is the best
“ poor man’s field ”in W.A., and the average returns for
years range about 2Joz. For the most part the reefs are
too small, though, for highly capitalised co.’s.
* * * *
Mt. Morven copper mine (W. A.) is the property
that was owned by Mt. Margaret RewardG.M., and aban-
doned in 1901. It is now held by W. B. M’Cormack and
party, who recently sent six tons to the Waratah
smelting works for 34971 b. copper and 9oz. 12dwt. gold,
valued at £137. This is said to be a fair average from a
big reef; so owners should be in for a good time. Ore
worth £23 a ton should pay well, even at such a long
distance from the coast.
* » * *
East Eingall, Day Dawn (W.A.), recently
offered its unissued shares at ss. when the market price
for those issued was 6s. 6d. Since then the latter have
naturally fallen to ss. too. Some gaudy reports have
been issued about the mine lately, and much has been
done to boom the show. In a winze below No. 2 level,
reef was said to be sft. wide and worth 22dwt. In the
shaft at 413 ft. reef was reported to be 3ft. wide and worth
61dwt. A level is now being opened at 500 ft., and some-
thing may be heard of the concern shortly.
» » * *
Great Eingall Associated, Day Dawn, which
was said to have been floated early in 1904 in Perth, has
reverted to original owners. Out of a capital of 250,000
shares owners were to get 125,000, together with £5OO
cash. Although nearly two years have elapsed since the
alleged flotation, the £5OO cash has never been paid. An
attempt Was recently made to unload the show on to
J. Bull, but that failed too. Yet some Perth papers raged
at random when, at the outset, The Bulletin held the
Fingall Associated up as a wild cat to be avoided, and
those Perth papers predicted that a great future was
assured for it.
* * * *
Murchison Associated, Day Dawn, has for years
been before the public, and, after ten years’ working, is
still in a bad way. In May last it was stated that the
shaft would be sunk at the rate of 80ft. monthly, but that
prophecy has not been fulfilled. A 10-head mill was
erected and crushings started, but results were so poor
that the mill was shut down. Mine is a wild cat, not-
withstanding that scrip is quoted at about 6s.
* * * *
Great Fingall, Day Dawn, reckons that it has
got fairly into the shoot at north end of property at
bottom levels. It must, however, be remembered that
the ore shoot is not more than 1300 ft. long, and that both
north and south it branches off, and no mine on those
branches has got anything of value. Divs. for a long time
ahead are assured ; but the silver lining has the before-
mentioned cloud at the back of it.
* * * *
North End G.M., Kalgoorlie, has been boomed
too much. A 15-head mill has been erected, but there is
no stone developed. Some interested people are trying
to work a coup, and speculators and investors should
know what to do in those circumstances.
* * * *
Value of Lake View Consols shares will take
some finding out soon. The co. has just paid £70,000 for
a half-interest in a Broken Hill mine, whose profits (it has
never made any so far) will depend on. the silver, lead,
and zinc markets. Besides that, it has interests in
Russia and S. America. The man who ascertains the
value of all these, and therefore what the shares should
be really worth, will have to put in 29 hours a day at the
job.
* ■* * *
Owners of the Fenian mine, in North Murchi-
son district (W. A.), have been offered £20,000 cash and
10,000 shares for their 12-acres lease, but have decided to
work it themselves. Six of them have drawn £3OOO
apiece already, and reckon they will get as much more
next year. Remembering the tricky nature of Murchison
reefs, the owners of the .Fenian are taking big risks.
Nine years ago £30,000 was refused for the Welcome,
Murchison, and for a time the owners thought they had
done the right thing. A little pipe of almost pure gold
ran almost vertically through pure white kaolin, and
over a thousand ounces were taken out in a week. After
that it was found the kaolin continued to go down, but
the gold didn’t. The mine turned out an arrant duffer—
just like the Mainland Golconda, New Chum, Fortune of
War, Star of the East, and 50 others on the Murchison.
* * * *
Taking a line from what neighboring mines are
doing, Broken Hill Block 14 ought to be able to make
a profit of 10s. a ton from its sulphides, apart from
anything it may get for its tailings, with lead at £13105.;
and on an output of 2000 tons a week, a difference of £1
for lead either way should mean about £5OOO per half-
year. With the mill in full work profit should, therefore,
be now £35,000 per half-year, on the capital of £155,000
(£125,000 in ordinary shares and £30,000 in pref. shares).
■» * * *
The market is working in the dark as to the
British Broken Hill’s profits ; and no Broken Hill mine,
except the Junction, has given more erratic results.
Present output is slightly over 3000 tons of sulphides a
week; and the stuff averages 15 - 8 per cent, lead and
lOToz. silver, as against the South’s 16'9 per cent, lead
and 7-3 oz. silver for last half-year. From that ore the
South Co. made 11s. Bd. per ton profit, and that was with
lead at £l3 2s. 6d. per ton. An extra 20s. a ton should
add about 2s. 4d. to the value of British ore. No Barrier
mine has had better developments underground during the
12 months than the British ; and it has not only a big
area, but a bigger unworked area of known ore-
bearing country than any other on the line. The
ore bodies are not as regular as in the South, but, for all
that, it is a mine of very great possibilities ; arid if it had
been handled as well as the South, it would not have had
its disastrous slumps. But, while Block 10 is in 100,000'
shares and the South in 200,000 shares, the British has to
carry 300,000. On account of the irregularity of its bodies
and the comparatively small quantity of ore actually
opened out, it is still a good deal of a gamble ; but it is
also a gamble in which there are chances of a big prize,

• * # *
A half-share in the C.S. A. (Oobar) has been sold
for £l5OO, the biggest price actually paid to date ; on that
basis the show is valued at £132,000. Meaning of C.S. A.
is something of a mystery. In a case up at Cobar, when
the O.S.A. was mentioned, and the Judge' wanted to
know what it meant, Crick answered promptly, “Can’t
Satisfy Anybody.” That was after the mine had been
unsuccessfully hawked around for years. But the truth
seems to be that, when the outcrop was found and first
worked years ago, the prospectors who held it were a
Cornishman, a Scotchman, and an Australian —hence
O.S.A. Men who have given it a try in the
past were, in particular, Cornish, Priest, and Andrew;
Oobar thought so little of it that when, a few years ago,
Cornish and Priest tried to get some money in to help
them continue prospecting, the only share-application
received was from Andrew. In 1894 a Broken Hill syndi-
cate had it, but turned it down, though Priest (who
represented the syndicate) begged for more sinking.
Later on some of the Great Oobar people had a look in,
and, with heaps more money to burn than any of the
previous holders, they were able to do what none of the
others had managed in 30 years.
* * * *
Crowl Creek, Shuttleton (N.S.W.), has been
crosscutting for the lode at the 530 ft. level since July,
and apparently couldn’t see it when it had found it.
Something that manager Armstrong couldn’t persuade
himself was the lode was passed through at 130 ft. from
the shaft, but, as nothing else was found, he came back
and started to open out at this point. Things
improve as they rise and drive south ; in the rise, now
about 30ft. up, there is 2ft. of ore, and in the drive a fair
face. Lodes may always be expected to “ pinch and
make,no doubt; but for all that, so far as it has been
proved, this experience at 530 ft.—the deepest level—isn’t
too cheerful.
* * * *
The Barrier (N.S.W.) has now started to pro-
duce wolfram. The claims are near the Tarrawingee rail-
way, under 30 miles from Broken Hill ; the seams are
narrow, rarely running to more than 6in., but the mineral
is pure, and worth, undressed, about £4B or £5O per ton.
* * * *
Magnetic concentration has been a success in
dealing with zinc ore containing iron pyrites and ehalco-
p.vrite, and large quantities of zinc ore are now concen-
trated in America by both the magnetic and electrostatic
methods. The Americans seem to get higher grade con-
centrates than have hitherto been obtained at Broken
Hill. A considerable quantity of American zinc ore
is exported to Europe; but there is a vast differ-
ence in sending a 60 per cent, concentrate
across the Atlantic and sending 40 per cent, ore
half round the globe. The lower the grade of zinc ore
the higher is the proportion of loss in distillation, and the
consumption of fuel on the poor ore is just as great as on
the higher grade. Cost of fuel and labor is the main con-
sideration in zinc smelting. Judging from the high prices
paid in America for ore, smelting losses and expenses
must have been reduced of late. Magnetic concentration
and improved recovery would, in the ordinary course,
mean increased production and lower prices for spelter,
and that may hit the Zinc Corporation pretty hard.
* * * V
N.S.W. Mines Department manages to get
itself suspected, whatever its real character may be. Only
the other day there was the open allegation Of a leakage
of information regarding a discovery at Orange. Now
Oobar and other people want to know what has been done
with Chief Mining Inspector Jaquet’s report on the
O.S.A. He visited the mine on Sept. 25, and the public
doesn’t know the result yet. Did Jaquet report, or did
he just go out there for fun ? If he has reported, who has
the valuable information that Jaquet no doubt collected ?
The public certainly hasn’t got it. If he went out for fun,
and didn’t report, then who paid the bill ?
* * » *
Gold, £509,000; dividends, £73,000. What a
record of patience and toil! Figures, as may be sur-
mised, represent the outcome of operations at a Victorian
deep-lead mine—the Madame Berry West. Claim is a
step away from the Madame Berry, but inferior in every
respect to that famous property. Isaac Wheeldon made
the mine, for in the early days it went through many
vicissitudes and frightened out all save the stickers. Now
a crowd of old-timers, with Joe English and Maughan at
their head, rule it. A big field of wash seems to have caused
a wide distribution of gold; so there are none of
the marvellously rich runs that gave the Madame
Berry riches. 00., a while back, bluffed the West Berry
Oonsols into giving it a slice of ground at the top end of
its claim for a slab of the West Loughlin lease. It is
pretty certain that the gold of the future will have to
come from the block so got, and not from the wash
followed into the upper part of the West Loughlin
ground. 00. pays three or four 6d. divs. each half-year.
Its halance-sheet shows how the money has gone. All
the calls made only totalled £9OOO. Gold won gave
£509,000. Divs. took £73,000, leaving £446,000, apart
from calls, to be accounted for. Wages took £300,000 ;
while timber, firewood, fuel, and ironmongery and
chandlery ate up nearly all the rest.
* * * *
Creswick (Vic.) mines are nearly as cheaply
managed as those of Bendigo. The Madame Berry
West, in A. J. Peacock’s office, has grubbed out so
far £509,000 worth of gold, and has had to sink three
shafts to do it. From first to last the board fees have
only amounted to £1052, or less than some Bull-managed
Westrahan co.’s pay their directors in four months. And
all the Berry group mines call for managing ; so far as the
directors are concerned, running a Westralian mine is
beer and skittles by comparison. The Berry West has in
one way and another spent up to now, apart from divi-
dends, £450,000, and all the office expenses of the mine,
including rates, rents, and compensation, have amounted
to only £lO,OBl.
* » * *
M‘Go wan’s Clarence United, Bendigo (Vic.),
having failed to live up to its 995. reputation, is now
trying its best to buttress a 30s. market. So far it has
put nothing very solid into that support. Where it looks
tor something to keep the pot boiling is in the formation
it hopes to tap at 2380 ft. Its winze from 2280 ft. carries
about a foot of quartz which enthusiastic Bendigonians
say consists of splendid mineralised stone. But there is
no gold, and, until the precious metal shows up, mineral-
ised stone won’t suffice to keep shares moving. All the
same, scrip is held very closely, and, therefore, fools alone
would take liberties with the stock. A speck of gold
would mean a 10s. rise.

* * * *
Willcocks’ efforts to get the office of the O.K.
shifted south from Cairns (N.Q.) will be helped on by re-
cent ructions. Alleged that the first that one of the
directors knew about the strike of 18 p.c. ore at the
deeper level was when he got an offer of 14s. for 5000
shares—a jump of about Is. 6d. (the jump went on to
17s. 6d.). Adelaide speculators knew about the develop-
ment long before it was officially reported, and Sydney
got buying orders while the chairman was still stating
(no doubt quite truly) that he knew nothing. Now the
question is : Did any of the other directors or executive
officers know ? Melb. Exchange is inclined to join Bris-
bane in viewing the matter seriously ; but Sydney hasn’t
shown much interest.
* * * *
Adelaide feels better since it got the “ pull ” on
Brisbane over the last O.K. boomlet. Brisbane left it
with Mt. Molloys to nurse at a high price ; now the "holy
southern city calls things square.
* * * *
The next flutter in Queensland mining will be
in Gulf-country shows, following the passing of the
Burketown-Lib'dale Syndicate Railway Bill. This Bill
was first put through in 1900, but the money was not
forthcoming; present Bill extends the time, and the
money is now said to be waiting. Townsville and Charters
Towers already have prospectors out, and a silver-lead
show, prospected by E. O. Davies, was easily put off into
a co. in N.Q. last week. Gaudy reports on prospects for
silver-lead, tin, copper, monazite, and other metals have
been made. The point that will want most watching in
every case js as, to costs. Even with the Lilydale line
built, anything like cheap working seems impossible. The
new Bill provides that the line shall be built by the end
of 1907 ; if it isn’t, the £5OOO deposit will help Kidston
through his Treasury troubles. The route will be from
the mouth of the Albert, on the Gulf, via Burketown to
the Lilydale, 120 miles altogether.
* * * *
Dalzell’s, the recent sensational Herberton dis-
trict (N.Q.) tin float, has left a good many over-sanguine
people lamenting. It was put off in Bs. shares ; and such
was the mana of John Moffatt and the other men who had
it in hand that shares went to £1 before scrip was issued.
Allotment of shares was represented as a good deal in the
way of a personal favor ; and when some cautious people
wanted to take their profit on some of the shares they
hadn’t yet got, they were covered with reproaches. Result
was shares were kept off the market, and the tighter they
were kept back the harder some people struggled to get
them, so the price sprang to 245. As The Bulletin
pointed out at the time, if the promoters, who knew the
mine better than anybody else, had reckoned the show
was worth 245. a share, they wouldn’t have sold it for Bs.
If the sinking from the tunnel had proved quite satis-
factory, the high quotation might have been justified ;
but that was the risk—a large-sized risk in North Queens-
land —and the sinking didn't prove satisfactory. The lode
wasn’t proportionate to the- size of the surface show.
There isn’t, therefore, any difficulty now about getting-
shares (issued as paid to 10s.) at 13s. 6d. So far as can be
judged from recent developments, the mine will turn out
all right—for the people, anyway, who got their shares
for Bs. For the others—well, they set their (want of)
judgment against John Moffatt’s; and in tin-mining
matters that isn’t advisable.

* * * *
Lyell Blocks (Tas.) at ss. 9d. gives to this
300,000 share show a market value of over £BO,OOO, and
anyone may ask why. Plant eats up about 5000 tons of
copper-bearing clays a week, and saves a shade over 1 per
cent, of copper. To pay 6d. a quarter means £7500, and
profits on the copper saved mount up so slowly that not
more than that div. can well be looked for. The mine
isn’t plain sailing, by any means. Country is soapy
schist, and the water gets in from the surface.
During the past month co. had trouble with one stope,
and the risk chokes off as many as does the poorness of
the ore. Tolhurst and Tom Harvey were the main props
of the mine for many a day, and, as London decided to
buy back its holding, they have netted a div. out of the
market without having to wait for problematical distri-
butions from the mine. Lucky pair—but they always
were Fortune’s favorites ! '' '
* * * #
What is up with Briseis Extended (Tas.) ? Is a
Bull raid contemplated, or are the “ snuffers” out? Any-
way, the stock is sickly, and the blue-nosed crowd who
flitted from Gladstone into Extended are not perky. The
coup over the engagement, as manager, of Williamson
from the Briseis fell rather flat. Everyone knows that
Paul Northey has fixed a standard, and to live up to it
without that apostle being in charge is not the -easiest
thing in the world. Then there have been whispers
that the bottom is showing ; but it is treason
to hint at shch a possibility, mucji less to ask
why boring is not gone on with. It is said, too,
that the public eye Was not dimmed to the fact that,
had it not been for the sale of reserve shares, co.
would have shown a very poor balance sheet. There has
been too much talk over the Briseis Extended. It
was the same with the Briseis, ditto with the New
Brothers Home No. I, ditto with the Gladstone, ditto
with the Wyniford, ditto with the St. Helens, and ditto
with the Mt. Stronach. Not one of these shows has come
up to expectations, so why should the public plunge over
them ?

The first period consisted of eight months. The crescendo
movement, it will be seen, arises largely from an increase
of over £lO in the price of copper. With this has to be
linked a reduction in costs from 15s. Id. to 13s. 3Jd. A
veil is drawn over the trading operations of the co. in
respect to its superphosphate works, save as relates to
expenditure. Perhaps this is excusable, as the co. has
rivals in the business ; but shareholders need to watch
this enterprise closely to see that it does not eat up
profits.
* * * •
■ J. Bull is again to be asked to put a bit into a
Northern Territory dredging scheme ; miles and miles on
the McKinley River and Bridge Creek have been taken
up for the purpose. If a Victorian or Maoriland syndi-
cate ran the business, as similar businesses are run in
those States, there would be money in it; but the Aus-
tralian Mining and Development Co., a Melbourne con-
cern, made such a mess of a similar enterprise a few years
back that nobody had the pluck to approach Colli ns-
street again. The N.T. is the only part of Australia where
the mining co. has had practically unlimited cheap dis-
colored labor; and is the only part of Australia where
every mining co. has come to grief on the rock of Costs.
The yellow laborer is in nearly every case horribly dear.
But the manager and the co. have heard so much about
“ cheap labor ” that when they have got that blessing,
they have started out themselves to do the thing with
Oriental magnificence ; and between that, and employing
cheap labor that isn’t cheap, all N.T. enterprises have
eome to grief The proposed new concern has the
advantage, anyway, of knowing what rocks to avoid.

A man up that way sends a note about the Anglo-
Melbourne scheme, promoted by the evergreen Butters,
late H. H. Drysdale, and others :—“ J. S. Butters holds
6000 square miles on the Roper (N.T.)—lOO miles frontage
on both sides. The eastern portion was recently sold to
the Scottish Investment Co., comprising 50 miles on each
side of the river. This co. intends to settle Scotch
families there. The thing is preposterous. The country
is not suitable for any kind of agriculture owing to floods.
The western side is suitable for stock-raising. Should
the scheme be carried out, it can only fail, and add
another to the long black list now stuck on the back-door
of Australia.”

At Bynoe Harbor (N.T.), a Chow, naturalised,
cleared £20,000 out of a tin show he got from a nigger for
a smoke. Bynoe Harbor is pegged out for miles by Chows,
and dummied by whites in Palmerston. One man has
about 20 claims, worked by the heathen ; his gains are 10
p.c. on results. A scandalous business, this dummying.
TJp in the Northern Territory Government
Geologist Brown has had a look at what he describes as
“ probably the largest wolfram deposit yet found in Aus-
tralia.” The shoot has been worked down to 30ft., has a
width of 14ft. in places, and a length of 40ft., and is still
underfoot. The shoot Is in a lode which can be traced 14
chains, and wolfram can be found all along it. The matrix
is quartz ; and the metal is mixed with copper and a little
scheelite. Arrangements had been made to send out a
ton a day from this mine, when the wolfram
market slumped, and the owners sat down to
think things over. The demand for the metal is
in so few hands that it is very easy to con-
trol the market so long as producers are not
united ; so they are scattered, and mostly “ tucker-
men,” and there seems no hope of fixing up an under-
standing among them. Probably the real value of
wolfram will never be found out until Australia gets its
own iron and steel works and can make tests for itself.
John Moffatt, the biggest mining man in North Queens-
land, says he has a deposit which could supply the
world’s market at present rate of consumption ; but
while the market can be hacked to pieces, the deposit
isn’t worth touching—which is another excellent reason
for starting to do things for ourselves, and getting our-
selves out of the foreigner’s hands, and making our own
market for our own raw products.
* * * *
Experience of the Waiotahi, Thames, has been
the most sensational in recent Maoriland mining history,
and recalls the time, 20 years ago, when the original pros-
pectors of the Waihi (now among the first dozen gold
mines of the world) vainly hawked the show round in
order to get some help to develop it. Twelve months ago
the Waiotahi was market-valued at about £2OOO ; to-day
it stands at £210,000. What the mine can really do is,
however, not yet fully proved, but by the end of the year
it ought to be opened up.
* * * *
A name that a whole lot of people would be
glad to forget has turned up again in Australian mining
news ; Alex. Stewart has joined the London board of the
British Broken' Hill. Stewart’s five years’ career in Aus-
tralia was about as sensational as any man’s. He came
out as assistant to General Manager Howell, then on the
point of retiring from the Broken Hill Prop. ; in a few
months he followed him as General Manager, and was the
most unapproachable chief the B.H. Prop, ever had. He
was a Scotchman with mining experience gained chiefly
in Spain, and had a passion for method. It used to be
said that, when a man wanted to sneeze he had to fill in
Form 5371 Z, and lodge it at the office. His idiosyncrasies
made him the best-advertised mining man in Australia;
and when the old Chillagoe people had a boom to work up
they rqped in Stewart as manager. The public fell
in wholesale- They had had doubts about Chillagoe;
but doubts disappeared when an ultra-cautious man like
Stewart was willing to throw up the B.H. Prop, to take
the Chillagoe job. They argued that Chillagoe was bigger
than Broken Hill—and as they got in at boom prices, the
old Chillagoe crowd got out. Stewart’s salary was under-
stood to be £lO,OOO a year ; but that was onty mentioned
to “prove” how vast a thing the. new show was. The
crash soon came ; but Stewart was gone, broken, it was
understood, in health ; and he never returned. There is
very little doubt that, so far as Stewart was concerned,
he had simply made a bad miscalculation of the value
of the mines. George Smith, his lieutenant, and sup-
posed to be one of the very few men in his con-
fidence, was left behind to fight the battle ; and it’s
something in Stewart’s favor that Smith’s confidence in
his ex-Chief was unshaken. Smith, anyhow, made
nothing out of the fiasco, and, completely knocked out,
crept into a comparatively poor but unexciting billet as
an inspector of mines in N.S.W. ; while Stewart, who had
been used—probably without his knowledge—as the
decoy duck in the most disastrous mining fiasco Australia
has had, set up in a quiet way in England as a consulting
engineer.

Latest London float is the Loch Mines, Ltd.,
in 450,000 2s. shares, to take over a basketful of proved
wild-cats—the Lady Loch, the Forrest King, Forrest
King North, a 20-stamp battery, and 463,188 shares
in the Jacoletti Gold Mines, Ltd. It’s a lot to
get for the money ; and, anyhow, the 20 stamps
should be worth something. Nearly every one of
the concerns now included in this marvellous collection
has done a lot of damage to the public hitherto ; and it
will be the public’s own fault if the basketful is given the
chance of doing it any further damage.
“ J.Y.” voices a real grievance :
Three years ago I had a good appointment in Maori*
land. I embezzled, and found myself in the dock listen-
ing to “ His Honor ” saying he did not know why I did
such a thing, how sorry he was, and that two years’ hard
labor was the term of my imprisonment. This little
homily of his was quite wasted. If he did not know why
I was there, I did. ' If he was sorry, I was sorrier, and I
had to do the time, anyhow. The next stage was the
gaol, where I hoped the two years would be worked out
without further comment. I was put in a gang with a
“lifer ” (attempted wife poisoning) on one side of me and
a “ ten-year ” man (violent assault) on the other. The
second Friday I was there I was surprised to see four
women come marching down the yard. One of these was
a double-chinned fat old dame in silk, wife of a merchant
who failed for about £BOOO, and paid 2s. sd. in the £. (I
got two years for using £74 6s. 9d.) The other three were
under-brained, over-dressed, curiosity hunters, who
gazed intently at the broad arrows on our clothing, and
looked with apparent interest at the heap of stones we
were “ napping.” One of them wished to shake hands
with the wife-poisoner, and they all regarded us like a
menagerie of wild animals. This was the addition to my
sentence, a horror I had to submit to each alternate Fri-
day during my two years—every time a different crowd
of empty-headed, bedecked, befurred, bejewelled, vain-
glorious women, whose idle curiosity brought them to
the gaol to gape at the adversity of fellow human beings.
To these female gazers and tattlers I would say this :
If you could hear what the “ hardened ” criminals say
about you when you have gone, you would shun the gaol
as you would the cholera. If you heard the curses your
presence brings from the lips of the better class prisoner
(if there is a better class), you would never set foot
in a gaol again. The visits of these fussy, officious,
misguided women have raised up a hatred in my heart
against the whole female race. If the Maoriland Minister
~of Justice closed at once and for ever the visiting system
to the gaols (except in the case of relations), he would
take from the prisoners the worst punishment they have
to bear, a punishment that was not included in their
sentence by the Judge.
—
Thus W. D. Johnson, the defeated leader
of the Westralian Labor party :
The Labor party would have to alter its methods. It
was not the caucus and the pledge that were wrong. The
party would have to broaden its views when it had the
responsibilities of managing the State. There were even
greater things than the planks of the Labor platform.
One was the question of finance. The party was drifting
to ruin, and it could only improve its condition by send-
ing men to Parliament who realised the greatness of their
responsibilities, who could rise above the State Labor
platform and grapple with the questions before them.
They wanted quality there—men who were loyal to the
Labor cause and were able to take upon themselves the
responsibility of ruling the destinies of Australia.
Therein Johnson struck the mark. A party may
get along in Opposition without a fiscal policy, but
when it takes charge of the Treasury without any
definite financial principles, it is a bottomless ship
without a rudder.
The electric plant of the Federal Palace Hotel,
corner Collins and King Streets, Melbourne, furnishes a
good example of a thoroughly successful electric installa-
tion, in technical as well as in commercial respects. This
plant generates all electric current required for the hotel
lighting ; it consists of a 20 horse-power gas engine belted
to a “Ganz” 12 kilowatts dynamo, a “Ganz” switch-
board containing all the controlling and measuring instru-
ments, and a storage battery with 62 cells. The generating
machinery runs continually during the day time at its
full capacity and highest efficiency ; the surplus current
being stored into the battery; the latter supplies all
necessary electricity required during the night; the
battery forms also a substantial reserve in case of acci-
dents. Compared with the previous method of working a
saving of £4OO per annum has been effected by this plant.
The contractors for this plant were The Austral Otis
Engineering Company, Ltd., corner Hanah and Kavanaglr
Streets, South Melbourne, as sole agents for Ganz and
Company, Budapest, Hungary.* [3.]

Wilfley Ore Concentrating Tables. Important
notice to mine managers and users of Concentrators.
The Supreme Court of Victoria has decided in the action
brought against N. Guthridge Limited that the well-
known Wilfley patent is valid, and has been infringed by
N. Guthridge Limited by the manufacture and sale of
tables which they call the Card Concentrating Table.
The Court granted a certificate of the validity of the
Wilfley patent and ordered N. Guthridge Limited to pay
damages and costs of this action, and granted an
injunction restraining N. Guthridge Limited from in-
fringing the Wilfley patent. This is to give notice
that the Wilfley Ore Concentrator Syndicate Limited will
enfore its rights against all persons buying or selling or
otherwise dealing in or using such infringing Concentrat-
ing machines- Edward J. Rigby, Attorney for the Wilfley
Ore Concentrator Syndicate Limited, care of Austral
Otis Engineering Co., South Melbourne.*

The Queensland Smelting Company Limited,
Aldershot, Queensland, announce that they are buyers,
smelters, and refiners of gold, silver, lead, copper ores,
matte, bullion furnace and cyanide products. Tariff and
particulars obtainable from the manager.*
It is of great importance to those interested in
mining- to understand that the judgment in the recent
action oi the Wilfley Co., does not deprive the industry
tt ' e latest development in concentrators.
ineOard concentrates oh an entirely opposite principle
to the Wilfley. In the latter the dressing takes place on
tiie smooth surface at the ends of the diagonal riffles
which !s. claimed by Wilfley as the essence of his invention.
Whilst in the Card the dressing- takes place in the
continuous channels and not on the surface of the table,
hence much greater capacity, higher concentrates and
poorer tailings. The injunction applies merely to two
experimental tops which were never sold by theiicensees,
N. Guthridge Limited, who are continuing the manu-
facture and sale of the Card.*

.The British Broken Hill Silver Mining Co.,
after careful consideration of all types of boilers, have
placed an order with Babcock and Wilcox for three large
boilers, each of 500 h.p. Superheaters, chain grate
mechanical stokers and economiser have also been
ordered from the same firm. This now makes 40 boilers
of the Babcock and Wilcox type supplied to the various
mines and industries at Broken Hill. Wherever economy
in fuel and maintenance of boiler lias to be seriously con-
sidered by competent engineers, the Babcock boiler is
installed. Hence the recent unanimous decision of the
Expert Admiralty Committee to install Babcock boilers as
the standard for the British Navy.*

The strength, lightness, durability and low cost
of spiral rivited pipe have given it a place of superiority
above any other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting strength, and is protected, inside and out, with a
perfect coating manufactured from asphalt. Asphalt
coated steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 years without
any sign of decay, and may last as long again. Spiral
pipe is manufactured by Mephan Ferguson, at Wanganui,
New Zealand, and at his engineering and pipe works,
Melbourne, where the latest up-to-date machinery is also
used for making boilers, fluming, refrigerators, bolts, nuts,
and every discriDtion of cast or wrought iron work.*

Aboriginalities.
The plaint of “ E.G.” :
“ Snell’s ” par. (28/9/’O5), written in derision and
humorous scorn of the man v'ho “ outed ” a woman,
and protested that “it was done in a fair light,”
reminds me. “Snell ” obviously never had a scrap with
a female, and I want to move his heart to more sympathy
-—with the man. When I was a lad I had a sister some-
what older, stout and muscular —so muscular that more
than once for a joke she put her broad back under a full
2001 b. flour-bag, seized one ear over her shoulder in
orthodox fashion, and humped it up an inclined
plane from dray to store-room. Une day we
fell out over the priority of right to Scott’s
“ Antiquary.” 1 was in temporary possession, and repulsed
her demands. Thereupon she resorted to physical force.
I was punched, and thumped, and walloped, and gener-
ally and continuously handled in such fashion that an
effort to regain my own self-respect had to be made.
Therefore 1 shook myself loose, and sparred for an open-
ing amidst a whirl.ng mass of beefy arms and huge lists.
The opening came, and I shot a straight left on the tip-
tilted end of a nose which yielded like dough to the im-
pact. I have never before, or since, punched so soft and
yielding a nose, nor have I ever, except on t lie face of a sud-
denly awakened somnambulist, seen such a look of helpless
bewilderment as spread over my sister’s countenance.
She had never in all her eighteen years had a punch on
the nose before, you see, and it opened to her a new view
of life and mankind. It also relieved the rush of hlood to
her head which had precipitated the misunderstanding.
“ You wretched coward! ” she said, and collapsed in
tears. “ That’ll teach you,” 1 retorted, and I left, feeling
like “Snell’s” man, that it was done in fair fight, and
I’ve never been able to feel differently since. But when I
picked a wife, she was a little one with no fight in her. I
didn’t want to have to punch her nose. 1 have just read
this to her, and she said 1 was a coward. I asked her
what else I could have done, and she looked volumes of
contempt. That’s her way, and it’s worse than my
sister’s.
* * * *
“ Uclke ” : Ever noticed the way in which
deadly enemies in the feathered tribe will band
together against a common danger ? While
driving through the bush recently I saw a goanna
steadily climbing a tree, keeping in view a
magpie’s nest, which had been built in a branch.
The owners of the nest were keeping up a frantic
attack on the intruder. Presently a number of
soldier birds and a tomtit joined forces with the
magpies. Later a crow, two butcher birds, and a
sparrow hawk swooped down upon the now un-
comfortable “ go.” At the end of the engagement
a one-eyed, miserable “go.” was making a light-
ning calculation as to whether it would be quicker
to jump or to fall to the ground, whilst a butcher
bird was determinedly chasing the tomtit. Three
days afterwards, when re-passing the nest, I
noticed a crow committing a burglary there, and
the only help the screaming magpies got was
from the ever-willing soldier bird. The crow got
an egg.
‘ ‘ Ray X. on camels :
The camel may be mild-eyed, and that is all that can
be said for him in that direction. Speaking generally, he
is the most cantankerous, spiteful, revengeful, smellful
beast on the planet. If he takes a dislike to anyone it is
a fixture, and if anyone treats him badly he is cool and
Calculating in his revenge.
Once we were out prospecting on the Westralian border
with several name's, one of which was a savage bull that
caused us cons.derable delay in loading o’ mornings. One
day Frank lost his temper and banged him over the head
with a pick-handle. That camel thereupon swore an oath
on the Koran to be revenged. Several times he tried to
crush Frank in his sleep, and once he made a bite which
our mate was just able to dodge with the loss of a trouser
leg and the gain of a deep, poisonous scratch which abso-
lutely declined to heal. Two months later, ajter a long
day's journev to make a water hole, the sleeping camp
was suddenly roused by a death shriek which made even
our nigger rouse up enough to mutter, “ White pheller
blurry fool ” before he went to sleep again. We found
Frank as dead as the father of all camel drivers. The animal
had crept up silently and in a business-like way had lain
down on his chest, killing him almost instantly.
Once a bull camel and a cow tumbled over our tent,
barely allowing us to escape with our reputation (we had
little else on, the night being hot). We remonstrated
with pieces of quartz from a distance. The bull got
annoyed and gave chase. I climbed a desert oak and my
companion a large boulder, on the point of which he
stood with his reputation waving in the wind until the
bull saw fit to retire.
Even when the ship of the desert is in a calm, so to
speak, the unspeakable odor of his breath and the way he
rolls his food about in his interior are enough to make
the Sphinx feel sea-sick. Long will I remember their
disgusting presence, when I was flattened out with some
sort of fever out back from Arltunga—how they lay
about the camp, and stank, and turned Monday’s break-
fast on top of Saturdays, and attracted all the flies in the
territory to one grand carnival.
Well, to give the devil or camel—same thing—his due,
he has made the back goldfields habitable after having
mainly enabled us to find them. But he is a mixed
blessing’, and is only really liked by those who gained
their knowledge of him from the pages of Eastern
romance.

“The Prooshan” : “Janor” (B. 26/10/’O5),
in commenting on my “panning off” par, shows
that he knows a bit about it, and a close inspec-
tion would doubtless show bis finger-nails worn
to the quick from friction with the subject. It’s
many a year since I washed a dish or “ spat in the
very eye of the gold and sand.” “Janor’’says
“it works well in handling coarse gold.” I quite
agree with him, and, as a matter of habit, always
insisted on having coarse gold to pan at—it is
easier to treat, looks nicer in the dish, and gives
greater satisfaction. “Janor” mustn’t think,
however, that dishing days are done, for many a
poor devil can’t raise a cradle or box, and goes
per force of circumstances with a dish and a bent
hoop-iron scraper. He goes likewise bootless on
the coldest days of winter at the back-aching job,
in old creeks and odd corners, hunting for the few
grains of existence that he just manages to scrape
together. “ Janor” should get on to a “poverty
point ” community proper, and then he’d soon
find that dishing was still alive.

“Dave H.R.” : As to the dingo (B.
2/11/’O5). My father, way back in the ’sixties,
while on an out-back station, one day gave a
blackfellow two figs of tobacco to procure him a
wild dingo pup. He trained the animal, and,
when it was about twelve months or so of age, it
was the most faithful canine friend a man could
desire. My father had this dog for some five or
six years, when it died of distemper. I know a
selector near Barraba (N.S.W.) who has a tame
dingo. He will snap at no one, but is very shy,
and invariably flees at the approach of a stranger.
About dingoes barking : there are lots of them in
Culnooey scrub, and at night there is a great
noise there.- I don’t know whether it is George
Reid looking for Socialism or whether it is the
dingoes barking ; but it may be Bruce Smith
cheering for the Japanese.

“Nomad”: Have visited most of the
shearing-sheds in the New England district and
find that none is as up-to-date as those of Southern
Queensland. In the majority of the sheds in the
Northern State will be found a complete station
school of arts. All the latest books, periodicals,
papers, and games are at the disposal of the
shearers and rouseabouts for the small fee of (id.
p.rweek. Every detail is carefully attended to,
even to the appointment of librarian, and the hour
of closing the show (9 p.m. as a rule); and no
gambhng is allowed.

“ Yigilans ” : Amongst the things you see
in the Northern Territory when you are out with-
out a gun is the railway from Port Darwin to
Pine Creek. It runs bi-weekly, and has, on an
average, one European passenger and a couple
of Chows in the 140 miles jdurney. At every
stopping place after you leave the Adelaide River
you see a horde of jabbering Asiatics, but rarely a
white man. To the Adelaide, a distance of 100
miles, you run through a wilderness, where there
are no inhabitants but the lengthsmen. These
used to be Chows also, but the heathen have been
supplanted by Europeans at Bs. a day. Another
illustration is to be found here of what whites can
do in the tropics. The sleepers are steel and the
heat great, yet men have been here for a score of
years and are jolly and fat. The Chows will soon
have the railway on tribute, and run it with a
dragon-shaped engine and a joss on the cow-
catcher for luck.
“Agra”: Go on the
land, young man ! Recently
knew a hard-working farmer
in South Gippsland, who,
after 15 years of terrible toil,
was just getting his head
above water. He was almost
past work by that time, and
dependent on his two sons,
aged respectively 21 and 17.
Both boys caught cold while
cutting scrub. The eldest
died of pneumonia in three
days. The younger one lay
at death’s door for a fort-
night, spitting blood. When
the father was told that he
would get about again, but
would never be of much use
on the farm, he took to his
bed and died within 24 hours.
The widow had to sell the
farm for a song. Her two
eldest daughters are now
domestic servants. In an-
other instance, in the same
district, a farmer and his
family invested £750 in a
selection. They worked on
it like slaves for 19 years,
spending £2O per acre in
clearing some of it, and were
then glad to sell out for £750.
Yes, why not go on the land,
young man ?

“ Parvo ” : I know a lady who had a hen
wanting to “set.” The bird was obliged with a
clutch of eggs, but such was the cussedness of the
fowl that, after a few days, she tired of her quiet
life and decided to leave. A friend advised that
the desire to do her duty to the birth-rate would
return if a dose of spirits were administered, and
the housewife tried it. The truant made back to
the nest while “ under the influence” ; but when
the effect went off, the hen did likewise. The
owner decided that it would be cheaper to lose
the eggs than to keep the fowl in grog, especially
as her drinking capabilities would probably in-
crease as she acquired the taste. There would also
have been the risk of her breaking eggs and
generally raising Cain in trying to defend her
nest against imaginary snakes. It would be
interesting to see, in the poultry-yard of the
future, a number of prospective mother ehoolties
waiting around in the early hours for the bar to
open. Then there would come a time when dealers
would distinguish between “ drunk eggs ” and
“sober eggs”; and the hen with hiccups would
begin to degrade the landscape, and the rooster
with the red nose would come home and break up
the fowl-house. Better leave things alone.

“ Wood B.” : I agree with “ Nulla ” as to
the danger of a bite from a “ goanner.” I’d almost
as soon be bitten by an adder. One thing which
must be placed to his (the go.’s) credit, however,
is that he can take his grog like a coachman.
Some time ago, three station-hands caught one in
the bar of a wayback pub. One chap opined that
“the poor bloke wanted a drink,” whereat his
goannaship displayed several inches of parched
tongue. They shouted in turn for him, pouring
the chain lightning down his throat. At each
repetition of the dose, an expression of ineffable
joy overspread his bland countenance. They then
let him go, expecting to see him do a serpentine
cakewalk to the nearest tree. Instead, however,
he began to indulge in familiarities with the bar-
maid. The lady was angry, and caught him by
the tail, and banged him thrice against the door-
post, and his drunken career would doubtless have
ended there and then had not one of the men
rescued him. “Poor old chap,” said he, “I’m
afraid he’s done for. Let’s try him with another,
anyhow.” Two more drinks were poured into
him without effect, and a boy commenced to drag
him away for burial when he suddenly revived.
The men were too disgusted with “ the dashed old
humbug” to respond. Just then, the landlord’s
cattle dog put in an appearance, and thego. sprinted
like a flash to a pine tree, about 150 yd. away,
beating the dog by the length of three dogs. All
hands followed to see him negotiate the tree.
They found him reclining on a branch 12ft. up,
and, beyond a rakish tilt of the head and a fatuous
leer, there was nothing to show that he contained
three whiskies, two rums and a long beer,

“ Yarrum ” ; People troubled with cat-
music o’ nights have a remedy at hand in the
common toad-fish. A Maitland lawyer, troubled
with insomnia and neighbors’ cats, took several of
the fish from Lake Macquarie, and used them
with such effect that there was a dearth of pussy
in his neighborhood for many years after. A
cocky, living at the lake, lost a fine lot of ducks,
which gobbled toad-fish left on the shore by
prawn-netters. I believe the toad-fish would kill
a herd of elephants—fish-eating elephants.
THE AUSTRALIAN BARD.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Talk not to me of bards of old,
Whose fame through ages long hath rolled ;
Enshrine them not in bronze or gold,
Or foreign stone ;
They rest well in their mother mould—
Leave them alone 1
Come, fill a glass in kind regard
To one whose life is dimly starred—
The hall-marked true Australian bard
Who gaily sings,
With little hope for just reward,
Australian things.
He tells us in a ringing tune
The charms beneath the sun and moon,
The sombre bush where spirits croon
At eerie hours,
And e’en a prosy dull lagoon
Awakes his powers.
But stop and drop your swag a spell,
No doubt you started off quite well,
No doubt your patriotic yell
Will encored be.
Australian bards should sing like h ,
Both gay and free.
I mind it well when first I tried
To climb Parnassus’ sirubby side,
My knowing friends with vigor cried
And jeering spite,
“ Well, by my faith, that bloke has hide —
Thinks he can write.”
Long coils of rhyme I nightly wrote,
I sang the true Australian n >te.
On Nature’s pictures I would dote
By midnight oil,
And thought the day was not remote
To quit hard toil.
I thought that editors would print
The sterling verses from my mint,
And praise and payment without stint
I would command.
For when great we did on them squint,
Said “ Man, they’re grand.
“ The Austral note is ringing true.
The downy wattle’s saffron hue,
With gum trees wild, and skies of blue,
Rare wreaths you twine ;
And We can sniff the kangaroo
In every line.
“ The wombat, 'possum, native bear,
Your song of praise do rightly share.
The lyre-bird in his sylvan lair
Your fame will tell.
The mopoke by your name will swear,
And dingoes yell.”
Life doth not hold sufficient days
To here record the words of praise
That I got for my staving lays.
Their sentiment
Did win a whole cartload of bays—
But not a cent.
Tho’ I in Fame’s bright glare did shine,
They did not pay me for a line,
That I for once might fitly dine
As I’ve desired,
Or patch those dungarees of mine
Where most required.
The Inky Way for bards is rough,
The meat they gain is small and tough,
And ragged almost to the buff
They run their race,
While hackneyed cheap imported stuff
Fills printers’ space.
vie.
W.H.

“ Paul Cupid ” : Among a recent batch of
“deaths ” I saw the name of a man who did as
mean a trick as I’ve ever known to be played on a
laborer. A sailor deserted his ship, and got
employment from this person at 12s. a week.
After being there three months he wanted to see
at least some of his wages, but was put off with an
excuse. He was husking corn one day when a
policeman chanced to come round collecting
statistics. The employer saw his chance, hurriedly
gave the sailor some bread and meat and advice,
told him the policeman had come for him, and
generously offered to keep the officer in conversa-
tion while the alarmed sailor decamped. That
mployer attended church regularly, and died in
the odor of sanctity.
A GOOD ALL-ROUND ARTICLE.
Some remarks made at the last meeting of the Newtown
Council by an alderman who claimed from a long trade
experience to know a good deal about the matter, are
calculated to cause some public alarm. According to
him some of the morning coffee consists largely of burnt
corn, the jam which delighted the younger members of
the family was not always composed of wholesome fruit,
the piquant sauces so admired as adjuncts to cold mutton
or corned beef are made from injurious compounds, and
what is sold as pure malt vinegar, is sometimes only a
chemical concoction. These were only a few of the
articles upon which the speaker furnished startling in-
formation as to their component parts.— Sydney paper.

than take ’alf a pound of our Reliability
Brand. It does equally well for soap or for
butter."
“Helter-Skelter” : The appointment by
the Education Department (Vic.) of a supervisor
to inspect and report on the housing of its female
bush teachers is in no way premature. Recently,
on arriving at a backblocks spot in 8. Gippsland to
take charge of the local school, a teacher (female)
was directed to the only available boarding-house,
her predecessor (a male) having batched in the
school liumpy. After resting in a room which
served as a kitchen, dining-room, parlor and draw-
ing-room all in one, she asked to be shown her
bedroom, and was escorted to an apartment
entered only from the “ drawing-room ” and by a
doorless aperture. On the teacher remarking the
absence of a door, the landlady reassuringly in-
formed her, “ Oh, you’ll be all right; no one will
hurt you.” But the idea of seven, or eight gaping
youths having a full view of her boudoir didn’t
appeal to her, and she curtained off the place
where the door ought to have been with some
material which she fortunately had in her trunk.
The camp allotted her had a “ natural carpet,” and
a bush-made wooden bed, whilst an empty case
and a tin dish did se vi< e as a wash-stand.

“ Alba ” : I have put in a number of years
studying common fleas. A mill owner in N.
Queensland showed me a particular specimen cap-
tured in a bunk occupied by a sick Kanaka. It
was dull red in color, with a black spot in the
middle of its back. Viewed through a microscope
it made one shiver and swear off sugar. Its body
was as stiff as a battleship ; it had the hind claws
of a devil fish ; and its eyes shone like scraps of
molten fire. The black spot on its back became
inflated like a camel’s hump when it drew nourish-
ment from its host. A keeper, employed in a cir-
cus, introduced me to a lion-flea once. Under a
glass it became a baleful horror capable of alarm-
ing a rhinoceros. Menagerie owners speak enthusi-
astically of the lion-flea ; it has to rise early and
bite hard to extract nourishment from the lion’s
shin bone. The Australian cow-flea is not a back
number. Have gone among a mob of milkers
wearing white pants, and in nine seconds have
been speckled from heel to waist by the ferocious
parasites. A Sydney Hebrew says that the pawn-
shop insect is a match for anything that jumps.
Being deprived of its lawful refreshment, while in
pawn, it becomes a very earnest insect. He is
willing to back the stranded portmanteau and old
overcoat flea against any lion or tiger nipper the
Zoo can produce. I will put up a theatre flea to
stiffen both in seven seconds—the one that bounds
over the peanut man the moment the music starts.

“ Nym.” : The Fisheries Act is a dead
letter as far as Vic. is concerned, especially round
the Big Trickle waterways. The vandal anglers
are, in some cases, prominent men in the so-called
local Game Protection Societies, yet are the first to
break the law on the sly. Until we have a rod
and gun license, the fees of which go to sub-
sidise paid inspectors, the Game Act, either
for fish or fowl, will be little better than a.farce.
Even now a nice little technicality would arise if
a zealous assistant-inspector brought a charge
against an angler caught (say on the Campaspe)
with a cod in his possession out of season. The
trout becomes awfully like the cod, especially in
waters liable to the sludge curse, after a season or
two. People may fish for trout with impunity (and
worms) while the close season is on for cod, perch,
and native fish generally. Who would dare swear
a cod was a trout, or vice versa ?
* * * *
“ Roma,” from Gippsland :
If it were not for way-backers, patent-medicine vendors
would have to shut up shop. Everything- that is recom-
mended in the newspapers is tried for all sorts of imagi-
nary complaints. If a neighbor takes ill, the nearest Mrs.
Cocky sends all her remedies to experiment on the
sufferer—sometimes with disastrous results.
One woman up this way (S. Gipps., Vic.) got a cure
for sandy blight 15 years ago, when the small cockyites
were afflicted. One bottle was not used, so was carefully
kept till wanted. Last week a neighbor was discovered
to have something growing over the eye. The sandy-
blight cure was promptly forthcoming as an infallible
remedy for cataract, cross-eyes, wall-eyes, ophthalmia,
granulation, or blindness. What the result will be
remains to be seen, but at present things are only
middling.
Another case happened way back amongst the moun-
tains where doctors are rare. A cocky’s 18-year-old
daughter took ill one night, and all sorts of household
remedies were used without avail. At length all the
neighbors for miles around were sent to, and they
promptly responded to the call, in person, with their
medicine chests in their aprons. By the time Miss Cocky
had about 10 gossiping neighbors around her, and had
tried all their remedies, she was forced to admit the true
state of affairs, which she had up till then concealed.
When the kookaburras were announcing the arrival of
dawn, 10 disgusted she-cockies were trudging homeward
over the mountains to start milking, all bewailing the
loss of a night’s sleep and innumerable bottles of patent
me heinev— all for.a hussy with an infant.
“ Werrimee” : One clay, while out shooting
near Casino (N.S.W.), I came across an old
gin standing at the butt of a gum tree,
holding a piece of bark about a foot square
in her hands, while several dogs sat on
their haunches star-gazing beside her. Her
other half, glistening in the altogether, was
100 ft. up the tree, extracting honey from a hole he
had cut in the side of a limb. As he dropped the
comb, piece by piece, the gin (when not interfered
with) caught it dexterously on the sheet of bark,
and passed it to the billy-cans. The dogs, how-
ever, gave her a lot of trouble. One or two would
yelp and spring at the descending comb, some-
times catching it (and smashing it), but mostly
sending it and the bark and the gin spinning in
three different directions. Then would follow a
whirlwind of sticks, bedraggled skirts, unkempt
hair and vituperation, mingled with a deep
baritone snort of disgust from aloft. The storm
moderating, her long, thin fingers and the billy-
lid went to the rescue of the depreciated segments
of “sugar-bag.” Murri and his wife practise the
good doctrine, waste not and thou slialt want less.
The young bees and bee-bread are eaten ; at times
honey, comb, bees, and bee-bread are chopped
up together, and a feast made of the conglomera-
tion. About those Richmond River towns, Mr.
and Mrs. Blackfellow often earn an honest penny
by bringing in wild honey for the whites. This is
generally bought in the comb; but, unless par-
ticularly stipulated, the comb and other ingredients
are retained by the blacks. Strained honey from
this source, however, is a doubtful luxury, as
it is just as likely to be strained through the
tail of old Maggie’s frock as through anything
else. The blacks are very fond of native bee
honey, of which there are two kinds on the
Northern rivers, known in the locality as boo-
yan and cubbi. They frequently made this
into toffee by simply boiling until it coagulated,
and old men as well as piccaninnies derived much
pleasure from sucking the lolly-sticks. Probably,
in the course of time, had they been unmolested,
a creditable confectionery business would have
been developed among them.

“ Moide ” : Heartfelt thankfulness is ex-
pressed by bushwomen at X. (Gfippsland, Vic.)
over the departure of the late teacher. I am re-
lated to her successor, and am spending my vaca-
tion with her. Many and varied are the delin-
quencies of the former ped. In awestruck
whispers they tell me : “ Why, Miss, what would
you think of ’er ? She were that balmy she useter
sleep all night with ’er window ’igh up, and the
cold wind blowin’ in on ’er,” Then comes the re-
lation of the “last straw.” “One mornin’, Miss,
we foller’d’er to see what she did in the gully
before brekfust; and there she were, Miss, with
’er arms outstretched ’oldin’ up ’er ’ead and
whiffin’ loud and long through ’er nose. Oh, she
wuz quite orf ’er ’ead, and we wasn’t on fer board-
in’ ’er any longer. No one else wanted ’er, so she
’ad to go.” We camp in a lean-to at the back of
the school, and carefully conceal the fact that we
are “ tarred with the same brush” by adjusting
the window when the cockies are in Snoreland,
and what time the mopolce calls to his mate we
indulge the “deep breathing” cult.
“ 5.P.8.” : The bush liar walked up with
a jaunty air. The boss surveyed him critically and
he looked hard at the boss. “Any chance of a
job, boss ?” he said abruptly. “ Can you milk ? ’
said the boss, casting a furtive glance at his white
hands. “ Milk ! ” said the stranger, “why I
reckon I orter be able t'r seein’ as I put in 14 years
oil one dairy farm.” “Humph” said the boss.
Then he reflected, after which he asked: “ Can
you butcher?” “Butcher!” said the traveller dis-
dainfully. “That’s my trade; served seven years
at it.” The boss said “Ah !” and did a think.
“Do you know anything about breaking in
horses?” he inquired. The bush liar indulged in
a fit of boisterous merriment. “Lor bless yr
heart, boss,” he said, “I’ll never forget the six
years I put in on Werribee Park breaking in, and
they were wild ’uns too, I tell y’r.” A humorous
twinkle danced in the boss’s eyes. “I suppose
you can drive bullocks?” he said. “Drive any
blamed thing that ever was hooked to the bars.
Bless me soul, a man oughter learn something
after four years experience.” The boss reflected
at great length. “How old are you?” he said
abruptly. “ I’m j ust twenty-seven, ” said the bush
liar, “and nary a tap of work have I done this four
years,” and he heaved a sigh as he spoke. The
boss looked sad. “I don’t think I have anything
for you just at present” he said, “but I noticed
an advertisement in the Coretong Courier for a
compositor; perhaps you ” “Compositor!” the
other interrupted, “why that’ll just suit me. I put
in five years at ” But the boss suddenly gave a
sort of strangled yelp and rushed at the bush liar,
and the bush liar ran as if running had been his
sole occupation for 40 years.

‘ ‘ Orthos ” : Travelling west of the Darling
River, where ex-Minister Crick discovered the
youthful genius making nitrogen and oxygen gases
with apparatus made all on his own out of pickle
bottles, jam tins, etc., I came upon a bevy of bare-
footed urchins at work on a mud bank. Thinking
they were making mud pies, I called out “How
is it you are not at school?” “ This is school,
mister,” they answered. “ It’s too blooming hot
to work indoors the master says, as the stuff
bakes, and you can’t work it up proper.” A
faint idea of the new syllabus dawned on me.
Discovered the dominie arrayed in mosquito net,
surrounded with piles of official correspondence.
“The new syllabus,” he mused, repeating my
question. “ Oh, it’s all right. I was a bit afraid
of it at first, but I’ve mastered it. lam supposed
to teach modelling in plasticene once a week,
but my nippers demand it every day, and they
get it. Just look here. This is a bit of work
they presented to me on my birthday ” ; and he
pointed with pride to a life-sized figure of himself
in Darling mud, and about as much like him as a
Myall black. “ The open air is good for their
health, and between times I give them a lesson
in pruning mulga scrub.” I rode off with my
temperature anything over 150 deg. in the shade.

In S. Australia proper there are now
among aborigines three-and-a-half times more

“CAVEAT EMPTOR."
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Hicks sent his horse for sale, you see—
An old flea-bitten grey—
And, in a written guarantee,
In black and white did say
That said grey horse had done a mile
In forty-two and splendid style.
The grey was ridden round the yard,
But not a bid was made.
Perhaps the times were passing hard ;
Perhaps they were afraid
To ride an equine marvel who
Had done a mile in forty-two.
Then Coogan showed to Hicks a mare
Whose points he said he knew.
’Twas she, he was prepared to swear,
That single-handed drew
A boiler up the steep incline
That led to the ’Goanna Mine.
He said he would not be debarred
From any friendly deal,
If only for the high regard
Which he for Hicks did feel.
Nor would he let the value stop
A just and even-handed swop.
The deal was made ; Hicks took the mare,
And Coogan took the grey,
But, Lord ! the way that Hicks did swear
The next succeeding day !
An 1 Coogan’s comprehensive curse,
If possible, was even worse.
He wished Hicks might in Tophet bake
For his consummate guile
In saying that the grey could make
That record for a mile.
“ Old man,” said Hicks, “ your wrath is vain,
That mile was travelled in a train.
“ And what,” continued Hicks, “about
The boiler and the mine ?
Your blessed cuddy couldn’t rout
A melon from a vine ! ”
Said Coogan : “ ’Twas the truth, my son;
The boiler was a kitchen one.”
N.F.W.

8. We are gem experts.
9. We carry a large stock of unset stones.
10. We receive them direct from the mines.
11. We sell at London Prices.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
A notable figure at a recent Melbourne
garden party was Prime Minister Deakin’s
mother, aged 83 years.
Obit. At Narrandera, N.S. Wales, on
October 31, journalist George Jarman in his 83rd
year. He had lived in Australia since 1855.
Seems that W. N. Willis won’t be back to
N.S. Wales as a Xmas box—if he ever gets back
at all. The Government isn’t worrying over the
delay in his return.
“Larry” Harnett, N.S.W. Sergeant-at-
Arms, who has just lost a slice of his salary, has
spent thirty-one years in the Sergeant’s chair at
the end of the Legislative chamber.
Vic. Premier Bent has a fine past before
him, and he is proud of it. At Kilmore the other
day he boasted in a loud voice—“ Fifty years ago
I took first prize for radishes. I was only beaten
in leeks by one man.”
John Plummer, hero of a thousand Free-
trade fallacies, now figures in the N.S. Wales
press as an earnest champion of Australian
industry against the Yankee Harvester Trust.
It’s never too late to mend.
J. W. Walshe, fed, liquored and testi-
monialized in Sydney and suburbs, makes back to
the United Kingdom after nearly a quarter of a
century’s exile. He was imprisoned with Parnell
for conspiracy in 1880.
One Patrick Collins thus addressed
Ballarat Land Board in support of his application
for 10 acres : “I have 18 children—nine sons and
nme daughters—and I’ve not done yet.”
The application of P. Collins was granted. And
if The Bulletin were a land board, it would also
grant Mrs. P. Collins 100 acres and a feeding-
bottle. 8
Current allusions to Chief Justice Parley's
relationship with the Guinness people, of Dublin
brewery fame, recall the fact that the Guinness
who founded the stout business proudly claimed
descent from a fire-eater named Magennis. No
one looking at the N.S.W. Chief Justice would
suspect him of being related to a person named
Magennis.
A Goulburn (N.S.W.) family is putting up
a record in sudden death. An aunt died when
returning from a funeral; then a brother expired
while lighting a fire; another brother dropped
dead while digging in the garden; a third
brother passed out suddenly when addressing a
political meeting, and a sister was last week found
in a dying condition in her kitchen.
“ IF.”
We could take action about the tariff if both parties
were agreeable.—DENRLS.
DEAKIN: "I’ll g[?] you such a hit in the tariff
directly” (a pause) "if you're a[?]reeable.”
Christopher Newton, stepfather of Mrs.
Dailey, who gave evidence last week in the Dailey
divorce ease, is the second son of the once head of
the large Sydney firm, Christopher Newton,
Bros., and Co. C. Newton, senior, began business
in the middle of last century in a small right-of-
way in Pitt-street, about where Farmer and Co.’s
stands now, but at his death he was renting large
premises lower down, for which he paid Ebenezer
Vickery a yearly rental of £3500. Once the old
man, through no fault of his own, was compelled
to compromise, but years after he met every
penny of his indebtedness. After his death, iii
the ’eighties, the business went to pieces, it being
alleged that heavy investments in the once famous
Sunny Corner silver mine brought about the
downfall.
One dreadful feature of Thomas Bent is
his bull-headed persistence with a joke. Once a
remark of his has been laughed at it becomes a
stock item, and he skull-drags it into every subse-
quent oration with abominable iteration. For
instance, he once said that if boom food were
given to babies they would grow as big as himself
m three days. Thoughtless people laughed,
whereupon Tommy docketed it for future use,
and now he hauls that commonplace sentiment
into every speech. If he were called upon to
read a funeral oration over a dead celebrity he
would assuredly say that if the deceased’s tomb-
stone ate boom food it would become a much
bigger tombstone than it is. People always
laugh, because the wheeze is sprung on them
■suddenly in the middle of grave remarks, is
absolutely uncalled for, is pertinent to nothing, and
is just flung out because Bent has recollected it,
and is afraid to postpone it lest he should forget
and be unable to sleep with the undelivered joke
lying heavy on his chest.
The S.M. Herald, in a recent article
entitled “Behind the Scenes in Parliament,” told
how N.S.W. Postmaster-General Daniel Egan
once, in the Pittvvater district, bailed up with a
gun the driver of a provision cart, and robbed
him, Egan was camping out with some friends
and food had run short. Eater on the driver of
the cart, a local settler, waited with some others
on the rowdy old thief to ask for better postal
accommodation, and Egan didn’t see how, in the
public interest, it could be granted. But when
that settler privately informed Egan that he
recognised him, the bushranging Minister threw
the public interests overboard in return for
secrecy. These were the good old days that the
lory party looks back on with deep regret—the
spacious days vvlien there was no Labor party,
and when a Minister could be a silly yahoo flourish-
ing weapons and stealing property on the public
highway, and giving concessions out of the public
revenue to hush the matter up.
