Apropos Iveagh and Darley (B. 16/11705). The connec-
tion between the recently created Viscountand the newlv.
made P.C. is this : —ln the latter part of the 18th century
Frederick Darley married Elizabeth, sister of Arthur
(.runness, the Dublin porter brewer, and had, among
other children, Henry, whose name will be found amongst
the attorneys of Dublin, and who held office as one of the
in Chancery. This Henry married a Miss West,
and had sons and daughters, the eldest being the Chief
Justice of N.S.W. Iveagh is the grandson of this Arthur
P ,, ' n »ess- Frederick Darley was High Sheriff of Dublin
in W™, a year when the Sheriff’s duties were mainly car-
ried out at the nearest lamp-post. In 1808-9 he was Lord
Mayor, and subsequently became Chief Police Magistrate
of the city, and for 40 years—for life, in fact—he was an
alderman of what Daniel O’Connell called the “Rotten
Corporation,” which was wiped out in 1841.
About Henry :
Henry Butler, Melbourne’s Tory rag-seller, whom the
Argus quotes with emphasis on every possible occasion
was quite rude last week. Henry the Great went to
Mornington to eat and drink and say things at the Royal
Yacht Clubs dinner. He voyaged on the s.s. Flinders
owned by his friends MTlwraith & Co. The vessel was
light, and went to Mornington with what an amateur
might thmk a sickening list. The skipper, mindful of the
fact that another skipper had recently shorn off the end
of the pier, refused to go alongside. Henry and his
mends were sent ashore in a boat. There was' a big sea
running. The oarsmen, all amateurs, succeeded in snap-
ping one oar before they were 50yd. from the ship,
-then the boat broached to and the water came aboard
and Henry got wet and was half dead. At the dinner
Henry said fearful things about the condition of the
boat and the company which owned it. The
Argus faithfully reported all that Henry had said in
his excitement, with the salt still in his hai'r. But next
day the shipping companies fell from a considerable
height on Butler and the Argus. A column of denials
and explanations was published, but, all the same libel
proceedings are rumored, and Henry (who is dry now) is
told that dog should not eat dog.
Apropos that Chief Director of Agriculture
(B. 16/11/05). Amongst the officials sent
out in the first jleefc was a Superintendent
of Agriculture, one Nicholas Devine.
Ihere was no agriculture to be superin-
tended, and “ Rick ” was put to more use-
ful work than overlooking nothing ; he
was made an overseer of convicts.
Devine was a County Cavan man, and
for his services had a grant of land, about
100 acres, at what is now known as Mac-
donaldtown. He died there in 1830 at the
great age of 104 years. After his death a
servant named Rochford produced a will
"w hich left him the whole of the Devine
property. He promptly sold it and dis-
appeared. Some time after, Devine’s
relatives in Ireland heard of the transac-
tion and sent out an emissary to make
inquiries. The result was a law suit of
long-drawn-out proportions against the
holders of allotments who had built valu-
able houses thereon. After much expen-
sive legislation—the whole N.S. Wales
Bar (what there was of it) being engaged
—a compromise was effected.
Australia, in compensation for the
loss of its Danyell O’Connor to America,
is getting as an exchange visitor William
Jennings Bryan—a gentleman who is also
m the perfervid oratory line of business.
Bryan will be remembered as the man
who ran a serious election on the bi-
metallism insanity, and who, on the
occasion of his first nomination, was
carried round the hall by a crowd of
frenzied partisans till his braces gave way.
Anglican Bishop Clarke, of Melbourne,
having been promoted to be an Archbishop, makes
the second Archbishop of that city, the other
being R.C. prelate Carr. Dr. Carr, who was for
so long the only Archbishop, states now that he
objects to being called “R.C. Archbishop.” He
insists that he must be “ the Archbishop” ; letters
otherwise addressed he will take no notice of, even
if he has reason to suspect that there are’large
cheques in them.
Juliau Salomons is more or less resting
after his busy and brilliant N.S.W. Bar career, and
J. H. Want, as this is written, lies terribly ill
Add Wise, absent in England, and three “ top-
notchers ” are out of the running. The work goes
to the remaining “ silks’’—Pilcher, Gordon, R?M.
Sly, Reid, and Wade. There are rumors of other
aspirants for silk. Adrian Knox and Danger
Owen on the Equity “side,” and Wilfred Blacket
Garland, Shand, and Ralston, on the Common
Law “side,” are, it is said, about to apply for the
silk gown. Salomons is one of the few men in the
profession who, having accepted high judicial
onice, have afterwards returned to practice.
Another who had this experience was Martin, of
Maonland. Both rose to large practices on their
return from the Bench.
From the press gallery :
There are two political cranks in the Reps.-Joe
Cook and Conroy. Cook is a sneering-, whining sort of
person, who conceals his violence under an affectation of
polish and courtesy. When this affectation is brushed
aside the real tigerish instinct of the politician is
seen behind, snarling* and licking* its chops, with
glaring eyes and bristling whiskers. That was
what stood revealed in the House last week when the
Deputv-Speaker was forced to name Joe Cook. Beside
himself with passion, Cook shouted in tones that echoed
high above the turmoil and hubbub of the Chamber-
‘ Name me ! Name me ! Name away ! ” His friends
calmed him down ; but some day they won’t, and that day
Cook will eat the Speaker or Isaacs or Bigjohn Forrest or
BiH Lyne. Conroy’s frenzy is more apparent than
Joe Cooks, and he is at first glance a political hooligan.
.There is no polish about Conroy—no mock courtesy.
A prosy, stodgy speaker as a ruie, no one ever knows
when he is going to suddenly hurl some scathing gibe or
insult at some innocent member who happens to
meet his eye. Bast week in the House, the sight of
Dr. Oulpin disposing himself to sleep lashed Conroy into
ungovernable fury. His uproar made the windows trem-
bie and members shrink away from him. There are only
two men in the House who can stop Conroy when he
gets like that. One is Joe Cook—from a fellow feeliri'"-
doubtless—and the other is Pumpkin Smith. Conroy is
really fond of Pumpkin-a further proof of an ill-regulated
mind. Both Cook and Smith pulled Conroy down and
reduced him to sanity. Conroy will seize the mace one
Chamber 1 Speaker ’ and run amoli through the
MATE, INDEED.

A proposal to give Henniker Heaton a
public dinner finds favor in one or two quarters,
cind ii lot of disfavor in others. On a previous
visit to Sydney the same suggestion was sent
round, but was coldly received. However, there
was a little meal in Parliament House, and Hen-
mker happened to be there along with a small
mob of other people, and cables flashed the intelli-
gence to London Times that N.S.W. Parliament
had entertained the great Henniker at a public
dinner. Parliament hadn’t entertained Henniker
but that made no difference.
In reply to a correspondent’s demand for
further particulars The Bulletin’s oldest inhabi-
tant writes
The first Anthony Hordern established himself in King-
street East (Sydney) in 1823. He went to Melbourne in
the late thirties and bought original town lots which
became very valuable and which were sold in 1872 pursuant
to his will. • '
The second Anthony, in partnership with a brother
opened on Brickfield Hill, between Goulburn and
Liverpool-streets. The new building now covers the
spot ; they dissolved in 1853, Anthony going to the Hay-
market, the shop recently occupied by the Hordern
grocery. Anthony No. 2 died in 1877.
Anthony No. 3, partner with Samuel Hordern, died in
1886 on the coast of YVestralia.
. Anthony No. 4is now living. I believe each Anthony
is the eldest son of his father. I cannot find that
Anthony Hordern” at any time was on the site now
occupied by the A.J.S. Bank, as has been alleged. A son
John once had an establishment on the corner of
George-street and Charlotte-place—now Grosvenor-street.
Anthony No. 1 wasapparently never in business anywhere
except in King-street East.
Passed over recently at Adelaide, aged 85,
Isaac Puddy, joioneer railway maker. He was a
native of Somersetshire
(Eng.), and, after some
English railway experi-
ence, came to South Aus-
tralia in 1855. He super-
vised the construction of
the permanent way on S.
Australia’s first genuine
railway, from Adelaide to
the Port, opened on April
21,1856. Some other works
he had a hand in are the
Adelaide to Kapunda line,
Adelaide station yard, the
loop-line from Port Ade-
laide to Dry Creek, part of
the Kingston to Narra-
coorte line, the tramlines
from Adelaide to Kensing-
ton and from Adelaide to
North Adelaide, the mac-
adamised road up Mount
Osmond, and the Holdfast
Bay line. He was a com-
prehensive man, take him
for all in all.

“ Priam ” from Melbourne :
Pursey Chirnside was “invested” at the military
review in Albert Park (Melbourne) with the “ insignia of
the Companionship of the Order of St. Michael and St.
George.” VVhat happened was that Pursey C. (in frock
coat and hell topper) walked ouc to the Governor-General
received a box with the gaud in it, and walked away
again. There were 2000 troops and 10,000 citizens
present to see Pursey C. remove his box. What, sort of a
soul has the man who appears in front of a crowd like
that to receive the cheapest gaud in the empire !

A Maorilander, who is a somnambulist,
lately in his dreams fell from a hotel window on
to a corrugated iron roof 15ft. below, but only
incurred a few bruises. This is his eighth experi-
ence of the kind, and he is getting sick of it. One
night, some years ago, he went for a long walk in
his sleep, and on his return the noise made by a
big crowd awakened him. The pub. where he
lived had been burned down in his absence.
“ N.” about British royalties :
King- Edward dubs Louise of Fife Princess Royal. Dig a
little. The odds did not appear very long against her for
the throne at one time. Her husband comes down from
Countess Errol, daughter of William IV. and actress
Jordan. What a revenge by the Fates if Fife had become
—well, such King of England as he could be. William
IV., as Duke of Clarence, had 10 children by Mrs. Jordan
—the Fitzclarences. They were all sumptuously treated.
Yet poor Dora Jordan was treated most shabbily. What
strange obliquity it was of the clergy not to insist that
she was William’s wife. She had been faithful to him for
20 years. They lived at Bushy Park, Kew. The royal
cadger absorbed her very large earnings as principal
comic actress of Drury. To be sure, he contributed what
he could to the household, but to a great extent he
sponged on her. When Princess Charlotte, daughter of
the Prince Regent, died, the statesmen declared that
those dry old bucks Clarence, Kent, York, Cambridge,
and Sussex must wed nice young ladies. Heirs to
the throne were wanted. William parted from his Jordan.
He married Princess Adelaide, and had two miser-
able infants, daughters, both dying in infancy.
The most sickly thing at Windsor Castle used to be a
sculpture of one of those kids. Probably the King has
put it in the coal-hole. Dora Jordan got the announce-
ment of her virtual divorce from Clarence while playing
Nell in a farce called “The Devil to Pay.” The poor
woman burst into a passion of tears in the midst of a
comic scene. She afterwards broke through a proviso
that she must leave the stage, and this was cruelly made
an excuse for stopping- an allowance given her. Her
beftuty, youth, and attraction were gone. She perished in
miserable poverty in France. Doubtless a' clerical lesson
of retribution ! What a wretched, mean-spirited crew
were her 10 children, the Countess of Errol and the rest,
every one of them well provided for while their mother
starved. Fanny Kemble says in her autobiography that
one of the Fitzclarences, a perfect noodle, had a fat billet
as a clergyman. He was Rector of Mapledurham. He
wanted Fanny to write a sermon for him. She burst out
laughing, with, “ Well, this is rich, a clergyman coming
to an actress to write him a sermon ! ”

Melbourne Leader is publishing some
pioneering reminiscences compiled by F. M.
Snodgrass (Janet Mrs. Clarke’s brother) from his
grandfather’s letteis ’Ome. Mr. Snodgrass, an
amiable bachelor of the leisured class, has been
hawking his M.S. round Melbourne in pursuit of
a publisher for years past, and tells—in all inno-
cence—the ingenious excuses offered him by the
descendants of Barabbas to prevent their refusal
of his screed offending the wealthy Clarke clan.
Grandpa Cotton, the original chronicler, was a
wealthy nobody of the squatter tribe, and his
badly-written recollections relate mostly to the
small-beer matters in which- the squattocracy
delights.

Mates-o’-Mine.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When you’ve watched them from the picket,
Kickin’ goals or playin’ cricket,
And you’ve got your betting-ticket,
Mates-o’-mine,
Just a word into your ear :
Though smilin’ Peace is here,
It may be War next year,
Mates-o’-mine.
And what are we to do ?
In numbers we’re so few —
Ain’t it up to me and you,
Mates-o’-mine,
Though far from war’s alarms,
And sport has many charms,
To learn the use of arms,
Mates-o’-mine?
Ain’t it up to me and you
To spare an hour or two
To see our country through,
Mates-o’-mine ?
And learn to hit a mark,
And heed the sergeant’s bark,
When we’re drillin’ after dark,
Mates-o’-mine;
And flaunt no gaudy clothes,
No ribbon, braid, or bows,
And never look for foes,
Mates-o’-mine ?
But be a good relation,
Though prepared to fight creation,
And a credit to our nation,
Mates-o’-mine ?
N.S.W.
NULLA.
The Asiatic and the Aborigine.
On the question of the administration of abori-
ginal affairs in North Queensland Leonard M.
Cullen writes :
You seem to be excellently well satisfied with Dr.
Roth’s administration, remarking that “ some mean-
minded persons who desire to exploit the aborigines ” ob-
ject to Dr. Roth. I would take it as a great favor if you
would allow me to advertise myself in your columns as
one of those mean-minded persons. Your commentator
judges, of course, solely by Dr. Roth’s report. If Dr.
Roth were one-tenth part as clever at the work of ad-
ministration as he is at writing reports he would be a
paragon.
There are still large numbers of aborigines left in this
district (Cardwell to Cairns). Dr. Rotii, I believe, puts
the number at 2000, and of these the great majority earn
their living by prostituting their women. Opium is the
main incentive, as it is almost the sole payment. There
is not a month that numbers of them do not die as a con-
sequence, and never a word of inquiry or of comment by
Dr. Rotii. If Dr. Rotii had tried to put a stop to the
opium traffic with the Chinese and the wholesale prosti-
tution of their women by the blacks, one might blame the
lav* bqt as a fact he has never made the least attempt to
cope with the evil. The Chinese are not allowed by law
to employ or harbor the aborigines. As a matter of fact,
they are almost the sole employers and harborers in the
district. 1 myself am probably the largest white employer
of aborigines in this district, but 1 am quite aware I am
doing it in defiance of the written law.
Dr. Roth goes to Westralia to ferret out misdoings in
relation to aborigines. Let me tell you a story hap-
pening just recently in Dr. Roth’s own territory. A
black girl, born and bred here, is married to a boy and
gives birth to a child. Her boy takes to opium and
sells his gin to a Malay. Later he returns to get her.
She is working’ here, her child a few' months old, and
refuses to go. Charlie gashes her with a razor, leaving
her with a hole iii her side you could put your hand in,
and flees. My brother sews up the wound, and looks
after the woman till she recovers. The boy returns
and coaxes or forces the woman away. For a while he is
a pensioner on the bounty of the Malay, with plenty of
opium, and is quite happy. A little while, and calling
for his pension, he is confronted with a shot-gun. Now
he is a discon tented wanderer. Shortly, at a big cor-
roboree, I see the woman, and coax her to come and pick
coffee for me. She works a week, and then, becoming
opium sick, departs with her noble husband toGeraldton.
Here Charlie becomes again the petted pensioner of a
Chinaman, but shortly, as usual, gets sour looks and
presently a beating, being forced to depart without his
lubra! Goes to the police and complains. The police
come and take the woman from the Chinaman, but not to
return to her boy or her tribe. One of the black troopers
finds her comely in his eyes, and she becomes the
property of the police camp. Two months la.er and she
is cast out—diseased and w'orn out.
Here is another story. A girl born and bred here is
married to a boy, and goes on a visit to the coast. She
takes to opium, and remains with a Malay. She also has
a child, which she leaves here. Some time later, she re-
visits her old home, and I prevail upon her to stop and
pick coffee for me. A week or so, and she tells me she
must go down and bring up her blankets and clothes.
Her husband remains behind w'ith me. A week or so passes
and she does not return, the Malay having detained her.
Her husband says, “I will go and fetch her.” I say to
him, “ You be careful. Don’t you let the Malay know
you want to take her away, or, from what I hear of him,
something will happen you.” He goes, and two days
after I hear a mourning in the camp. On inquiry, I hear
that the husband is dead. Now, he was all right when he
left here. Why, then, did he die so suddenly ? Nobody
inquires and nobody cares. Dr. Rotii is probably lectur-
ing in Tasmania, or somewhere, on the methods employed
by the blacks in scraping potatoes.
Similar tales of murder, outrage, and abominations
generally are getting more and more frequent. I have
written to the Queensland press trying to arouse public
opinion, and with no effect. Now I appeal to you, as
editor of the most widely-circulated journal in Australia.
You have great privileges and great responsibilities. Do
your duty in arousing Australians to a sense of their shame
and disgrace in the treatment of the race whom they
have dispossessed of their country and then left to the
tender mercies of the Asiatic.

The Lack of Concentration.
“ J.” on manufactures and manufacturers :
A had feature of the fight against the Yankee dumpers
is that, like the struggle of the Saxons against the Danes,
it is not a national movement, but a series of isolated
efforts by individual trades and firms. The Australian
Harvester people are putting up a good fight, but they
are alone, and the fight, in the public eye, settles around
the making of stripping machines. And yet the woollen
trade is a bigger thing than the making of agricultural
machinery, and even the despised boot holds within
its various shapes many fortunes. At present the Aus-
tralian manufacturers are making first-class men’s boots ;
as I write this I wear a pair which were made in Woolloo-
mooloo, and at 18s. fid. are better value than the Yankee
article at 225. fid. But, as far as I know, there’s not an
Australian maker turning out a high-class woman’s boot
or shoe. A fair medium boot, costing about 155., is the
best our people will attempt, and all over the place you
meet women walking round in German, Austrian, Eng-
lish, and American boots and shoes. The manufacturers
are not organised, and if they were they would
be seriously handicapped as far as Sydney is con-
cerned for want of a daily, paper in which to
present the facts of the case to the public. And, so far,
the men who have started to make something are so
heart-sick with the struggle In the face of the inadequate
tariff that they will not try and show the people the
untold possibilities there are in industries at present non-
existent. Even the Harvester people do not agitate for a
really useful duty on reapers and binders. They seem to
be content if they can have the making of strippers and
ploughs r served to them. They ask for half a loaf, and
will probably have to be content with four ounces. “ Play
Big ! ” should be the motto of every Australian Protec-
tionist. The Freetraders do it, and ask for nothing less
than the wrecking of every Australian manufacturing
industry. Manufacturers cannot blame the public for
believing the cry of Pumpkin Smith and the bray of Con-
roy that our manufacturers are a degenerate lot
who cannot make anything, when they themselves act as
though it were true. I have asked several boot
manufacturers why they don’t make high-class women’s
boots, and been staggered to learn that “ the Australians
cannot make a really light boot to equal the Continental
or Yankee! ” Yet it is only a matter of the right machinery.
I have a friend who runs a bicycle factory, and he howls
for 30 per cent, on bicycles, 40 per cent, on motor-bikes,
and not less than 50 per cent, ad valorem on motor cars—
he also howls for the right to import his parts duty free.
He admits that we can make as good parts in Australia as
they can in Britain or America, but as parts are not in his
line, he thinks they ought to come in free. Deep down,
the fairy tales Oobd'en told his fathers linger still. In his
own business he has proved them fairy tales, for with the
exception of the famous Humber, there is not a foreign
bike can compare with the Australian-made machine. If
manufacturers would only cultivate a national feeling
and consider these questions from a national standpoint
they would be doing better for themselves than they are
under the crude individualism that they at present affect.
After all, there are points about acting the part of a
keeper to one’s brother when the brother will reciprocate
by dealing exclusively with his keeper.

The “ Imperialists.”
N.S.W. Agent-General Coghlan reports that
Australian securities are depreciated because of
the resentment of the Kaffir magnates at the
action of the Commonwealth on the question of
the introduction of Chinese labor into the Trans-
vaal. Amongst those who have interests in what
is called the “Kaffir Circus” there exists a violent
prejudice against things Australian, arising out of
what are considered to be Australian opinions and
actions on the question of the introduction and
employment of Chinese. “ The existence of the
prejudice is a substantial fact that greatly affects
Australian interests in this country.” It may be
as well that Australians should know who control
the “Kaffir Circus,” and are affecting the des-
tinies of their native land. This can best be
shown by taking the share-list of the “Trust”
lately formed by Wernher, Beit, Eckstein and
Co. for manipulating by share transactions the
“Kaffir Circus.” Originally a £2,000,000 Trust, it
has within the last few months been developed
into a £6.000,000 Trust. Here are the original
subscribers :

There is Herr and Monsieur, Abraham, and even
Pacha, but a small, very small, sprinkling of
British blood. These are the cosmopolitan
financiers who own the Transvaal, and Austra-

These are the men who are “ British Imperialists,”

Some experiences of a Maorilander in Britain
Twelve months ago I came to England, from Maori-
land, looking for work, and find it is a great country—to
keep out of. I have seen pretty well all there is to be
seen in England, Scotland, Ireland, and Wales, and the
experience all through has been had. If I were a mil-
lionaire to-morrow I . would gladly give half to be back
once more in Australasia, if I couldn’t get taken back for
less.
Of the forty odd millions that make up the population
here there are only two classes—those who give tips, and
those who take them. At the Hotel Cecil you have your
bath, one “ man ” brings up the towels and another the
soap (two tips). If you want a drink of water, another
tip. When you go out there is a major-general or field
marshal, who swings open the door with one hand, and
holds out the other for a tip. No matter where you go it
is just the same, and if a tip isn’t forthcoming you soon
find out things.
The misery on all hands is appalling. Here, in Scot-
land, and in Ireland, I have seen women and children
with a few rags to cover their bodies, without shoes,
or stockings, or even hats, getting about in the bitter
cold of winter, and mixing up with the other kind in
fur-lined overcoats, and taking everything as a matter
of course. I have seen more grinding poverty and real
want in one half-hour in Liverpool and Glasgow than
during 30 y ears’ experience of Australia, and those towns
are infinitely better off than London.
As for politics, the worker in these parts troubles
his head very little about them. His great thought is
Beer. There is no inducement for the average worker
to get a vote. “To hell with a vote !” says he, “give
me work, and after that beer.” This about sums up the
democratic position. There are a few good democrats
sitting in Westminster, but not a fiftieth part of what
there ought to be.
I do not think many years will pass over before England
finds itself in a difficulty as to suitable recruits for the
army. During my stay in Liverpool I used every day to
pay a visit to the landing stage—where the great liners
depart from. Every week I saw thousands of the very
bone and sinew of England leaving for Canada and
the U.S. When one thinks of this drain taking place
from other centres, Glasgow, Belfast, London, South-
ampton, &c., a pretty good idea can be formed that in
future years only the weeds and wastrels will be left to
fill up the ranks'of the British army.
+
It is suggested that the High Court should be
asked to definitely fix the boundary line between
Victoria and South Australia. In the early days
by Imperial enactment the 141st degree of east
longitude was declared the boundary, but nobody
seems to have been able to properly locate degree
141. A proclamation of 1847 said it was here, and
here it remained for some years; then somebody
said it wasn’t here, but there-about two miles
further west—so in 1894 another proclamation
agreed that it was there. Next, somebody else
opined that it wasn’t there at all, but somewhere
between here and there, and S.A. claimed from
Victoria territory mile by 242 miles in extent.
Matters were left so, “without prejudice to re-
adjustment.” Now it is urged that one of these
days a sharp Lawyer may make trouble with the
point that the territory, if any, between the
present and true boundaries is a sort of Australian
Alsatia—that it is really not in any State, con-
sequently not in the Commonwealth, and there-
fore there is no law to punish any person nns;con-
ducting himself on that territory.


A WOMAN’S LETTER.
_ Sydney, November 21, 1905.
My Dear Arini,—
Lord Jersey began his second time in Sydney
by shaking hands with the engine drivers and
other railway material at Redfern. Lady Jersey
is said by those who knew her in the past
to be much more tolerant, and softer-looking
Her slight greyness improves her appear-
ance ; and her brightness of eye and brainy
expression are still there. Professor Anderson
Stuart has harpooned the Jersey for a
stone-laying at Prince Alfred Hospital next
Thursday.. As the rock will be placed with “full
Masonic honors,” it will surely be allowed to stay
so. That laid at the P. A. H. by the Duke of
York, and declared by him to be well and truly
moored to the crust of the planet, was presently
hoisted up and put where it was wanted on quite
another patch of the Cosmos.
The wife and two ewe lambs of Mr. Francis
Suttor have returned from furrin parts, and one of
the lambs immediately achieved a fine crop of
measles. Mrs. “ Ted ” Simpson, one of Sydney’s
smartest and most conservative, returns by the
Macedonia this week. With her comes her daughter
who has been at school abroad. Sundry Milner
Stephens and Fitz-Wentworths returned by the
same liner. Also a Captain Caws, who has come
out to take the ex-Government yacht Victoria
back with him. One of the returned, speaking of
Melba, says that it is almost incredible the way
she bosses Covent Garden. Calve was to have
been in London last season, but Melba blocked
her and others who might impinge on her stellar-
ship. She doesn’t even make a pretence of keep-
ing faith with the public. Nights she is adver-
tised to sing prices are raised, and the house
rushed. But if Melba is not in the mood, she
cloesn t appear. The Coghlans are now living at
.Kensington Palace Mansions, and the Babylonians
say of C. : “ He is a Herculean man, and treadeth
heavily on the enemies of his country.”
Some bright-red veils braved the bustle and
breeze at Rosehill races on Saturday. Now that
scarlet has been recognised to be the cool color and
suitable for red-hot weather, we may expect often
to see the revolutionary hue floating from the
feminine masthead. Even clothing “should be
lined with red to break transmission of the sun’s
rays, say the scientists. Whatever may be the
hygienic advantages of scarlet, it is a hot-looking
color. By the way, there is now an artificial
blush. You inhale a drug—amyl nitrate—and
blush like an early Victorian girl. This blush
may become fixed by some other invention, or bv
practice. J

An Australienne who went away to visit a rich
married brother living in London, tells how she
found him residing in a bachelor fiat and his wife
living at a swell ladies’ club. Their only child
was at a boarding-school. Relations between
this pair were always most friendly, but he could
rarely see her except in the public drawing-room
of her club, and, though she frequently went to
lunch or dine at his flat, it was always under
elderly chaperonage. To the amazed Australienne
the wife once remarked : “ Your brother is so in-
discreet, he often asks me to come without auntie
or mother.” Asked how she spent her time, this
lily replied that it went all too fast. It took her
the whole forenoon, year in and year out, to work
at preserving her figure, complexion, hair and
general condition. And she had evidently suc-
ceeded, for she looked a beautiful twenty-year-old.
Yet her daughter is fourteen.
Other day, I picked up a Schopenhauer essay
and read that “for the most part a woman loses
her beauty after giving birth to one or two
children.” Argument is that, having caught a
mate and produced her kind, beauty is no longer
necessary, but often dangerous. The disagreeable
old philosopher was very much out, judging by the
appearance of many ladies who have quiverfuls, let
alone “oneor two.” Recall the much-read story “My
Official Wife.” In it a pretty girl of something
under 25, by means of another’s passport, passes
without further disguise as the lady whom the
Russian chief of police knows to be a grand-
mother. I thought when I read that, the writer
was drawing the long bow very taut. But by
“taking notice” several women with fair-sized
families of well-grown youngsters may be called
to mind as looking like young unmarried women.
Amongst our recent Somebody visitors was a beau-
tiful English woman who has nine children, eldest
23. In the crudest noonday light this lady
would pass for anything between 25 and 30. Her
figure has the slender outlines of youth, her skin
the texture and her eyes the brilliancy of girl-
hood. She is one of the busiest hostesses in Lon-
don, and spends no time on special efforts to keep
her youth. It just keeps itself, or remains. No
doubt such an enviable state is largely a matter of
superb health.

keeper in “ Merely Mary Ann ” is comically good

their ’eads off, ” and now she wears the Cardigan vest
of one of the late unlamented, and the silver chain
and watch of the other. The vest doesn’t fasten
across the lady’s opulent figure. Great is the gap
thereof and also the gaps suggested by the black-
ing out of a tooth or two. The Brisbane girl, Irby
Marshall, who is the landlady’s J\ly Rowsy,” has
been giving a good account of herself in her giggle-
some part.

From S.M.H. Woman’s London Letter .
One thing is certain ; all the fitting is ever so much
closer. The idea is for length of waist and a round,
slender figure. They sell corsets for the Louis XV.
* so “ l ® es > which, as you know, terminate in a sharp point,
well boned, and the figure has to be got into subjection to
suit the line of the corsag’e.
Amd here’s Mrs. Molyneux Parkes and others
advocating for defence purposes the use of fire-
arms by. all female citizens between the ages of 15
and 20,. just the age at which “the figure has to
be got into subjection ” to suit the ever-changing
fashion. &
baiTister man tells me that during the hearing
of the Dailey divorce suit the court is thronged
with women. To spare the blushes of the corseted
and whale-boned sex the judge prohibited publica-
tion of detailed evidence in the case. But there is
evidently a section of the feminine public that has
painted out its blush and which declines to be pro-
tected from the shocking revelations. A few of
them try to give the impression that they are there
on reporting business, and make voluminous
entries in note-books. At a local police court
lately, during the hearing of a certain case, the
presiding magistrate ordered women to leave the
court. They did so—save one. She, a young,
pretty, well-dressed personage, sat at the press
table and continued to transcribe evidence that no
paper in the land would publish. But some of
the innocent males in court pitied her intensely
and wondered what brute of an editor it was who
would send a girl to do such work. Well, the
curiosity of the primal female lost us that famous
Garden, and curiosity sends the modern female to
listen at divorce court proceedings. “Nibbling at
the green apples on the shady side of the Tree,”
as someone puts it. The stockings of the women
who spend their time so are almost certain to need
mending.

One Virgil, not he who wrote of Arms and the
Man, but the inventor of the silent practice Clavier
system, is to arrive in Australia soon to lecture on
the royal road to pianoforting. There must be
something in it, for Mr. Edwardeg, examiner for
the Royal College of Music, says “ nothing makes
me so sure-footed on the piano as the Clavier
system.”
Mrs. Hickson, wife of one of the Commissioners
of the Harbor Trust and mother of Mrs. “Ted ”
Merewether, just missed death by a hair’s breadth,
the other day, when a bright blue signboard, about
twelve or fourteen feet long, fell on her in George-
street. There was a mighty wind blowing, and
the article dropped from a considerable height, yet
the lady escaped with a fractured leg.
Sophie Osmond, a perseveringly battlesome
presswoman, formerly of Melbourne but now in
London, is writing a series of short stories in
Sketchy Bits. “Jinks, J.P.,” the series is
named and the hero is Australian. One of the
owners of S. Bits asked her for a hero “ who could
bushrange, and detect crime, and carry his swag,
and get in and out of gaol and other tight places
with ease.’’ The article is being supplied. Aus-
tralia is still to the British mind the place where
the bushrangers come from, and the fact that the
bushranger is gone and the land-agent has arrived
hasn t yet penetrated the fog of .London.
The law must be a fearsome thing in Westralia.
Recently, when a maddened cow wrecked a
drapery shop, and came away with the new
season s fashions festooned on its horns, trod on a
young woman, and nearly killed two clergymen,
the police went away to get legal advice as to
their right to shoot at the animal. They saw the
Attorney-General about it, and he looked up
dusty precedents while the cow galloped round
generally. 1 inally the intricate legal point was
cleared up, and the cow perished about sunset.
A feature that will stand out like Wilson’s Pro-
montory, at next Saturday’s Jewish Benefit Con-
cert -bund for Russian Jews, will be the singing
by Mrs. Francis Cohen, the new Rabbi’s wife of
a Hebrew melody—“ O, Weep for Those.” Words
are Lord Byron’s, but the air is traditional. The
lady has a wonderfully sweet and highly-trained
mezzo-soprano of light quality.
The Maheno (named after a Maoriland lake)
the new turbine steamer, first of its kind in
Sydney, is a beautifully dyed, and “dressed ” and
painted steamer—olive green outside, with an
orange frieze and the well-known sealing wax
red funnels affected by the Union S.S. Co. The
vessel held a great reception on Friday night
m honor of its maiden trip, and left for Welling-
ton on Saturday with 400 passengers. Mr and
Mrs. David Mills hosted the festival. Mrs. Mills
was an Auckland belle of the past days, small and
blonde, and still keeps much of her charm. The
three Mills brothers went in for beauty all the
time. The Maheno did the trip from Melbourne
to Sydney in 29 hours, and the usual rapid run of
other craft, mail-liners included, is 44 hours.
Yours Affectionately,

THE CROWNING CHARM
Of a pretty woman is—not hair, not eyes, not
features, not figure, but a good complexion. “ Valaze ”
is the great creator and preserver of good complexions.
It removes wrinkles, freckles, tan, blackheads, etc., and
substitutes a fair, soft, smooth skin that is the delight
of its fortunate possessor, and the charm of all beholders.
“ Valaze ” is scientifically prepared from herbs grown in
the Carpathian Mountains. It is guaranteed to be non-
hair producing, bringing nothing but beauty in its train.
Price, 3s. 6d. and 65., posted fid. extra. New importations
from Vienna. Comtesse Potocca’s “ Voskpasta,” to be used
with “ Valaze ” Herbal Soap, 3s. per jar. A delightful

THE VARNISHED BOARD
MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, November 20 1905
Mr Dear Yarrie, —
A moneyed bride relates how thickly her dn
mestic path was strewn with difficulties when she'
started at the head of a mansion. Day after dav
cook was summoned for consultation, and fresh
• dishes were marked down to take hubby’s fancy
1 here seems little variety in meat,” complained
the amuous missus one morning. ‘ ■ It’s the fault
ot the beasts, was the laconic reply of cook
‘ JNonsense,” rejoined the missus; “I’ll seethe
butcher myself.” She did in her frothiest morn-
mg rig. Please state what meats there are”
commanded the apparition. The dazed deputy
ot the bull and sheep business did. When he
stopped, the vision in the soft muslin frills pined
for more variety of animal, and asked disgustedly
Is that all?” Blue apron thought for a while’
and then added, “Well, ma’am, the boss was
thinking of introducing a- little kangaroo and goat
flesh. Now when the bride tells the yarn as an
instance of domestic trials people guffaw, and she
can’t make out why. It seems cruel to her that
Nature doesn’t sympathise with the trials of a
young wife by adding to the number of edible
animals, though after all it is the fault of man
and woman in declining to eat the animals there
are. The streets are full of horses and solicitors
and such like.
A foreign Baron (another of ’em) is starring at a
very swagger hostelry. He wears a moustache in
the German pin-sticking fashion and toreador
vests. He also boasts a look of frequent tubbing.
“Such a clean man !” is a remark one hears with
frequency. One doesn’t know why. The baron
in question landed in ’roo-land apparently poor,
but, after a little time, money as up-to-date manna
fell from Heaven. Just now the local girls are
devotedly his. j •
Crochet work in an hotel lounge is the most
virtuous occupation a civilised city yields.
During the rush and whirl of the Horse Week
revels, one Toorak blonde, who had pegged out a
perch in town for the grand hustle of the year,
used to take up a cosy corner seat beneath the
shade of the sheltering winter-garden palm before
lunch, and ply her needles with the zeal of
Penelope knitting for the price of her next meal.
Ihe hotel johnnies, rather disgusted at this
industry in the first place, afterwards fell to
thinking that the blonde looked real cute at her
work, and, as they circled around, found much to
admire in the play of her most elaborately polished
fingers. If this industry was a pose, it was a most
successful one.
The most conspicuously-caparisoned Sydneyite
whirling here Cup-time was a showy dame who
made a point of displaying her lingerie and cami-
sole ribbons to every advantage through her fresh-
air yokes. Fellow-lodgers at her hostelry learnt
the whole tale of the under-wardrobe by heart
through the dame’s scheme of wearing transparent
or very open trellis bodices. Every day the cami-
sole, with its large ribbon bows, was changed
while the dress wasn’t, which showed that, in this
case, most importance was attached to what the
French delicately call “ the dessous.”
MY PIEBALD CO[?]NTRY.
The Acting'-Consul-General for Japan and Mrs. Iwasaki
gave a garden party yesterday afternoon at their resi-
dence, “ Ivanhoe,” Croydon, and entertained a number
of prominent citizens. The guests were welcomed on the
lower lawn by their host and hostess. Mr. Iwasaki wore
the Japanese national full dress. It consisted of what
looked like a pleated petticoat of brown silk. Over this
was worn a black kimona and blue waistcoat; on the
kiinona were embroidered the family coat-of-arms on
white buttons. The costume is always completed by a
tall silk hat —the British hat. — Sydney Telegraph.
“ Banzai! God save Bruce Smith! You can’t
beat the boys of the Geisha breed.”
The surgeon O’Haras unfurled the festive bunt-
ing last Wednesday. Mrs. O’Hara was in a quiet
sea green, and her crown-piece ran to a stretch of
foamy feathers. All the O’Hara bairns, in short
frocks and long legs, ran blithely among the
gathering. Miss Ruby Madden looked plump
and severe in a penitence garb of blue zephyr,
while her mama frolicked in a giddy muslin
a-sprawl with pink cabbage roses. Mrs. H.
Butler’s wonderful raiment also ran to a sprawl of
pink roses. The two M'Evoy dames and Mrs.
Norman Menzies were an aloof bunch of theatri-
cals. Mrs. Bland Holt and Miss Nannie Bennet
buzzed among the throng. The grandmothers of
the gathering struggled to get at a fortune-teller
who was part of the amusement. Girleens fresh
out of the college shell gazed in amazement at the
old dames. ‘ ‘ Haven’t they had their fortune ? ”
queried one petulant Miss. Probably the edddy


granny who rushes to the Sibyl doesn’t want to
know about a next husband, but about the size
and quality of her next world harp, and the color
of her future halo.
Rupert Clarke had word, Steeplechase morn,
that he’d have to get to the Cold Country. Three
days later he was aboard the China with quite a
family party—Miss Vera Clarke and the Reggie
pair.
There was no encouragement to sit out at the
G.-G. ball. The fountain court, which usually
lends itself so readily to the tete-a-tete in private,
was loudly illuminated, and chairs, busi-
ness-like chairs, were severely placed around
so that couples were in full view of each other and
the promenaders. That isn’t the way to help on
engagements in high life. It would be so toney
to say “Algernon proposed to me at the Govern-
ment House ball.”
One dainty Mdlle. wore the white of the newly-
fledged at the North cote ball. This was the
daughter of Madame He Bavay, a Frenchwoman
of some account in a set that rather prides itself
on knowing who’s who.
The shrimp-complexioned Stonington caddie
hies back to the Big Smoke in 1906. The Buchanan
leave being up, the Innocent Abroad ships him-
self back again.
The Bowes Kellys, after causing the birth-rate
to go up one recently, led the cake-walk at the
Australian Club hop. The G.-G. and his dame
changed partners with the Kellys, and the quartet
vis-a-vised at the head of the whole wool con-
tingent.
The drollest incident in connection with that
Australian Club ball is related by a Bananalander,
who had marked down the fizzy fling as the one treat
of the clothes-horse week. _ In order to feel fit for
the affair the visitor, a resident at the club, took
a nap after dinner. He awoke subsequently to
hear two voices murmuring spiced somethings to
each other in the corridor beyond his crib. He
hearkened as in a dream. “ I slipped Bred up for
two waltzes. Isn’t that shocking?” cooed one.
( J^ n me up, so it’s all right,” mur-
mured the other. The man grasped the fact that
the hop was in full swing if a flutterer had had
time to cheat a johnnie of two whirls. He rushed
™ one demented for a light and the time-piece.
Ihe dial pointed to a quarter after midnight, and
he solemnly cursed all after-dinner naps and settled
down angrily to snore again.
The most fetching of the young things showing
forth m snowy frills at the Australian Club hop
was Miss Olive Atherton (for whom you have not
to acquire a taste as most have to for olives).
She s a tall, shapely young Miss with small clever
features ; she has also a handsome sister who has
just come from a gad abroad. Mr. and Mrs. Tom
Atherton have now two dashing girls “ out.”
The Rev. Evelyn Snodgrass was the one spec-
tacled divine to look in at all the balls of most
account this carnival time. The unctuous shep-
herd spent a busy time at the Northcote fling
talkingyto the wallflowers, of whom there were
many.
The M‘Eacharns and the Gillotts have much to
answer for. The expensive style in which they
carried through their Mayoral terms has put the
fear of a poor or shabby front in the hearts of their
successors. The Strongs had their work cut out
to make ends meet. The Pleasance couple went
to greater extremes. And now the Weedons—a
couple not given to any dash or glitter—are seek-
lng to keep up the gaud Mayor end of the stick.
Already the pair have invested in a carriage, and
other extravagances are due.
Dame Forrest has to be applauded now and
then for her downright bold way of doing things.
Most fashionables, dumping their silver on a nag,
seek a bookie in a secluded corner for the bet, but
Bigjohn Forrest’s missus defies the whole popu-
lace. She thought of F.J. A. as the lucky tip of
' Sfceeplechase card. So, accompanied by the
cheild, she gathered up her skirts and faced a
bellower of the odds right in the gaze of Terrace
and Stand, and questioned his price. It suited
and the pencil-agent scribbled the odds, and Dame
Forrest made an umbrella and fan and glass stand
of the. cheild, while she dug into an omnium
gathenum 1 ag of puffs, pencils, cards, ball tickets,
tram passes and what not, for the silver.
The Judge Molesworths have bestowed on all
their girls Tennysonian labels. One of them, who
made her miniature presentation bob at- the
Northcote dance last week, is named Lynette.
ohe doesn t look it. Isabel or Florence would
have suited better. An elder sister (Elaine), now
tripping abroad, is likewise labelled unsuitably
Elsie would be much nearer the mark. It’s a
common thing to brand the babe that turns out a
missionary sister as Belle or Daisy, while the
infant that develops into a siren is called Angela.
The Lowther Clarkes have scattered pasteboards
r? r l awn party at Bishopscourt to meet the
G.-G. on Friday next. Once a year the Bishop
Clarkes break into a lawn revel at which society
chicks collide with the gaiters that are above
reproach, and think they’re in the way of grace
by the act.
The Carty Salmons have had Mrs. Harris, of
Sydney, here for the races. The brisk'Sydney
ex-mayoress put ’em all on for the functions of
most consequence, and figured in brocaded rig at
everything. She kept up the pace of a youngster,
and her high spirits never fell more than five
degrees at the most damping shivoo. Melbourne
has improved Mrs. Carty Salmon. That cheerful
young dame wore smart and tasteful raiment all
through the revels.
The Sydney Mayoral Taylors entered the lists
as entertainers while frisking at the Carnival here.
He gave two Grand Hotel dinners to his municipal
and other mates.
Mrs. Charlie Fairbairn rallied the damp wet
wmd-tossed Golfers to a gamble and evening snack
at Menzies’ the last Saturday in October. Pit was
the main diversion, and the strong-lunged sister-
hood, used to exercising fortissimo chords on the
cows that interrupt the range of the golf ball, went
strong and lustily at the noisiest game in this
strenuous parlor age. Miss Muriel Cumming
amassed more corners in chaff and oats than any
of the other vociferous criers and walked off with
the silver trophy. Mrs. Fairbairn by this Menzies
splash has distanced all the other golfing dames,
whose hospitalities rarely reach beyond a toasted
muffin and a tinned song.
A very charming dame, housed in a fashionable
hotel, relates pathetically how she had to give up
her pretty private suite furnished in Morris green.
It wasn’t economy, but she found that the lonely
males of the hotel made it their practice to im-
plore an invitation to join her in the green suite
for tea. Good-naturedly she yielded a few times.
Then more males came begging for tea with two
lumps of sympathy in it. But the dame was of
highly proper notions, and honestly had a pre-
ference for her husband’s society, so she decided
to gently decline the role of comforter in her own
green suite, but found it such a task invent-
ing excuses and administering the snub gently
that she gave up the green suite. Now the dis-
consolate male has to be content with a word in
the lounge or a nod in the dining-hall, and sadly
misses the comforts of that afternoon chat in the
snuggery. “I found,” says the dame, with a
pathos that has an undertone of satisfaction,
“ that I couldn’t keep the green suite and my
reputation at the same time. So the suite went.”
. An impudent young pinch-nosed eligible, who
indulges a penchant for attractive dames,
has run foul of two indignant husbands.,
Now, when he meets an attractive young madam,
he anxiously asks what sort of brute the spouse
is. “Does he look troublesome?” asks the
anaemic Lothario., Some day a large hulking
heavy-weight of'a husband will crush the life out
of the interloper, with one extra pressure of his
business fist. Nothing less, it seems, will teach
him not to peck at the prohibited apple. ‘ •

The servant trouble increases! The most up-to-
date Suzette inquires, after learning that there is
a satisfactory number of males in the family, how
many windows there are to clean ? There is a
story of a prominent, though rather poverty-
stricken, dame in a suburb that, in order to keep
her maid-of-all-work in good humor, she herself
polishes up 18 windows every Sabbath eve.
Mrs. Albert Miller entertained at Whernside on
1 uesday. A Conservatorium brand of music was
turned on, that magnificent cherub, Mr. Hal
Cohen, and Miss Ruby Grey contributing. The
silver-topped hostess wore blue-spotted chiffons
and a hat with nodding black plumes.
The foreign lover is an inflammable brand. One
of him has got himself tangled up in the affec-
tions of a plump, dressy dame, whose husband is
one of those troublesome persons who insist on
the public exposure of an insult. Things have
reached a painful pass, and the onlookers are in a
fever of excitement.
Mr. Sedgwick, a large swagger visitor with
tender feet, has scattered a few P.P.C. cards
around the Yarra-scented suburbs. Some elderly
dames are a squire the less. An old military swell
with a family tree packed away in his kit is such
a diversion for the old fry.
. Miss Lvelyn Scott contemplates an early plunge
into matrimony. For once friction in the well-
organised team of Williamson and Co. led to a
desertion or two. Miss Evelyn Scott, with fresh
plans in her mind, seceded from the ranks, and
purposes wedding her musical fiance before the
A mas cards are delivered. She will probably
.lom-uhe Jackson troupe and be the chief star
of their fraternal turn. The cheery mummer
made her start in stage life with Rickards and
would feel at home in returning to vaudeville.
A certain foreign Consul got through his levee
salute all right this year. In 1903 he started off
m brave uniform and reached the vice-regal
mansion intact. In descending from his cab some
strain told and his most responsible garment was
rent in twain. He dived back into the brougham,
gave the order for homeland got there as quickly
as possible. But the torn article couldn’t be
brought together again, and an apology had to
represent him that year. This year he had a
fresh outfit and got through all right.
Miss Alison Fairbairn gathered all her single
friends together on the Friday of Cup week for a
ply farewell to Miss Yera Clarke. The good-
bye tear dripped into the Dresden cup at Dun-
raven.

spermgsofthe mayoral term to the breezy beach

The old cl o’ sales have commenced. Theygene-
rally get brisk after a Cup, when the bills pile up
and last season’s chiffons are crowded out. One
loorak charmer, who prepares to flit to the Big
Smoke once more, relates sobfully how she ripped
the silk lining out of her skirts, then spread them
in a heap before the stale clothes garnerer. He
turned them over with the point of his boot and
with his nose m the air, finally offered, with
an air of ill-conceived disgust, “ Ten bob ’’—that
was the way he put it—for four frilly rigs that had
been chronicled through the season with gorgeous
epithets by the social scribes. She tools: the
miserable sum with a snort. Since then, she has
passed the word to her practical pals to leave the
suk linings, in, and do a deal with any ugly old
witch rather than with the man broker.

THE SPIRITS.
The Mikado has attended one of the national shrines,
and reported a successful conclusion of the war to the
spirits of his ancestors. —Press Item.
There is an unreliable rumor in Melbourne that
Dr. Maloney, M.11.R., may take proceedings
against Conroy, M.11.R., for wilful injury to his
bedding during the recent Federal stonewalling
debate. Dr. Maloney had brought in a couple of
pillows and blankets and proceeded to retire to
rest on the back Ministerial bench, but just then
an adjournment division happened, the Opposi-
tion crossed over, and Conroy seized the haber-
dashery and threw it violently out. The Mel-
bourne representative rescued his goods, and once
more went to sleep, this time more in the eye of
the Opposition than ever, on the second bench.
But it is alleged that one of the pillows was
frayed at the edge through Mr. Conroy’s violence,
and if he doesn’t pay for the damage trouble may
ensue.
Mrs. Maesmore Morris is off to the Land of
Fogs this week. She takes her small boy abroad
and puts him to school. For the past week or
two she has been the guest of the Morris family at
Elsternwick. It’s quite unique to find the dis-
carded spouse blamed by his family and their
whole sympathy going out to the divorcing wife.
Miss Queenie Cadden exhibits and demonstrates
her top 0 (finished in Europe) to the concert-goer
in hot December. The Victorienne has been
giving sample songlets all over the Cold Country
for a year or two.
Miss Amy Willard gives the local golfer a hint
how to be beautiful though a sport in the present
“ Princess mellowdrama. She comes into the
rustic scene in a trim cream opera rig and a French
sailor hat with a sportive quill pointing to the
setting sun. It’s the neatest rig on the neatest
figure ever imagined on the golf links. Evidently
there’s no dust and no dirt and no rain and no
cows on the English links, for Miss Willard after
encountering (in her own words) several severe
bunkers and hazards, arrives as fresh as paint to
the public view.

TRYPHENA.
That pathetic old ass, M‘Lean of Victoria,
doing a weep over the proposed standing orders
for the prevention of obstruction, said “ there was
a danger that Parliament might develop into a
tyrannical instrument of oppression.” The Par-
liament is elected by the whole adult population,
so Mac is probably afraid the people, through
their Parliament, will shamefully usurp their own
authority, and load themselves with chains, and
become such a burden upon themselves that they
will be afraid to speak for fear they should over-
hear themselves and consign themselves to a
dungeon. All this because it is proposed that the
people’s representatives should have power to pre-
vent M‘Lean’s friend Johnson talking to them
about nothing for four hours at a stretch.
It is generally thought that there is no
cure for stoutness, because the usual treatments
only confine themselves to combating single
symptoms instead of attacking the root of the
disease. Pyrolene Pellets remove not only the
superfluous fat, but also the internal deposits of
fat which hinder the action of the heart and lungs.
We advise a trial. Pyrolene Pellets improve the
breathing, give the heart freedom, and enable the
lungs to expand naturally. Sent free by post to
any part of the States on "receipt of Postal Note
for 3s. 9d. or 135., according to size required.
Arnold s Balsam Co., 364 a Little Collins-street,
Melbourne.*
Vigilans : The Northern Territory has
tried Chinese, and finds that they won’t do. A
man said to me the other day : “ Chinese labor is
not cheap labor ; the labor we want is Hindu
coolie labor. ’ “What do you propose to pay
them. I asked. “Eight pounds a year,” was
i the reply. Note the request— cheap labor. They
! don t pretend up here (N.T. ) that a sober, cleaii-
living white man can’t stand the climate. It is
only in the south that you get the hypocrisy about
labor that can stand the climate.” It is cheap
labor they are all after ; and when they’ve thought
,d it they have found it wasn’t cheap.
1 lie only way is to bind the labor before it arrives
And that means slavery.

Again the ter-
rorism of the
union. One re-
cent morning the
auctioneer at the
Sydney pig sales,
delighted at the
unusually large
muster of buyers,
put up the first lot,
descanted on the
worth of the pig and
the virtues of his an-
cestors, and then—dead
silence. The pig-buyers
had struck work, but were
all there to watch for
blacklegs. It-appears that
the law lays down the rule
that pigs shall not be driven
along the streets of the city
after 6 a.m., and as the
buyers had been flagrantly
and ostentatiously chivying their
bacon and ham in a live condition
through the public highways after that
hour, they were told that steps might pos-
sibly be taken to enforce the statute. Whereupon
the union of dealers damned the law, dropped its
tools and struck. And the Government in this
crisis ? No cries for free laborers, no Gatling guns,
no anything. Merely a polite intimation to the
strikers that they needn’t bother about any laws
unless they chose. You see, pig-raising is one of
our great natural resources.
Resolution recently passed by Sea Lake
(Vic.) Farmers’ Association :
That the Commonwealth Government be requested to
impose no duty on harvesters until the manufacturers
have reduced the price to £6O.
In other words, the Sea Lake farmers would
refuse the Australian manufacturer any Protec-
tion until he offers his machines at £lO less than
the present price of American machines —which is
a “dumping” price designed to knock out the
Australian manufacturer. Proof that it is knock-
ing him out comes in the recent discharge of 130
workmen by the Australian Sunshine Harvester
Co., because of the competition of the £7O im-
ported machine. It would serve the Sea Lake
farmers right if Parliament took them at their
word ; then, when the American trust had wiped
out the Australian industry, they would have to
pay for their harvesters anything over £lOO, as
farmers do in Argentina, where there is no local
manufacturer. It never occurs to the Sea Lake
farmers, by the way, to suggest that the Ameri-
cans should not be allowed to send any harvesters
to Australia till they are willing to sell at £6O.

POLITICAL
POINTS
If the Argus, and Sydney Morning
Herald, and the Daily Telegraph, and Bruce
Smith, and John Stinson were in Russia just
now, they would, of course, thank God that that
country isn’t cursed with arbitration laws like
New South Wales.
Sydney Telegraph now begins to write
letters to itself—at least they read as if perpetrated
by the same fine Roman fist which does the lead-
ing articles—from dispassionate but indignant
citizens with straws in their hair, demanding that
Sydney shall be the Federal capital whether the
Constitution allows it or not.
Apparently the Freetrade party hates the
Australian flag as much as it hates Australian
industries. It transpires that the Reid Govern-
ment informed the Admiralty, in reply to a
dispatch, that it didn't want the Australian flag
to ny on the so-called Australian Squadron. It
didn’t want to see the darned thing anywhere.
A protest from Adelaide :
Sydney legislators, State and Federal, are incessantly
vapping about N.S.W. being so great and prosperous that
it practically supports the other units of the Common-
wealth. Yet with the next breath they wail like bankrupt
bottle merchants if the Australian Government proposes
to spend 9d. outside of New South Wales. One would think

THE BLESSINGS OF BRITISH RULE!
The roll-call of Chinese slaves at the S.J.P.M.L. proper to, Johannesburg.

Possibly the “free breakfast table” gag
that Maoriland Premier Seek!on has started, with
a view to the election, is simply his gentle, astute
reply to the truculent Farmers’ Union. The
bucolic Union, which is mostly anti-Seddon, has
been bellowing a good deal lately about the
blessings of Freetrade in agricultural machinery
an 1 the like—things it uses but doesn’t make.
Seddon’s bland offer of Freetrade in the things
the Farmers’ Union makes is likely to set that
body roaring like a scalded lion.
Last week’s stupendous stonewall in the
House of Representatives began, as usual, because
the Deakin Government tried to get some work
done. The Opposition offered to let a certain
number of clauses of the Trade Marks Bill go
through in one night. The Government, which
is constantly taunted with not making progress,
wanted to get a larger number of clauses through,
whereupon the Opposition minority gave notice
that it would talk against time and prevent the
majority getting anything through.
Melbourne Trades Hall Council is discuss-
ing a proposal by Mr. Tom Mann for an Aus-
tralian six-hours working day “to solve the
unemployed problem.” This is the fool idea with
which Mr. Mann started his mischievous course in
Sydney. Why doesn’t the dear old Mother
Country keep its rabbits and thistles and Tom
Manns?
J. Lesina, of the “ extreme ” section of
the Queensland Labor party, fathers the (probably
false) yarn that both Kidston and Morgan, the two
men who are just now dragging Queensland out of
the mud, are iooking for soft billets. Lesina never
did anything for Australia but wag a fluent and
often foolish jaw. Probably he can’t understand
men ivorking for their country. Possibly he can t
understand men, real men, at all.
Labor-member Thrower has a question on
the notice-paper of N.S.W. Assembly as to
whether the new furniture for Parliament House
Library is being made by prison labor; and will
the Government give the work to free citizens ?
The idea that it is a good thing for the free citizen
to pay out of his earnings to keep the erring gaoled
citizen in idleness is one of those quaint super*
stitions that, it seems, will never die.
George Reid’s telegram to Joe Cook re the
rotten stonewall was a lovely tribute to the purity
and goodness of the bygone Reid-Deakin truce.
He wired Joseph as follows: “There is one great
benefit to be derived from present situation.
Deakin’s unhappy fall from lofty policy to abject
pandering is now apparent to all Australia.
What time Deakin lay low for Reid’s con-
venience his policy was “lofty.” When he walked
into Reid’s place he “ fell unhappily.”
Iron grave railings are now so freely im-
ported into Victoria that the local makers are
ceasing to manufacture.
Gnat Levi, one of the old Vic. House of 48
Rich Landlords, and a bitter opponent of the
Factories Act, has written a piteous letter to the
Melbourne press about the position of the Jews in
Russia. Yet this same Levi has all his life been
foremost in blocking every attempt of the same
movement which is trying to aid the Jews in
Russia to-day. One of the worst points about the
Jews is that they are ready to participate in all
the advantages for which the Democracy fights,
and yet, the moment they are emancipated, turn
round to kick the ladder by which they rose.
Had it not been for the movement of which latter-
day Socialism is the representative, the Jews in
English-speaking countries would to-day be treated
as they are in Russia, and the Rats’ Push would
probably be toying with Levi’s scalp in Bourke-
street at the moment of writing.
Shortly before last N.S.W. State elections
The Bulletin sent Dr. Arthur. M.P., a circular
asking him the following questions:—
1. Are you in favor of an absolute cessation of borrow-
ing, except- for renewal purposes— i.e., are you against
any increase of the public debt?
2. Are you in favor of establishing a sinking fund for
the repayment of existing debts?
3. Are you in favor of setting aside an adequate sum
out of revenue for the maintenance of public works and
the construction of necessary new works ?
The Bulletin got this reply from Dr. Arthur :
The delay in answering your letter was due to having
to give time to the consideration of such revolutionary
(in Australia at least) proposals. However, after shiver-
ing on the brink for a time I have plunged in. I have
made your questions the basis for part of my two last
addresses, and have bravely answered “ yes ” to them all.
The only exception I might make would be re borrowing
under.a compulsory settlement scheme, and then I would
have all the moneys received from the sale of land so
safeguarded that no Treasurer could touch them. I
would prefer to see taxation increased to enable us to pay
our way, and construct our own public works out of
revenue.
Dr. Arthur is again out on the brink—making all
speed away from the Pool of Healing, and looking
nohe the better for making his dip into political
honesty such a transient one.
A Norwegian and two Manila men have
just been convicted in Westralia of enticing a
commercial traveller, who was known o _
money about him, on board a boat under the pre-
tence of selling him pearls, and then murdering
him in a horribly deliberate manner, lo quote
the Westralian correspondent of an eastern paper :
“This adds another to the already long list ot
serious crimes committed in this State by aliens.
Murders by Britishers and Australians are almost
unknown.”
An example of N.S.W. State financing.
In a great flurry of enthusiasm the Government,
on August, 1904, paid £2082 for Milson Island
(Hawkesbury Ri /er) with the intention of build-
ing there an Inebriate Asylum. The hurry was so
great that there was no time to give full con-
sideration to other sites offering. Since then not
one stroke of work has been done towards build-
ing the asylum, and the drunk is just fined ss. as
usual. The money was, of course, loan money
and is supposed to be permanent and reproduc-
tive.
The N.S.W, Assembly had a day out last
Week. It knocked £l5O off the salary of the 1 re-
sident of the Legislative Council, £7O off the
Council’s Chairman of Committees, £9O oft the
Clerk of the Parliaments, £5O off the Clerk Assist-
ant, £4O off the Usher of the Black Rod, £2OO off
the Speaker of the Assembly, £4O off the
Assembly’s Chairman of Committees, £lOO oft the
Clerk of Assembly, £5O off his assistant, £34 oft
his second assistant, £33 off the Sergeant-at-Arms,
and £26 off the Clerk ot Records. But it didn t
knock anything off the Public Debt.
The folly and injustice of the system
whereby N.S.W. Parliament deals with the
estimates at the end of the calendar year, while
the financial year starts on July 1, is shown m the
cases of those public servants who have suddenly
lost, in some cases, £lOO a year. Their deductions
all date back to July 1, so that not only do they
lose part of their future salary, but they have to
refund back salary which they have probably
spent. And all because the Government is too
busy being coarse and uncivil to the I ederal
Parliament to keep its work up to date.
Who would think, considering the blood-
shot fury of the Cabbage-Tree Mob in the Repre-
sentatives over the proposed new Standing Order
to diminish obstruction, that it is merely an
adoption, in a modified form, of the system which
the British House of Commons has used for
years, and which exists in every State Legislature
in Australia? Anyone listening to the howls of
these undisciplined small boys, Kelly and John-
son, would imagine that it was a cannibal Standing
Order from some Parliament in the sands at the
back of Timbuctoo.
In order to lend support to the mock pro-
test of the Opposition against the proposal that
there should be in the Australian Parliament what
there is in almost every other Parliament the
closure—-Yes-No Reid haughtily handed to the
Speaker his resignation from the Standing Orders
Committee. As the fatted personage only attended
one meeting of the committee in five years, what
does it matter whether he resigns or not ?
Merely for record purposes, and inciden-
tally perhaps to illustrate the methods of a
Government that is a whale for reform, a return
should be called for of the fees paid to barristers
and solicitors since Joseph became Premier of
N.S.W. Lyne and See were both called upon to
supply information of this sort as regards their
Premierships. From 1899 to 1904 some £34,000
was thus dispensed, and if rumor may be trusted
the Joseph Government is no less bountiful in
legal tips. The return should, as in former cases,
specify names and dates.
Private Enterprise is quite willing and
able to do all that is necessary in supplying land
for closer settlement, says the N.S.W. Tory
crowd. How Private Enterprise proposes to do
it was shown up at Bungalow, on the North
Coast, the other day. An estate, cut up into
30 farms, was offered to 400 people, representing
nearly all the States, and not one farm was sold,
though as much as £24105. per acre was offered for
improved land and £l7 for scrub land. Go on the
scrub, young man—scrub at £2O an acre, and
without anything in the way of a State Land
Bank to help you through !
“ Itan ” writes :
The great majority of the Labor members in the
Bananaland Assembly freely admit that the time lias
come for the formation of a Democratic party, whose aim
would be to continue their work, but by the employment
of different methods. The Morgan Government has done
good service, but it w uld be strengthened if its sup-
porters were untrammelled by unions, which are princi-
pally guided by leaders who begin to think that their
turn is a long time coming. If a Democratic party were
organised now, the fight would be easier when the next
general election comes round. If things remain as they
are, and the Parliamentary Labor party refuses to sign
the Convention platform, there will he serious splits,
which must result in Tory candidates securing seats with
minority votes. As it is just now, there are four irreeon-
cileable Laborists of the Workkr crowd who sit behind
the Government and exhibit greater bitterness towards it
than even Philp and Leahy do in Opposition, and that is
saying a good deal.
An Australian Parliament ary testimonial to
fisherman Ferrier, the hero of the Warrnambool
wreck, remarks
We all feel that your conduct was worthy of the best
deeds done by British sailors in the past, and are proud
to know that Australia can produce such as you.
To this “Joseph Cook, deputy-leader of the
Opposition,’’ solemnly puts his name. But when
it comes to a question of helping Australia to
produce and find work for such as Ferrier by
vetoing the Asiatic seaman and by protecting the
Australian fisherman from cheap tinned foreign
importations, then Joseph Cook’s pride evapor-
ates, and his name is not there.
The N.S.W. collection of Freetraders in
the Australian Parliament is an evil minded lot.
The latest slander comes from Kelly, who insinu-
ated in Parliament that the leaders of the Labor
party forced the Arbitration Act into law in
N.S.W. and then turned themselves into lawyers,
with the result that the union funds went into
their pockets by way of legal fees. Kelly knows
that the Labor party has always advocated the
abolition of-lawyers in Arbitration doings.
S. Australia is getting another look-in at
the ways of Private Enterprise. For years past
the railways have been able to make contracts to
buy ice at from 30s. to 355. per 200011). ; this year
the best offer was £2 10s. There are only two
concerns that can supply the quantity required ;
and they mysteriously put up their price to the
State 40 per cent, simultaneously. It was a co-
incidence, of course; but the coincidence has led
the Government to look up ice-making machinery
of its own. It is just as well to sit behind a gun
when you have to argue with coincidences that
can jump 40 per cent, at one spring.
It was only after the Freetrade Opposition
in the House of Representatives had spent 30
hours discussing a proposal that “the Chairman
should leave the chair” that the affable Deakin
could stand it no longer, and proposed a new-
standing order for cutting short useless debate.
And it was time. The Opposition, after it had
settled about the chair, w ould probably have spent
a similar period discussing whether the Chairman
should leave the table, and whether he should take
the oil-cloth with him when he went away, and it
was ready for a w-eek’s frantic debate as to whether
he should embezzle the front steps or not.
The bell-toppered, smooth-vested Waxcel-
lency Madden, of Victoria, when recently laying
the foundation-stone of a new wing of S. Melbourne
College, said : “ This stone is well and truly laid
—but not by union labor” ! Union labor, for-
sooth ! The immaculate Chief Justice belongs to
the closest trade union in the Commonwealth a
union which restricts the number of apprentices
according to the number of journeymen, which
allows no “improvers” whatsoever, which fixes
and compels payment of its high fees for member-
ship and its equally high wages, and w-hich abso-
lutely prohibits the non-unionist—even the union-
ist from abroad—from practising his trade till he
has joined the union. Not by union labor ! O
Lord !
Queensland has been paying interest for
just 19 years on the cost of enough steel rails to
build 50 miles of road. The rusty stack is lying
at Croydon, from which
centre it was proposed to
extend the line to Clon-
curry, and a sum of £500,000
was voted by Parliament
for the work —19 years ago.
But a profligate Govern-
ment spent the cash on
something else, or lost it,
or lent it, or drank it, and
let the rails lie. The idea
then was to build a trans-
continental line from the
Gulf to strike the Central
system, and thus link the
north with Rockhampton
and afterwards with Bris-
bane. It is now suggested
that, instead of building
the line from Richmond to
Cloncurry, it should go
from Cloncurry to Winton,
and then on to Longreach,
which would connect Clon-
curry with Townsville and
Rockhampton trunk lines.
Then eventually the line
could be extended to Nor-
rnanton, or else through
the Georgina belt towards
Port Darwin. By joining
Charleville with Long-
reach, the link would be
complete with the N.S.W.
system, excepting the com-
paratively short stretch
between Cunnamulla and
Collarendabri. When Queensland can again afford
to look ahead, it will probablv stop the awful
accumulation of interest on that heap of steel rails
at Croydon to begin with.
Ex-Governor Jersey is regarded with
grave alarm in right-thinking cirdes. He persis-
tently refuses to have his leg pulled y
Stinking Fish party, and was heretical enough to
say that Australia is a fine place to live m-this to
a virulently Tory assemblage at a Melbourne gaud
mayor's dinner. Jersey ridicules the idea that
England is all the time telling itself what a bad
state Australia is in, and has the temerity to
assert that stories of English wails about Au -
tralian legislation are greatly exaggerated. He
points out that opinions cabled out at length to
Australia are frequently given by persons who
excite just about as much respect m England as
the phrenologist in the Domain does in Australia.
Jersey even went so far as to smile when the Six
Hatters were mentioned, and any man who would
smile at such a sacred subject is altogether outside
the pale of humanity.

The Disappearing Seed Trick.
This is one of the best and most popular tricks ever invented. It consist*,
of a nicely painted wooden Bell, Casket, and a neat little Bag of Seed, First
let the andience examine everything, then fill the Casket with the Seed.
Place a handkerchief over the Bell and another over the Casket containing’
the Seed. Tap them both, and on lifting the handkerchiefs, to the amaze>
. naent of everybody, the seed has vanished from the Casket and will be found,

or for evening entertainments or parties. The Trick, with full directions, will be posted anywhere, properly
