SPORTING NOTIONS.
On Tuesday, last week, somewhere about 30
sportsmen left Melbourne for Sydney on £14,000 worth of
automobiles and motor bikes in the great Reliability Test,
organised by the big Dunlop Rubber Co. On Saturday
last 14 of them came straggling into Sydney. Some
roared in at 45 miles an hour, others just wheezed, and
one broke down 50yd. from the finishing tape owing to
lack of fuel and the presence of a stray foot
passenger in front of the car. All along the
road public interest was at high-water mark.
After they got into N.S.W. the travellers were
astonished to see the change in the character of the
country. In Victoria they were never out of sight of
farms, and towns were met with every few miles. In
N.S.W. rabbits and an empty land stared them in the
face. All through N.S.W. the course was marked by dead
rabbits and iguanas. The tootling cars, rushing along at
45 miles an hour, paralysed the rabbits, and so interested
the “ goanners ” that they sat up and stared with dull,
conservative eyes on the apparitions till they were
crushed under the wheels.
« * * *
Although it was understood that the Melbourne-
Sydney motor competition was a reliability test and not a
speed race, the various heavy cars put up a tremendous
rush for first place. George Hobbs, on the high-powered
Mercedes, went off with a bound, and Stevens immedi-
ately went after him. Then Foster’s huge Talbot set off
in pursuit of both. This was the mightiest car of the
field, a huge 35-50 h.p. machine, capable of getting up to
anything between 60 and 70 miles an hour if anyone was
mad enough to force it over a Thomas Bent road. Last
time the car was seen in motion the passengers were
hanging on to the sides, and the great object was pro-
gressing by a series of kangaroo-like jumps over the awful
Victorian roads at a speed which seemed to make cer-
tain that the field would soon be collared. Then a water-
jacket burst, a cylinder cracked, and the big Talbot was
eut of the game. Hobbs flew on, and finished ahead of
everything. The second day saw Stevens break away,
and while he was travelling at over 40 miles an
hour to keep) ahead of Hobbs, two railway gates suddenly
heaved up in front of him. There was hardly time
to think, let alone stop, and through them he went.
They splintered as though they had been made of glass,
and the car, with its front axle only slightly bent, flew on
to keep ahead of Hobbs. It did. Later on Liddle in his
Panhard just grazed the front of a train as he roared over
the same crossing, leaving his paint on the cow-catcher.
That day ended with Stevens first home. The third day
Stevens, whose velocity closed gates could not allay, was
brought to a peremptory stop by a punctured tyre, and
Hobbs beat him by lhr. 9£min. The fourth day Stevens was
right out of it, the Mercedes beating him by 75min. Over
the last 129 miles Hobbs turned on all his power and
went for his life on the high gear over hilts and all,
including the Razorback, in 4hr. 9min. When Hobbs
arrived at Enfield, the first question he asked while
he got his breath was, “Where’s Stevens?” -Twenty
minutes later a cloud of dust and a clatter announced
that Stevens was second home. All this was very ex-
citing, but ridiculously dangerous.
* « » •
The performance of the motor-bikes in the test
was a really wonderful revelation of what the little
machines can do when properly handled. As far as speed
goes they were equal to the fastest of the cars, and B.
James, on his Minerva, actually raced the flying Mercedes,
and held his own till he smashed into one of the V-shaped
watercourses which are the curse of N.S.W. roads.
The speed was something in the neighborhood of 40 miles
an hour, and the rider was hurled yards, while the
machine sprang up like an india-rubber acrobat. Even
after that James was able to continue his journey,
although the accident put him out of the Test, and left
the Rand Cup almost a gift for his brother, E. James.
Both these men are as hard as nails, and have an utter
contempt for danger. They are pastoralists, and amuse
themselves by chasing kangaroos on their motor-bikes,
and shooting them at close quarters with revolvers while
racing across grass country on the machines. A man who
goes out and practises round a paved park has no chance
in a rough road contest against that sort of rider.
• * * *
The test was disastrous as regards the reliability
of the imported car on Australian roads. With the ex-
ception of Captain Tarrant’s Australian-made car, every
machine that competed arrived with bent axles. t English
and French makers having simply no idea of what sort of
roads can be met with in Australia, especially where the
highways are under the care of Victorian shire councils.
Of the cars that started over 50 per cent, were knocked
out by breaks-down and accidents. Some of the smashes
were due to careless driving and general recklessness,
but all motorists are agreed on the point that the
imported cars are unsuitable for Australian roads, and
that we require a specially-designed and built car for our
own use. Evidently, if Australians desire to rush round
the country in motor cars with a reasonable hope of
getting back without the aid of the despised moke, they
will have to settle down to make their own vehicles, as
they did to make their tyres. The only all-Aus-
tralian-made car in the contest was the 8-h.p.
one driven tjy Captain Tarrant, last year’s winner.
It was made in his own workshop to his own
design, and the workmen were still engaged on
it while the horns of the competitors were tooting
on their way to the starting- place. It ran straight out of
the workshop to take part in a 570-mile reliability contest
without any previous test or trial whatever. It got over
the distance in 30hr.~4min. 30sec. without any mishap or
breakdown, the only delay being due to a punctured tyre.
Altogether, Australian industry showed up very well;
of the seven cars that have to take part in the run-off, six
were fitted with Australian-made tyres.
* * * *
Walter Rutt, the German cyclist who was here
last year under special permit from Kaiser Bill himself,
has arrived at Fremantle, on his way to annex the
Summer Wheel Race, Sydney Thousand, and the Austral
jf he reaches Melbourne in time to get into condition.
Rutt was the handsomest cyclist on the track last year,
although his legs were not in it with the beautiful pale
understandings of Ellegaard. He brings with him the
Flying- Jew, Henry Mqyer, the man who has twice run
third in the world’s championship final. Also, he won
the <Jrand Prix de Paris—the blue ribbon cycle race of
Europe. Meyer met and defeated George Farley when
the Australian champion was in Paris, and has the
reputation of being able to stay as well as sprint. Also,
he is understood to be a wheelist who takes risks, and
raises dizzy streaks of speed per medium of the dope.
Neither of these riders will strike the £6 ss. minimum
appearance money, as they are specially engaged.

Melbourne Bicycle Club is going to distribute
£ll6B prize money at the Austral Wheel Race meeting
this year. The field will comprise 14 scratch races and
10 handicaps. The Austral itself will carry £5OO for the
first, £lOO for the second, and £2O for the third, while the
winners of the semi-finals will divide £3O among them.
The classic race will remain a two-mile event, and be run
on the asphalt track at Melbourne Exhibition. The
meeting will start on December 2nd, and end on the 16th

The calm thoroughness of Ma'oriland bikist
brothers Arnst is disconcerting. At Mudgee (N.S.W.)
King’s Birthday Sports, “ Jack ” collared the big wheel
race, then “Dick” scooped the five-mile scratch race,
whilst brother “ Jack ” got his wind by pocketing eight lap
prizes in the same event. Then “Dick” sort of relaxed
himself by entering and winning both the “putting the
shot” and “throwing the hammer” events. With
Mudgee over £5O the poorer, the brothers Arnst departed
for Melbourne Austral meeting.

Cycles in the Dunlop Reliability Test.
The Bulletin has before it a typed letter,
which purports to have been issued by Mr. P. G. Forbes,
of No. 11, first floor, Temperance and General Building's,
Little Collins-street, Melbourne. It sets forth that Mr.
F. G. Forbes has discovered a system of betting which is
distinguished by its “mathematical accuracy” and its
“surprising simplicity," Under it the backer will never
get on to a scratched horse, he need only have one invest-
ment on a race, and the average returns will be five wins
for one loss. In the circular, which is addressed to a
gentleman (apparently a complete stranger; at Boggabri
(N.S.W.), Mr. F. G. Forbes offers to sell the secret for £l,
and to invest any money the Boggabri gentleman likes to
send, taking only 10 per cent, commission for himself.
Now, why this amazing generosity to a gentleman at
Boggabri ? There is only a certain amount of money
in the ring, and if 20 energetic people get on to a
system whereby they can win five times out of every six
they will break it hopelessly. Even one man, with such
a system, and playing up his winnings manfully, would
break the ring, for there seems to be no limit to the
amount of the investment (in his overflowing desire to
do the square thing by the gentleman at Boggabri Mr.
F. G. Forbes offers to operate with any investment he
likes to send). Then the goose that deposited the golden
egg will be dead, and even if all the 20 people paid
Mr. F. G. Forbes, of the Temperance and General
Building (No. 11 first floor), instead of some of them
getting the secret at second hand from some incautious
individual (say a gentleman at Boggabri) who had
already bought it, the inventor would only have made
£2O. And out of that he must pay stamps, advertising,
and the rent of the office in the Temperance and General
Building (No. 11 first floor). This is an awful throw-
ing away of money on his part. Why doesn’t he
shut up his office (No. 11, first floor) and save the rent,
and also save the stamps that he wastes on correspond-
ence with gentlemen at Boggabri, and bet with the money
on his own infallible system, and break the ring himself,
and retire on the proceeds? If anybody at Boggabri took
advantage of the generosity of Mr. F. G. Forbes it would
be little short of. robbery.

A smart piece of work was done in Melbourne
onOup night. The race wasn’t over till 3.35, yet by 10
o’clock next morning fancy-goods shops in the city and
suburbs were selling picture post-cards showing the finish
of the race. Pity the energy wasn’t spent on a worthier
subject.

“ Woodki ” : Blue Spec’s win is another example
of the frequent success of the averaging of weights
system, noticed in The Bulletin last Oup time. The
average worked out to Bst. lib., and Blue Spec at Bst. was
the nearest thereto. Demas (Bst. 31b.) in the Hotham
Handicap was another.
* * * *
In the Bankruptcy Court last week James
Duncan threw the light of revelation on Sydney pony-
racing clubs. James declared that some of “ the clubs
often entered his ponies without his knowledge or con-
sent, and that it was quite usual when he had won a
prize and thought he owed the club a fiver to be informed
that he was indebted £lO and over.” He also declared
with bitterness that when a pony won a few prizes the
weight was made so heavy that to get it off it was neces-
sary to let the ponies get right out of condition. In fact,
Duncan generally intimated that the poor owner was
much worse off than the miserable punter. But the clubs
are prosperous 1
* # * *
For many a long year Dame Fortune has smiled
upon the giant firm of Anthony Hordern and Sons. Once
in a while, too, she remembers the employees of the firm.
Charles Stidwell, a salesman in Hordern’s dress depart-
ment, drew Blue Spec in the big sweep on Melbourne Cup
and in exchange for a modest 10s. received £11,400. Stid-
well, who has been in Australia about five years, is a native
of Devonshire and intends returning temporarily to the
Cold Land. Some few years ago another assistant, named
Limbert, drew a big prize when Tarcoola won the Cup.
The good luck of these two men ,is quite eclipsed by the
remarkable fortune that came to a third employee. In
the early nineties a young man employed as a storeman
left the firm and went to Westralia and dabbled in mining.
He found himself in time the possessor of a quarter of a
million.
*• * w *
“Adelaide”: S.A. never had a bigger chance
(on paper) of beating Victoria at cricket than at the late
meeting. The Melbourne men were mostly experiments,
but four of the old hands won the match. Saunders,
Collins and Armstrong took the whole 20 wickets ; then, i
the S. A. total being 378 in two attempts, Armstrong and:
M'Alister together put up 322 in one 1 Local fielding was;
awful; 14 missed chances are recorded. Newland at the
wicket had about half of these. Local clamor demands the
retirement of Hack, who loses in the field whatever he
may make with the bat; and of Coombe, the stylish
bowler whom all good judges like, but who never gets
wickets. Old Jack Reedman (who has been playing since
1888) took four Victorian wickets. Roy Hill, making a
first appearance, bowled Ransford and Warne, and no
one else took more than one. Carkeek shaped very well
at the wickets for Victoria, and is a free, if ugly, left-
handed bat. Of the other new men, Christian never
looked like taking wickets, or Horan like making runs. ;
N.S. W. ought to have a soft thing for the Shield this year.
* • * *
A feature of Sydney’s grade cricket, on Satur-
day, was the comparatively rotten start made by North
Sydney against Gordon, which used once to be a part of
itself. Even Alphabetical White managed to paste only
16 opposite his name, and the side’s top score was a
miserable 38 by N. Deane. Gordon seemed to have in
M'Cartne.v a bowler of some deadliness—he took six for
36 ; which is unusual when North Sydney is at the sticks.
The whole side vanished for 136, and at the drawing of
the stumps Gordon had one wicket down for 32.
A certain amount of mild interest attached to
Saturday’s cricket, as several of the Australian XI.
screwed their courage up to the sticking point and ven-
tured to take a hand. Cotter swung the willow for the
Glebe, but was bowled by Kerim, the Philistine—that is
the Syrian—when he had made only 17. For the same
side Newell slogged 117 before the same dusky bowler
with the nose clean bowled him. E. R. Bubb had the
misfortune to get stumped when his century was only
four runs away. Altogether, Glebe’s struggle with Red-
fern was the bright particular match of the day, the
Abattoirs putting 365 to their credit for the loss of nine
wickets. The Asiatic, Kerim, was Redfern’s best bowler,
and in spite of the skill with which he makes the ball
break either way at choice, he could do no better than
five for 126 runs.
The only other century of the afternoon was
Blaxland’s 105 for Balmain against Central Cuml riand.
Whitting and Robson made 55 and 40. Nevertheless
Central Cumberland’s bowlers managed to make good
averages, Cronin taking three for 32 and Cranney five for
60. It was a dull, grey afternoon, with rain falling; the
light failed much sooner than usual, and the term of play
was considerably shortened.
* * * *
So far this season, Victorian cricket has been of
the common or garden—the back garden—variety. The
ten association and the six league clubs now play all in
one tourney for the alleged premiership ; but as they are
still merely clubs, not districts or electorates, they excite
as much interest as a cat-fight. The third round closed
on Monday (King’s birthday), leaving East Melbourne a
trifle ahead of Melbourne and North. Two rounds passed
without a single batsman achieving a century, but the
third saw Gerber (Carlton) make 104, Mitchell (Fitzroy)
104, and Peryman (Melb.) 132. Notwithstanding low
scores all round, no star bowlers have risen ; the same old
trundlers of past seasons vary from moderate to good.
Jones, of North Melbourne, improves his wicket-keeping,
and bids well for inter-State, if not international, con-
tests. As to attendances —well, Tarrant, of Fitzroy, com-
piled a whole six runs on Saturday, Nov. 11th, in 70
minutes, and that sort of thing wearies spectators. Cer-
tainly he put on 55 in the next 70 minutes, but the few
enthusiasts present had gone home before then. Mean-
while, junior cricket, in which the teams genuinely repre-
sent districts, though not perhaps so scientific, draws
five times the crowds.
* * * *
Playing for Fort-street against Leichhardt, at
Petersham (Sydney), t’other day, a youngster named C.
Landon put up the respectable total of 369. He played
faultlessly till past the second century, when he gave a
chance, which, being declined, he didn’t repeat. This is
the second highest score ever knocked together in N.S.W.,
the record being held by schoolboy O. H. Dean, who last
year scored 412 for Sydney Grammar School against
Newington College.
*■* * *
Warne, the Victorian batsman, playing against
the weak South Australian bowling, succeeded in amassing
nine runs in 80 minutes before the luncheon adjournment,
and four of those were in one hit. The presence of such
a depressing person in representative cricket ought to be
severely discouraged. He resembles a funeral on a wet
day.

It was the leg-breaks of G. L. Garnsey that
demoralised the Bananaland batsmen in the recent inter-
state match. The Bur wood bowler took in all 11 wickets
for 172 runs off 249 balls.
* * * *
The N.S.W. team to battle for the Sheffield
Shield against Victoria and South Australia will have
quite a new and' shiny aspect about it. Most of the
just-returned XI. haven’t recovered sufficiently from the
shock of England’s drubbing, and of those who are
available it isn’t certain whether they can get a place in
the team. The splendid batting and bowling of the
N.S.W. team against Bananaland make it reasonable to
expect that the team to go south will not differ very
much from the one which went north. Since the mem-
bers of the Australian XI. have just returned from a
heavy tour, the N.S.W. selection committee could do
much worse than leave them out, and give some other
players a chance.

Australia’s champion swimmer was to have
arrived in Sydney yesterday (Wednesday) morning. In
all probability the papers will be flooded with portraits of
him in his swimming costume. This will be a grave
mistake. Everybody has seen Kieran with his clothes
off. What the public really wants to know is what Kieran
looks like with his clothes on, or if he ever wears clothes
at all ?
V * * ■*
There was fun galore in Port Jackson at Sydney
Sailing Club’s race. Fifteen started, Keriki being the
scratch boat. At the very start the boats were bumping,
and at all the rounding marks they crowded one another
“.on to the rails” in a fearsome fashion. Australian was
rushed along by her crew in a startling manner, and was
going through the fleet like water through a sieve when
Arawa put her about and forced her to lose time. Squar-
ing away round the lightship, Australian set spinnaker,
ballooner and ringtail, and with ’em all on ran through
the crowd into second place. At the rounding mark
Mona got her spinnaker overboard, Donnelly’s blew off
skyward, and the extras of half the fleet showed signs of
taking charge. Then the boats collided and got all tied
up together, and there was struggling and “ langwidge.”
Australian and Alert sailed out of the jumble and
finished in that order, with the scratch boat, Keriki,
third.
* *
*
Sydney’s Prince Alfred Yacht Club had an
ocean race on Saturday. Five miles E.N.-E. from Sydney
Heads a mark had been put down, and the prize for
getting first round that and home again was the Petrel
Cup, a bauble presented by Commodore Dempster, to be
won three times before any man can put it in his cup-
board and ask you to have a drink out of “ My Cup.” The
steamer Cobar, which followed the race, was fairly
crowded, and when the Heads were passed 'and the long,
jobbly roll began to state its views on current events
there was woe for the passengers. Seven started, and
before reaching the Heads the limit boat, Kukuburra,
was passed by Scotia, Cooya, and Magic. On the thrash
to the mark all interest centred on Magic and Heather,
which strove hard to outpoint one another. Magic
rounded 22 seconds ahead of Heather. Behind came
Scotia, Fleetwing, Cooya, and Culwulla. As the rain
came down the breeze failed, and the sail home was slow.
Fleetwing moved well in the light wind, passing Scotia
and Heather, but Magic dipped on ahead and won by
4min. 9sec. In such a long race, and an ocean race at
that, this was very close indeed.
Said that there are doings in connection with
open boat sailing on Sydney Harbor which would make a
pony owner blush. Anyhow, quite recently the public on
the steamer that followed the race made a loud demonstra-
tion against one crew, and invited it to come on board and
‘ see the mess we’ll make of you.” The crew didn’t and
it was gravely proposed by one angry backer who had lost
his money, to attend next week’s race armed with road
metal. The motion was not carried, but there was alack
of heartiness in its condemnation which augurs ill for that
crew if it meets any of its friends on a lonely road on a
dark night. Quite a number of other things have
happened lately to show that the control of the sport is
very lax. ,

Just now a small army of professional athletes
are working round the continent on a time-table that will
land them in Kalgoorlie what time “the Hundred” is
competed for. Some of them, like Postle, the Queens-
land crack, are making exes, on the way. Postle
dropped in on Stawell (Vic.) last week and mopped up
130 yd. and 440 yd. events, and departed with a few
pounds to help him on his road.
* * * *
The Australasian Amateur Athletic Conference
has decided to impose a fine of £5 on any association not
represented at an Australasian championship meeting,
the State association controlling the meeting to pocket
the fines. While the conference was about it, why wasn’t
the fine made sufficiently heavy to do some good? In
many cases it will be found much cheaper to pay the £5
than make the journey.
* * * *
Victorian Athletic League has determinedly set
its face against professional peds. raiding the country
under assumed names. Any pro. caught racing at any
meeting urtder an alias will be remorselessly wiped out.
There’s justa chance that pedestrianism, if it is kept clean,
may revive in Australia ; and the League is at least trying
to keep it clean.

J. R. Costigan and A. E. Kelly recently fought
a prize fight at New Haven (U.S.A.). Costigan was
knocked out, and is now suing Kelly for $5OOO damages
for assault. This is a new aspect of the noble art.
* * * *
The Bob Eraser versus Jack Willis glove contest
at Queen’s Hall, Melbourne, the other night, was a nice,
neat, gentlemanly encounter so far as it went, and it went
13 rounds, with Bob on top all the Way. Willis, who is a
deft middle-weight, had just come out of the West, and
his cqndition was a trifle sloppy, but the chances are that
Fraser would account for him at his best now, as Robert
is a vastly improved fighter, and an artist at just missing
things. He wants power as a hitter, but he can evade
like the oiliest lawyer in politics. In the thirteenth round
Willis decided that a bad hand was a reasonable excuse
for quitting, and he begged to be excused. He had put
up a game fight, and was excused with the greatest good
will. Fraser is a man whom two or three of the best in
his class in Sydney should be inquiring after. He can be
relied upon to make a good fight against anything going.
The scrap at the National Sporting Club
(Sydney) between Bandsman J. Thompson and Arthur
Cripps started quietly enough. There was an air of Potts
Point boxing about it, and Cripps varied the proceedings
with exhibitions of footwork which suggested that at one
time he was connected with the dancing trade. Thompson
started to “ make it willing,” but the wary Cripps dealt
out body blows that had the effect of taking the edge off
Thompson’s appetite for closer acquaintance. The erowd
began to get restless, and men leaned over the shoulders
of those in front and hissed directions as to the exact way
they wanted the other fellow killed and buried. Cripps
took no notice. He was going to play his own game, and
he wanted Thompson to attack him. Thompson, getting
nervous at the talk from the benches, rushed in and tried
to land on the jaw. He reached the middle of the face,
and the blow caused Cripps to miss with a right cross that
might have ended things very suddenly. Two more on
the body caused Cripps to reach for a clinch, instead of
which he caught a right on the jaw, and he bent down
and hit out wiklly at the military man’s commissariat.
Thompson received a sounding blow on the ribs, but
instead of falling as was expected he sprang at Cripps
and rained blows on him. Cripps grazed the bandsman’s
chin with his right, and Thompson stood off quickly,
dealing a nasty one on the mouth as he went. After that
Thompson went slow, and Cripps tried to do the hurri-
cane act without much effect. The tenth saw Cripps
taken by surprise and forced to save himself by clinching
—the bandsman belted him hard for two rounds, but
failed to knock him out. Then his own strength started
to give way, and in the I4th Cripps right-handed him on
the jaw twice in rapid succession, and down went Thomp-
son. He Came up to meet the right once again, and
when the bell went be arose in a daze and was led by
Cripps to the chair. Then the towel soared. It was a
skilful fight, but Thompson did not battle up to his
regular form.
* * * *
Walcott and Yates put up a dreary exhibition
at Sydney Gaiety Club on Saturday night. Yates seemed
to be consumed with a desire to avoid assault and battery,
and apparently did not understand that the audience
wanted to see him hit. His caution was disheartening.
He poked cautious, reconnoitring fists into his enemy’s
face, and the enemy retaliated with s wipes at his jaw and
bashes at his ribs. Yates simply would not try to slay
Walcott, and a weary audience was not sorry when, at the
end of the 7th round, the towel of judicious surrender
was thrown into the ring, after being used to wipe Yates’
face, and Walcott was seen streaking for the pay office.

According to the Minister of Defence,
Captain Cresswell, Naval Commandant, estimates
that Australia requires for its coastal protection
“three cruiser destroyers, 1C torpedo boat des-
troyers, and 15 torpedo boats of the first and
second classes.” He proposes that these should
be “procured” at intervals spread over seven
years. It would be a great deal better if they
were built here instead of “procured”; and in
any case the squadron proposed is a darned poor
apology for a fleet where there are all these thou-
sands of miles of coast-lme to defend. If Aus-
tralia were the Isle of Man, or the Isle of Dogs,
this small bawbee way of doing things might be
good enough—but it isn’t. It is also explained
that “it is not intended that our navy should go
out to sea and engage an enemy,” but if the bulk
of the proposed navy happens to be at Sydney,
and the enemy starts wrecking Brisbane or Mel-
bourne, and the navy doesn’t go out to sea and
engage him, what blessed use will the navy be ?
The proposed flotilla is better than nothing, but
the flotilla that is only better than nothing is a
makeshift.
The Bulletin receives a foolish letter,
signed “Alex. Wilson,” in which Alex. Wilson
suggests that N.S.W. State Parliament should
select a site for the Federal capital, and that Earl
Jersey should decide between that site and Dal*
gety, the Australian Parliament’s choice. This
paper doesn’t see what Earl Jersey has to do with
it, nor does it see what the N.S.W. State Parlia
ment has to do with it.- Certainly, if the N.S.W.
State Parliament has a voice in selecting the capi-
tal site, the State Parliaments of Queensland,
Victoria, Tasmania, S.A. and Westralia should
have just as loud a voice, provided the place they
suggest is within the limits fixed by the Constitu-
tion. The capital is to be the Federal capital of
all the States, and they will all contribute to its
cost. Certainly N.S.W. supplies the land, but
then the W. Premier demanded, as a privilege
or a right, that his State should supply the land.
N.S.W. can’t now claim a special right of
selection to compensate it for being deprived
of the land when it truculently demanded that it
should be deprived of the land. The same thing
can’t be at once a right and a privilege and an
injury. If the supplying of the Federal territory
is a privilege, then let State politicians be con-
tent with the privilege they have got, and not go
howling insatiably for more privileges. If it is a
wrong and an injury and a robbery, let them
simply tell the Australian Parliament to fix its
capital in South Queensland, and be done with it.

“ C. 8.” writes :
You say (B. 2/11/’O5) that “ the proud and sensitive
Jap” may be noted in “all the brothels from Oairns, etc.,
to Kalgoorlie.” This is only half the truth. There are
kimonaed prostitutes in Melbourne who are hired out by
the “proud and sensitive” little Monkey-man to the
Syrian and Hindu colonies in Exhibition and Little Lons-
dale streets. Officialdom denies their presence, but a
blind man can find them any time. The Jap woman of
pleasure can also be found in Brisbane. Some years ago
three of her settled down in Fortitude Valley, where she
was well known to the time-expired Kanakas. Eventually
some Kanakas raised trouble, and a battle-royal ensued
between them and the little brown bullies from Chrysan-
themum land. Then there were police court proceedings,
and the hurried departure of the soiled Geisha. Next
some kimona girls took a cottage near Breakfast Creek.
Here, again, officialdom denied their existence, but a row
over a canary brought them all before the police court
and they fled north to Townsville. After a time they
again sailed southwards, and are in Brisbane now. An
attempt to “vag.” their bullies failed, for the Bench
decided they had “ visible means of support.” The lawful-
ness of the visibility was a matter the amateur Solons
didn’t trouble with. Also Jap girls of pleasure have been
known as far inland as VVinton and Barcaldine.
Some remarks—not wholly untruthful—
from London Daily Chkonicle
We do not know, and cannot, therefore, profitably
speculate upon, the attitude of the Australian Common-
wealth to this new alliance. The military and naval
impotence of the Commonwealth is such that as a conse-
quence of its hostile legislation against the Mongolian
race, the occupation of Sydney, Melbourne and Adelaide
would be an irresistible temptation to the captors of Port
Arthur but for the fact that Australia, though virtually
an industrial republic, is still nominally within the circle
of the British Empire. According to Major-General
Hutton, late Federal Commandant, a force capable of
taking the field does not at present exist in Australia.
Lieutenant-Colonel J. C. Neild, Senator of the Common-
wealth, states that the people of Australia are living in a
“fools’ paradise.” When General Hutton quitted Aus-
tralia he handed over to his successor at the end of 1904 a
force of 18,000 troops, a reduction of 8000 in three years,
the departments necessary for a mobile army having yet
to be created. Having no navy and neither funds, know-
ledge nor plant for the creation of a fleet, the Australian
public are as defenceless against the Japanese as the
Koreans, although they refuse to contribute to the cost
of the British Navy beyo-sd a trifle which does not defray
half the upkeep of the strategically useless and practically
obsolete squadron maintained by British taxpayers in
Australian waters.
Canada, which does not contribute one cent to the cost
of the British Navy, has rivalled the Commonwealth, at
all events on the British Columbian side of the Continent,
in her cavalier treatment of our Asiatic allies. Does
Canada approve of an Imperial policy which depends for
defence of the Indian frontier upon Asiatics, whose ambi-
tions are boundless and whose real feelings towards Eng-
land are those of an efficient, hungry, and impoverished
swordsman for an opulent and impotent employer?
A “fools’ Paradise,” “as defenceless as the
Koreans,” the “strategically useless arid practi-
cally obsolete squadron,” and England as the
“impotent employer” of the efficient and hungry
Japanese swordsman ! What a picture for Aus-
tralia, which has just been over-drinking itself on
Trafalgar Day.
Griffith and Allan, Patent Attorneys of
Sydney, have opened a branch of their business in
Melbourne—(Broken Hiil Chambers, Queen-st.),
under the management of Mr. H. P. lveogh of
Melbourne.*
Dr. Tim Hynes, of Adelaide, who has just
come back from Japan, says that the Govern-
ment has Europeanised that country to the extent
of vetoing nude male bathing when women are
adjacent. So the bathers wear trunks, which
they put on just before they jump into the water
and discard when they emerge, being then rubbed
down by attentive Japanese girls before donning
their ordinary attire. The letter of the law is thus
scrupulously observed, but the spirit of things is
as nude as ever.
Again the “poor farmer, the plucky, self-
pliant primary producer.” The Vic. Agricultural
Department is now building 13 large silos in
different parts of Bent’s State, “to encourage
farmers to adopt modern methods in the produc-
tion of ensilage and to systematically test its value
for feeding dairy cows and sheep during dry
weather.” And when the farmer has waxed fat
through this State assistance and supervision he
will puff out his fat waistcoat and bellow against
“ Socialistic legislation.”

<< jjonio” Anenfc the rule in the Public
Service that, everything else being equal, merit

The following are extracts from letters
which appeared in Melbourne Argus in February,
1880 :
We are anxiously awaiting; news of your elections. The
injury these men have done the colony is immeasurable.
I could have got thousands of pounds to invest, but the
story of the Ministry having their “ hands on the throat
of capital ” has spread far and wide. But for these men,
English capital would have flowed into the colony and en-
couraged enterprise and labor, which is now stagnant.
The withering curse of the Berry blight has so thoroughly
shaken the confidence of capitalists and others that free-
hold property, in which alone I was dealing, has not
only depreciated very considerably over 40 per cent, as a
g’eneral average value all over the colony, but has become
positively unsaleable at any price whatever ; neither does
it now appear a bond fide security to finance upon.
The habit of remarking that confidence was being
destroyed and capital driven out of the country
was apparently just as strong 25 years ago as it is
to-day. Probably it existed even in the days
when Hebrew capital was driven out of Egypt
and Pharaoh lost confidence in the Red Sea.
This should delight the heart of Bruce
Smith. In parts of the N.T. the Chinese, who
have taken root and know their power, already
refer to Australians as “ fan-qui ’’—foreign devils.
There isn’t any doubt in their minds as to who
really owns the country.
Hawthorn (Vic.) Women’s National League
got a surprise the other evening, when a handful
of the Very Best People of the suburb turned
up to extend the glad hand to their washer-
women arid listen to a lecture by Lonsdale,
M.H.R., who gave Socialism particular Tophet
for about an hour, and was cheered with much
enthusiasm. Then he turned to find a solu-
tion of the ills that afflict humanity, and threw
land-tax at that women’s league while it squirmed
in agony. The land-tax, said Lonsdale, was the
only thing possible, and he related instances of
landlords’ iniquities until that league reached for
its silver-mounted smelling salts. As he sat
down, a youthful socialist, with a sense of humor,
asked the chairman if he would take a show of
hands of the members on Mr. Lonsdale’s sugges-
tions about the land-tax, and the chairman said it
was a fine evening, or words to that effect, and
hurriedly declared the meeting closed.
“L. ” : Observe how seldom the name-
brooch wearer’s name fits her appearance. Old
Brown was lamenting to me the other day that
his wife (aged 48 and wearing badly) would insist
on sporting a brooch, presented to her many years
ago, which bore the name of her babyhood, “Little
Popsy.” Just then we were passed by a “ Grace ”
who must be the least graceful woman in Australia,
and a “ Queenie ” who was remarkably unlike the
queen of a poet’s dream. Then I went home, and
noticed that the brooch of my 14-stone landlady
called her “Dot.”
“Kyle”: The proposed Barren Jack
(N.S.W.) water scheme promises to raise the old
row with S.A. about the division of the waters of
the Big Trickle. It seems a pity that something
large and definite and final can’t be done with the
shocking stream. Has any enthusiastic but insane
engineer ever tried to calculate the cost of divert-
ing the Murray at Morgan (S.A.), and starting it
north to empty into Lake Frome or Lake Blanche
or Lake Gregory, instead of leaving it to go
moaning uselessly into the sea at the Murray
Mouth ? Australia hasn’t so much fresh water
that it can afford to go dropping it into the sea
after this fashion.

The German lottery promoter thinks very
highly of England’s “Empire-building” policy—
especially the new penny-postage part of it. It
costs him 2|d. to send a letter to Australia direct,
so he sends bundles of them across to England,
and this afflicted country is beginning to be
littered with them. And now the manufacturer
of German notions is following suit.

The Inky Way.
Even Nelson was all the better for having a sub-editor
on the premises. He proposed, “ Nelson confides that
every man will do his duty.” The fleet sub. suggested
“expects,” Nelson changed the first word to “England,”
and it stood at that.

In the life of London journalist John Robinson it is
stated that Douglas Jerrold, when editing Lloyd’s
Weekly, gave great offence to the royal family. His
consisted in adversely criticising a sermon in which
the Rev. Hugh M'Neill, of Liverpool, compared the Prince
Consort’s visit to that city to the second coming of Christ 1

“O’Fipp”: After long study of Argus leaders I have
formed a theory. There must be a rule that no leading
article shall contain less than 20 quotations. It also
appears to be understood that most of the leader’s state-
ments should be put as questions. Apparently the Argus
Office contains a trunk-full of miscellaneous extracts in
prose and verse, and when the tame Jove of the establish-
ment wants to forge thunderbolts he opens it and
dredges up a handful indiscriminately. Then he starts :
“Let the members most concerned remember the
words of the anonymous thinker who inscribed his
thoughts on the bricky pages of the Egyptian Book of
the Dead— 1 1 have not driven back the water at the time
of its rising. I have not turned aside a watercourse.’
Solemn thought!—especially for Mr. Deakin, for has he
not indeed turned aside the watercourse of Mr. Reid’s
majestic progress as Prime Minister ? Every right-
thinking person will here remember M. de Voltaire’s
apologue, which we will not insult our readers by quoting.
Enough that (as Mr. Allan M‘Lean, M.H.R., puts it in his
poems)
‘ Ah me 1 ’tis done ! *
(We quote from memory.) Has not Mr. Deakin told his
‘most dainty and honey-sweet mistress,’ the Common-
wealth, in the terms of the late Mr. John Ford’s problem
play, ‘ Tis Pity She’s a Woman with a Past,’ ‘ Though you
like my uncle’s wit better than mine you shall marry me,’
and, as new Prime Minister, taken forcible possession of
her young affections ? But, as M. le Seigneur de Mon-
taigne some years ago pointed out, ‘To-morrowe is a
newe daye.’ Is not Mr. Reid even now, like that common-
sense person, Signor de Boccaccio’s Friar Onion, 1 laugh-
ing in his sleeve at those who had designed to make a jest
of him ’ ? All will remember what Mr. George Meredith
has said, and the views of Mr. W. E. Henley, And,
like M. Rabelais’s Panurge, may not Mr. Reid
yet clear the Federal ship till there is ‘ never
another sheepish soul left lurking on board’? May
we not hope with Cains Petronius Arbiter that this
will be done ‘without indecent haste’ (‘decently and in
order,’ or ‘ ohne hast, ohne rast,’ as the Hebrew and
Teutonic thinkers expressed similar sentiments)? And
will not Mr. Reid remember the words sometimes attri-
buted to Madame Roland—‘Liberty! what crimes are
committed in thy name ! ’ and, now that he has the
liberty of a leader of His Majesty’s Opposition, will he
not, we ask, so use it that none shall be able to say of
him that he is
‘ Such stuff
As dreams are made of ’ ?
Dreams, we mean, of what an unidentified seer has called
‘ the liberty that degenerates into license.’”
The art of Argus leader-writing is a good art, and there
is nothing else on earth just like it.
• * * *
The depth of inanity is reached by some English papers
in articles written round the photographs of “ children of
notable people.” Any sort of a title is taken as a proof
of notability, and the discerning journalist can find in a
child a few months old as many signs of genius as those
discovered by the peripatetic phrenologist who volunteers
to “ read yer bumps fer a bob.” The more unintelligent
a child looks, the brighter are the hopes held out of future
greatness. A sample, from the Boudoir, treats of an infant
about two years of age:—“ Everything connected with
machinery is the joy of this little boy’s life ; he loves
trains, and railway lines with miniature' telegraph posts,
and bis last and dearest possession is a crane, that
ingenious contrivance whereby great weights can so easily
be lifted. He is but a mite at present, but has quite a
respectable collection of mechanical toys, model bridges
and railway signals, which work up and down in the most
approved manner, and other things of the kind too
numerous to mention. Perhaps some day he may become
a famous mechanician.” Perhaps he may ; but on the
other hand he may simply grow up to fly round in a
motor car like Willie Kelly, M.H.R., and run over dogs
and cow*.
•V * * *
“L.L.”: Ernest Favenc’s interesting remarks on
writers’ mannerisms and compositors’ vagaries (20/7/’O5)
remind me of my type-snatching days on Newcastle
Herald years ago. Editor Corrigan wrote a bad but
consistent hand, though his copy was good enough when
you got used to it. He had a habit, however, of abbre-
viating ; and one night a new comp got the leader to set.
He took “ Govt.” to be “Goat,” thinking it was some
sling-off or other, and he set it “ Goat ” all through the
article. As a rule, the old man read his own stuff, with-
out troubling the proof-reader, but this night he didn’t
turn up, and the proof lay on his table unheeded. There
was a species of earthquake in the office next day, and
the unfortunate reader got “particular.” For many
months Corrigan wrote “ Gonnmnnnnt ” carefully at fuil
length, that being as near as he could get it. Mr.' Favenc
gives “ lovely,” “ doze,” and “ cheery ” as words that un-
dermine the comp’s intellect; but I think the word that
rattles him most of all is “exorcise.” “Exercise” he
knows, but very few of him can be brought to admit the
existence of “ exorcise.” But it would be hard to decide
whether the scribe or the stamp-lifter most deserves the
crown of martyrdom.
* * * *
“ Hassan ” : Sacri! how I hate, how despise this canaille
of “ Pro Bono Publico,” this “ George Pile,” this “ Father
of Ten.” In France it is not so. One listens not to such.
Bah ! I, fresh to the papers of this country, write, ah,
such magnificent, beautiful sentiment, an article, say, of
interest undoubted on “ L’entente Cordiale.” I take it to
Monsieur le ridacteur. He is pleased—he is touched—
Aha ! he weeps upon his table. “ Noble,” he says. “Ah !
Philanthrope, I embrace you.” But he does not print,
and I desire new boots. Why does he not print ? I
whisper in your ear, M. le ' Bulletin. He does not
print because this thief of a “ Veritas,” this
cochon of an “E Pluribus Unum” argue in many
columns as to the habits of God. For nothing ! They do
not desire payment. They write—ah ! the such miser-
able wash-hog, wb oh no one reads—for la gloire. La
gloire! Lieu! That is not all. Permit me to weep,
monsieur. I Idas ! I write the tale of France’s wrongs.
Joy ! It prints itself. I write more—France has many
sorrows. But, behold! This vile “Justitia” has
answered—always without pay-one, two columns. And
the further sorrows are crowded out. The ridacteur
weeps with me, but does he print? Do weeps buy
biftek ? Do weeps keep the door from the wolf ? Bah !
Canaille d’ inkslingaires ! I like to push their faces.
* * * *
A Melbourne man tells this story. Travelling in a
railway carriage, with one of the Argus Mackinnons
and another English-looking johnnie seated opposite to
him, he was reading Tiie Bulletin. Said the other
johnnie, “The Bulletin is a clevah papah, I’m told,” to
which the Mackinnon replied, “Scurrilous rag; no
respectable person reads it.” This in a very loud’ and
significant tone of voice. The disrespectable man on the
opposite seat also had a friend beside him, from whom he
borrowed the Argus in exchange for The Bulletin.
Presently he exploded with wrath at the foolishness of
the morning journal—it’s twaddling Tory leaders, and
the columns of puerile discussion about the Twelve
Best Roses, or the Five Fattest Freetraders. “ I’ll never
look at the d d dreary thing again,” said he, throwing
the copy out of window. Then the haughty Mackinnon
looked as though he wished he hadn’t spoken.
* * * •
“0.”: Strange how a man’s trade sometimes gets him
down. At a funeral I met a proof-reader I knew, and
after the ceremony we went meditating among the tombs.
He criticised the wording and punctuation of nearly every
inscription we saw. Coming to one of particularly bad
construction, he produced a pencil, and made corrections
in spelling and capitals, deleted commas, and so on.
After that he wanted to stop and proof-read every slab on
the way to the cemetery gates. I just managed to get
him away in time to take up his night’s work on the
Daily Megaphone*

KIDNEY DISEASE, CRAVEL, STONE, &c.
Pertinent Points.
POINT I.— You are more concerned in the
prevention of Kidney disease than in its dis-
covery ; where you discover it, the outlook is
very gloomy, the mere knowledge is enough
to appall the strongest.
POINT 2.— We are particularly concerned in
the prevention and palliation of Kidney
disease, and you can enjoy the benefit of
Kutnow’s Powder or reject it. You are a
perfectly free agent.
POINT 3.— The existence of Kidney disease in
the early stages is very difficult to determine,
and can only be successfully diagnosed by
means of microscopical and chemical examina-
tion of the urine.
POINT 4.— Simple derangement of the urinary
secretion is not evidence of Kidney disease,
but is clear evidence that the Kidneys and
bladder require to be flushed and antisepti-
cally cleansed.
POINT 5 —Further evidence is indicated in
deposits or sediment, white or milky fluid,
pain in the region of the bladder, extending
into the back, thighs, &c., with execretory
scalding sensation.
POINT 6.— The systematic use of Kutnow’s
Powder will increase the efficiency of the
kidneys, keep the bladder clean, and prevent
gravel and stone.
POINT 7.— The main advantage of Kutnow’s
Powder is its entire freedom from sugar, an
essential point where Kidneys are concerned,
especially in Diabetes mellitus which is
characterised by the presence of sugar in the
urine.
POINT B.—The last point is the best point. It
is a recognised fact in medical circles that
Kutnovv’s Powder is the most efficient of all
uric acid Solvents and Eliminators, and in
this respect is a boon to suffering humanity;
it never fails to do good. Try it.
Important Questions
Do you value the health you possess, and do you wish to preserve it
in full manly strength and vigor ?
If you are unfortunately stricken with Kidney disease, are you
willing to try a comforting palliative ?
Would you, if suffering from derangement of the Liver, care to test a
certain remedy for it ?
Are you willing to try something which will improve your general
health and by no possibility do you harm ?
Is it your wish to eat well, digest well, feel well, work well, and
sleep well ?
Then get the grandest remedy in the world for Kidney trouble and
all Uric Acid diseases —-which is undoubtedly Kutnow’s Powder.
The leading physicians of London, Paris, Berlin and New York have
tested the power of Kutnow’s Powder as a natural solvent and
eliminant of Uric Acid.
The result has been a veritable triumph for Kutnow’s Powder. They
not only prescribe it in their private and hospital practice, but
use it personally as a guard against serious disease.
IMPORTANT WARNING!
Kutnow’s Powder can be obtained at all respectable Chemists,
Medicine Vendors and Stores throughout the world. It is necessary,
however, that you discriminate between fraudulent imitations, which
are absolutely worthless, and the genuine Kutnow’s Powder. Do
not be deceived, therefore, by the general appearance of a bottle, but
bear in mind that to be genuine every bottle must have the fac simile
signature, “ S. Kutnow and Co., Ld.,” and the registered trade-mark,
“ Hirschensprung, or Deer Leap,” on the carton and label on the
bottle. This signature and trade-mark should be seen in order to
obtain the

In the House of Representatives, the
leadership of the Opposition is a shifty office.
The nominal leader is G. Reid, who generally
isn t there. His deputy is J. Cook, who, when in
the House, keeps his pince-nez and his high un-
melodious voice constantly in front of the Speaker.
When both are absent, Wilks takes a turn. Should
Wilks not be available, Pumpkin Smith, Kelly,
or Dugald Thomson, or Lonsdale, or anyone drops
casually into the vacant chair, and questions the
Ministry regarding its intentions for next day, or
next year, or next century. The farce of “ Oppo-
sition leadership” reached its verv best on
the night when the end of the Postal Estimates
was reached. It was midnight, and Williemotor
Kelly was the only Oppositionist. Deakin moved
the adjournment of the House, and Kelly stepped
forward with an air of intense gravity and import-
ance, to inquire the order of business for next day.
For one brief glorious moment, Kelly was Oppo-
sition, Opposition leader, deputy leader, deputy
sub-leader, deputy assistant temporary leader, and
Opposition whip all rolled into one. He was the
most simultaneous Kelly imaginable.
“ Only a few old she-oaks,” said Minister
for Works Lee, ridiculing director Maiden’s
objection to giving up the Macquarie-street end
of Sydney Domain for the new library. “ Only a
few old she-oaks—there is nothing in that, be-
cause there are millions of them in the country,
and thousands are falling every day.” But
director Maiden’s point was that under those very
oak-trees the village Hampdens of the days before
Responsible Government used to hold their meet-
ings and discuss affairs of State. It was purely a
sentiment of “ Woodman, spare that tree.” This
country has just been bellowing about Trafalgar,
yet Nelson’s ship was only a few old pieces of
timber, and the great wooden figurehead of Nelson
which was proudly dabbed down on Trafalgar
Day in front of Sydney Exchange with a white-
washed wooden telescope in its wooden hand and
a flag round its tail, was only a hunk of fuel. But
that was imported sentiment, so Minister Lee
grovelled to it. The other is our own bit of
sentiment, and Lee’s thick timber intellect can
see nothing in it save a few old she-oaks.
“ Woodki” : Some sort of military training
is badly wanted, if for no other reason than to
counteract the verandah-post habit so common
among youths. I know several cases where
joining a militia regiment has made a sturdy,
manly young fellow out of a prospective cigarette-
fiend.

Mr. Hearne. Dear Sir, —I beg leave to draw your
attention to a remarkable Cure of Bronchitis, which disease
attacked me last May. It was looked upon by the doctor

looked about for something to try. I thought it a hard
thing, after 72 years’ experience of the ups and downs of
this life, to die in such an inglorious manner ; besides, I
was very poor in flesh, and thin, and this stimulated me
to make an effort for recovery, if only to improve my con-
dition. I saw your Bronchitis Cure advertised, and
thought I would give it a trial, so I sent to W. H. Harris,
chemist, Gill-street, Charters Towers, and got a small
bottle of it. I had scarcely finished it when I gave up all
thought of leaving my wife a widow, and hopefully looked
forwarded to many more delightful days of up and downs,
principally downs. The second small bottle caused the
Bronchitis and me to part company, greatly to its regret,
I opine. The third bottle stood me on my legs to receive
the congratulations of my friends.
I have been a resident of Charters Towers for 30 years,
and, being gifted with the spirit of good fellowship, have
not the least objection to you making any use of this
testimonial you think proper, as a means of relieving
others similarly situated to myself.
JOHN SHEPHERD,

The Cave-Man’s Wife.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WANDER not far, my child, my child !
The stars are dim and the winds are
wild,
And out in the dark the lions roam ;
Then bide thee here till the man comes home.
“ For the man is great in thew and limb.
And the tawny ones skulk wide of him ;
He went from here when the day was fresh,
And home he’ll come with the red deer’s flesh.
“ Pile the pine-knots,” and she piled them higher ;
And her hair was red as the red pine fire ;
And to and fro as she walked the cave,
It swept and rippled a lustrous wave.
“ Wander not far, my child, for see,
In the dark outside, they wait for thee;
Their eyes are green, their jaws are wide,
And they pace and prowl in the night outside.
“ Child, O my child, the hours grow long,
But fear no fear, for the man is strong;
He went from here when the moon was fresh,
And home he’ll come with the red deer’s flesh.
“ He said, ‘ Keep thou this child from hurt,
Lest they, the woodland things, alert
To whet their jaws should find and slay.’
He left his couch at break of day.
“ Only last tiight, with his knotted club
He slew the maneless lion’s cub ;
Wherefore, my child, it is clear to me
The maneless one doth wait for thee.
“Listen ! All night, though winds are high,
I catch her vengeful forest-cry ;
Then draw thee close to the good red fire,
And pile the pine-knots high and higher.
“Sweet, wouldst thou sleep? Then lay thy head
Here on these arms, and dread no dread,
The fire burns red—they dare not show
Within the circle of its glow.
“ Child, 0 my child, thy tears are warm
Upon the pillowing of my arm;
Thou dream’st! When lions fall asleep,
Do lions also sigh and weep ?
“ Eyes like the blue of bending skies,
Thou hast, my child, and in them lies
A light, a shade, a mystery
That bird nor beast doth share with thee*
“Yea, it is so, I think that they
Who roam the woods by night and day
With padded feet and tangled brow,
Are not, my child, the same as thou.
“ Hark ! it is he. Awake ! Awake !
I hear a neat cry in the brake;
The blood upon his arms is red ;
Hb shakes the forest with his tread.”
So to the fire he draws him near,
And casteth dbwn the new-slain deet,
And notes, as she piles the pine-knots higher,
That her hair is red as the red pine fire.
N.s.w.
RODERIO QUINN.
Only a Letter.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
MY Dear Man, —
Fancy your keeping your word about
not writing to me unless I wrote first!
My beautiful disposition prompts me to be
magnanimous, and you will be pleased with me—
if you ever get this.
I told you I would never come back to the old
life, and I will not. But that was a nice time
after the theatre when you came home with me
and drank whisky and soda—and talked. How
we talked ! But I meant no more than you did,
you nice big man. I asked you what you most
liked about me, and you said, “Your wit!”
Then you said, “ Your pretty hair,” and kissed it.
I can’t think why I let you, for I promised never
to take you seriously, and you said in a rather
annoyed tone, “ Thank Heaven, I have met one
sensible woman ! ” I had such pretty shoes and
stockings on that night; do you remember ?
Well, I am quite happy here —at least I think I
am. Big cities do go to one’s head, and as for
dinner in town, a hansom and a theatre —why,
they are absolute bliss. Men in towns are so
kind. At first, when they all held my arm
crossing streets and getting into trams, I took it
for a spasm of affection. Now I know it is sim-
ply kindness of heart. Last Friday I really had
a busy day. I’ve got a new hat—all roses. You
will like it—l mean you would if you ever saw it
—and a silk petticoat (you’ll never see that), and I
had morning tea with a nice, good man who takes
me seriously. We talked politics, travels, books,
and I really met with his approval—but I do like
a more personal note ; I haven’t got much time
for fun. I shall soon be rapidly descending the
hills of time, on legs, thank Heaven, not too Chip-
pendale for their burden. Lunch was more to my
fancy—another woman and two men. We agreed
that we all had a weakness for dainty food and
were cursed with no consciences.
At four we went on board a war boat to after-
noon tea, which was champagne. My officer had
such a nice cabin. I asked him if he had read
“ Captain Amyas, ” and he walked over to me and
looked so queer that I found it was getting late.
I haven't read the book myself. Sailors are so
excitable—aren’t they ? Then we had two han-
soms, and took a little carriage exercise ; our
hansom seemed so small ! Finally, we went to an
hotel for dinner —our dress-baskets were there,
and I wore a new and very becoming dress—pale-
pale green, with lovely iridescent trimming all over
the skirt. The body- was pretty—open at the
ba,ck, open at the front, and sleeves of the same.
Dinner was a lengthy feast, and we both found
the fashionable waist a little trying. We
went to a very amusing play, and the hero
was so like you, he made me feel delightfully
reminiscent.
Supper after the theatre, and hansoms home ;
the men wanted to come in and smoke, but we
were firm, and said we didn’t keep a Sailors’ Rest.
I’m glad you never wrote to me, but your letters
would be entertaining. You’re really rather
clever, you know, in your way ; and you’re so nice
and big. I always look upon you as a sort of
widower since you told me you had been true to
one woman for 10 years, with very occasional
lapses—you are so charmingly candid.
I met a man with a lovely voice the other night.
He sang my soul into thin air when he started
“TheSweetest Flower that Blows.” It reminded
me of that last evening when you sang it to me.
He was as nice as his voice, too; which is unusual.
And he had read all the books I like, and told me
I was like Dolly in Anthony Hope’s book. I
laughed and said, “ Ten years ago.”
He is coming to see me to-morrow night. He
is well-off, but too young. I shouldn’t like to be
accused of child-stealing at my age. O ! I’m
going to be very careful and say very little ; for
though open confession is good for the soul, it’s
very bad for the reputation.
I’m never going to write to you again ; and I
hope I shall never meet you. I really mean it !
But, if I do, will you have altered ? Or will you be
the amusing, cynical wretch you always were ? I
am cultivating conventionality and domesticated
habits, so could no longer appeal to you.
Good-bye—I’m never coming back !
K.
Along the Castlereagh.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
JACK REGAN returned to the Castle-
reagh after 10 years’ absence. The young-
sters had forgotten him ; even the old men
found it difficult to recall him. Still he had been
kept in their minds. Whenever the district was
inclined to forget Jack, his mother would produce
his last letter written in a far land, and read its
contents to her neighbors. There was always
money in Jack’s letters and he was always on the
eve of a big financial coup.
A small crowd gathered at widow Regan’s on
the night of her son’s home-coming. The lamp
was placed carefu. 1 " in the centre of the table
while the visitors rega h d him solemnly.
He sat near the fireplace, his stiff hands grip-
ping his knees. His face was paler than most
men’s and his eyes remained fixed on the opposite
wall.
“ How did ye get on, Jack lad, whin ye left
Australia? How did the sea fit ye?” Uncle
Jim leaned across the table while aunt silently
kicked his shins. “ How did ye get on, lad, when
the ship started a-rowlin’ ? ” added uncle.
“He never took much interest in ships,”
answered his mother, quickly. “He never was
fond of ships.”
A depressing silence fell upon the crowd. The
home-comer stared dumbly at the opposite wall;
his stiff hands seemed to cling desperately to his
knees.
Bobbie, who was not to be suppressed, edged
towards the fireplace, and looked into the face of
the widow’s son.
“ Have yer been up North, Mister Regan—by
the Gulf, I mean, where the pearl-fishers are ?
Did yer see anyone divin’ for pearl ? ”
Jack Regan stared at Bobbie with the eyes of a
man who had seen live things crawl on the floors
of Gehenna.
“ I’d a-gone North with a schooner only for
mother,” Bobbie added.
Casey in the far corner smoked thoughtfully.
“It do be harrd worrk thravellin’. Have ye been
in Morocco, Jack?” he asked, sorrowfully.
“Morocco has good sile, I hears. Phwat kind
iv plough do they use in Morocco, Jack ? ”
The widow looked at Casey half-pleadingly.
“It’s a longtime since he was in Morocco,” she
said, hurriedly, “ and he has such a bad memory.
Jack’s been in so many places. ”
She stole timidly to the kitchen and returned
quickly with a tray full of biscuits and thick slices
of cake.
“Araby is a foine counthry neow,” put in
Moloney, pleasantly. “The sile is soft, an’ the
beeutiful horses throt betune the palm threes.”
Moloney was a contractor, but bricks and mortar
had not effaced his ideas of landscape poetry.
“ Did ye call at Araby on yer way out, Jack ? ”
“Out—out,” repeated the widow’s son.
1 ‘ Where ?’ ’ He looked half-savagely at Moloney,
his eyes growing large, as though a clock or a
hammer were striking between his temples.
“How can he remember such places?” His
mother regarded Moloney somewhat anxiously.
“He has visited hundreds of places. The letters
he used to write from different parts of the world
are quite confusing. America is one of the
places.” She paused emphatically. “The United
States of America.”
Moloney’s baby crawled round to the fireplace
and clutched wildly at Jack Regan’s knees. He
looked down suddenly, and then slowly, labori-
ously his hands unlocked themselves, as though
bands of steel had hooked his fingers. With a
slow fear in his face, he bent and touched the
baby on the cheek. The child grew suddenly
rigid ; then howled bitterly.
The widow seemed to be holding herself, as
though in the presence of a tragedy. “Hush,
hush, deary.” She stooped and took the child in
her arms.
“ Poor Jack isn’t used to little ones. Those
foreigners have spoilt him.”
Casey and Bobbie ate all the cake. Moloney
was the first to leave, saying that he had to finish
a game of crib at the Swag and Pannikin. Uncle
Jim and aunt slipped away with Casey and his
wife.
Only Judy Oroonan stayed. She was 90 years
old, and she remembered Sydney in the convict
days. Her skin was almost black with age and
the heat of many summers. She nibbled a biscuit,
and her fingers trembled about her mouth with
ape-like insistence. She stared at the silent
Regan with the eyes of a bird.
“Ye deceived them all to-night, John,” she
half-whispered ; “ all but me and the little child.”
He did not look up; his hands had regained
the vice-grip on his knees.
“John, ye’ve been in gaol nearly half your life.
Ye’ve the cell-liobble in your walk. Look at your
shoulders ” —she tapped him slightly with her
finger—“the gaol-stoop, the convict’s back.”
She broke into querulous laughter. “Round an’
round the yard ye go, week, month and year.”
The widow Regan shrank from the terrible bird-
like eyes, the uplifted finger. “Round an’ round ye
went, John Regan, an’ through the night ye
heard the fool howling in the next cell. Yer ears
pricked for the padded footstep of the warder.
Ten years of it ; eh God ! an’ you a boy.”
She crooked her finger at the white-faced man,
Lien limped to the door and passed out.
“Mother.” He looked round slowly, and his
face kindled suddenly. “I’ll make a fresh start
to-morrow. If my bed’s ready I’ll turn in.”
Long after midnight, when the cattle bells were
wandering plaintively about the homestead, he
heard a tapping at his bedroom door.
“ What do you want, mother ? ” he asked.
“ What are cell-hobbles, Jack ?”
“ Oh, it’s a short stride ye drop into pottering
up and down a narrow cell. It might wear off.
Good-night, mother !”
“Good-night, Jack !”
ALBERT DORRINGTON.

Chanties.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WHEN the runner from the boarding-
house has thrown you on the ship,
Disregardin’ all your protests, to make
the Southern trip;
When your head’s a buzzin’ beehive with a
hornet’s nest on top,
And you’d pawn your soul for liquor, but you
cannot get a drop,
Then they boot you from the fo’castle—trampin’
round and round you go,
Heavin’ up the bloomin’ anchor to a chanty sad
and slow.
’ Way down Rio ! ’ Way down Rio !
Fare you well, my bonnie young girl—
We’re bound for the Rio Grande !
When a month at sea is over, and you’ve started
earning pay—
When you’ve worked out your advance-note, and
the crowd is feelin’ gay—
Then the dead horse will be hoisted to the loo’ard
mainyard arm
(It’s a sort o’ stuffed resemblance of the sort that’s
on a farm);
An’ first you drag it round the decks—it’s just a
shellback’s play—
And you sing the dead-horse chorus loud before
you hoist away.
Old man, old man ! your horse is dead.
And they say so. And they hope so.
Old man, old man ! your horse is dead.
Ah ! poor old man !
When she’s divin’ through the rollers, threshin’
round the bitter Horn,
When you curse the frozen canvas, and you
wonder why you’re born,
When your hands have lost their feeling, and your
toes begin to freeze,
And the hail is raising blisters through your slop-
chest dungarees,
When the foot-ropes slip from under, and the
lines refuse to coil,
Oh, it’s gaudy reefin’ tops’ls to the tune of “ Paddy
Doyle.”
Oh, we’ll heave aye, and we’ll haul aye,
An’ we’ll pay Paddy Doyle for his boots.
When the voyage is nearly ovqr, and her nose is
pointed back,
When the girls have got the tow-rope an’ they’re
hauling in the slack,
There is little time for sleeping, there is little
watch below,
For the westerlies are hummin’, and the barky
has to go ;
There is many a mile to travel to the docks at
London town,
So its “Set the main to’ gallant,” and we strike
up “ Sally Brown.”
I love a maid across the water,
Oh, ho, roll and go,
She’s Sally herself and Sally’s daughter,
And I spent my money on Sally Brown.
When you’re twistin’ up the river—when at last
you are in tow,
When you've trimmed the yards all shipshape-
given the kites their harbor stow,
When you’re packin’ up your oilskins, an’ your,
hook-pot, and your bed,
And wonderin’ how you’ll like it, with a roof
above your head,
When you’re thinkin’ of the pay day, when the
girls come flockin’ roun’,
You will catch yourself a hummin’ the song of
“ Blow Him Down.”
As I was a walkin’ down Paradise-street,
Hey, ho, blow the man down !
A pretty young lass I chanced to meet,
Oh, give us some time to blow the man down !
M.L.
TAIWA.
The Packman.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE Packman divided a lump of bread with
his rusty knife, and handed it over with
the billy.
“There,” he said, “yer kin git yerself outside
o’ that lot. There’s a pannikin behind yer.”
The Packman’s hands were unclean, and the
fuchsia firewood had made the tea smell vilely of
onions. But I wanted it too badly to be particu-
lar. For I had just done 15 miles of Otago hills
on creek-water and three pipes, long-drawn-out.
“ Did yer happen—happen ter see ’’—the Pack-
man was nosing round in the broken manuka
scrub—“ Did I bring my pick up the hill ?”
He had left it in the mouth of the drive, where
I had found him. I half got on my feet to go for
it; but he pushed me back.
“ Sit down, you wi’ yer wimbly legs. Men
ain’t what they used ter be. I’m wuth three o’
yer yet.”
He crashed through the scrub and over the
comb of the hill, with the oilskins flapping wetly
round his long bent figure. I heard him stumble
down among the rocks, splash into the race with
a grate of falling shingle behind him, and wade
up towards the tip-head. Followed a squeal of
delight, and a girl’s voice, high and excited :
“ Maudie ! Maudie ! Here’s a real digger in a
claim. Tell him to stand out in the middle while
I get the camera on him.”
The other voice was shriller yet. It explained
things to the Packman with six notes of exclama-
tion, and I heard him wading back.
“ I ain’t workin’ no more ter-day,” he said.
“Oh, but just for a few minutes. We want to
take your photograph. .. Maudie, hold him
there till I get the finder. . . Maudie ! ”
I wriggled to the edge, and looked over. The
Packman was clambering out of his race where
the water romped cleanly down to the rotten old
wooden boxes. The two girls were in the manuka
on the far side, fluttering, and taking uncertain
sights with a kodak.
“We’ll give you half-a-crown—five shillings.
I’ve been looking for a digger all day... Well,
you can get the back of him, anyway, Lil.”
The Packman scrambled hurriedly, but I heard
the click. Then he flopped down in the manuka
beside me, pulled off a boot, and emptied out the
water.
“Them petticuts is allers arter me,” he said.
“But they ain’t got much this time. A gel
caught me t’other day in me shirt an’ trouses —
’fore I’d mended ’em up, too.”
He jerked off his other boot with a swish, cast
his oilskins, snakewise, and sat in the sunshine,
steaming. He was a very old man, and the
wreck of what had once been a powerful man,
too. His big hook nose had sinuous veins down
it, and hair sprouted from his nostrils. The face
was mostly hair; but the cheeks and mouth showed
in-fallen, and the few broken teeth were yellow.
“Five bob isn’t bad for standing a couple of
minutes,” I suggested.
“Don’t want it,” said the Packman. “I can’t
never drink more’n four-ten at ago now—shouting
the crowd an’ all. An’ I got four-ten. That five
bob’d on’y go to Hagan, an’ he’s got ’nough out’r
me already. I ain’t goin’ ter set fur my photer in
that race, catchin’ bronchiter fur ter rake in five
bob fur Hagan.”
He searched the pockets of his tattered cardigan
with unsteady, knotted hands, and I asked diffi-
dently :
“ Do you mean that you drink every penny you
make? ”
“Not till I kin do a proper bust.” The Pack-
man brought out a stinking pipe and a plug. “ I
can’t git beyond four-ten now—not to remember
it. I used ter, mind yer. I used ter. When
there was two thousan’ men on the field, an’ me
packin’ from Gore reg’ler, I cud put down my
twenty in a night, an’ pull out fur Gore in the
morn’n. You couldn’t do that, young feller.”
“No,” I said humbly.
“ No. Nor none o’ this gen’ration can’t. Tell
yer, we cud stan’ up ter our stuff then. There
w r as Watty Mills, he carr’d a sack o’ flour from
the Old Gully ’crost the hill there ter the crik—a
matter o’ two mile—fer three drinks. ‘ Blow yer,’
he said, backin’ up ter it agin in the crik, ‘ I’ll
carr’ it back fur three more.’ He bruk hisself in-
side an’ it killed him, o’ course. But he ’ad his
fun.” He thrust out a lean hand and arm where
the muscles twisted like rata vines on a dead tree.
“ See the cronk way that sets at the elber ? It tuk
five men an’ a bobby ter do that.” He worked it
proudly with his other withered hand, and his old
crumpled body seemed to swell and gather
strength. “ Thirty poun’ I put down that night,
an’ it tuk five men an’ a bobby ter holt me. Then
Marsh, ’e got a handcuff on, an’ that’s the way
this was did. But they’d never ’a’ got me—they’d
never ’a’ got ine if I’d ’ad room ter swing out.
Sherriff’s bar wasn’t no place fur men ter fight
in.”
He drew at his pipe till the dottle glowed red
as his eyes ; and his long nose and jaws worked as
if he was biting on a sweet memory. He was
quite certainly a wicked old heathen, and horri-
bly dirty; but his very decay was charged with
the strength of a man who had lived his life, every
inch. I said something about his present work
being rough on him at his age.
“Now, you jes’ look here,” he said, beating one
long, crooked finger on my knee. “ I’ll jes’ con-
vix yer. If I could booze day an’ night I’d be
dead in a month. No fun in that, eh ? So I gets
my limit an’ wades in, an’ finishes off, an’ stands
out ter it agin. That kips me ’ealthy. That an’
tent-sleepin’. They other chaps tuk ter house-
sleepin’ an’ died. An’ wives ; p’r’aps that ’elped
it. I tuk my fun wi’ men allers. Wi’ men.” He
sat scratching his scraggy throat with his long,
dirtj nails, and his nose worked harder. It was
a whole savage, glorious life of the Middle Ages
that he was looking into. I had no part in it. I
stood up, and shook hands.
.“I’m going up the Old Man Range,” I told
him. “ And if 1 get the billet I’m looking for,
perhaps 111 be back in the township next week to
hunt you up.”
“Don’t come ’fore Thursday, then,” he said.
“I ’spect ter be fit agin by Thursday.”
“Do you mean?”
“ I tolt yer I got my four-ten, didn’t I ? What
d’yer take me for ? No need fur thanks. I’d ’a’
had butter if I knowed yer was cornin’. Good-
day. ’
* * * 9
It was daybreak next Friday morning when I
rode through the empty streets of the township
with word from my new boss about some fat
cattle. Hagan’s pub was still shut. But the
back-yard lay up to the street fence, and over the
palings I saw the Packman sitting beside all the
dead marines that Hagan had used between last
night and ten years back.
The Packman was going through them syste-
matically, meeting each bottle-mouth with his
own, and tilting his head back. I ranged up
alongside.
“Rather a mixed drink that, isn’t it?” I asked.
The Packman lowered a bottle, spat out a fly,
and looked at me. His eyes were dulled.
“ If I’d had that extry five bob Hagan’s wife’d
be wearin’ a noo hat ter-morrer,” he said. “There
was suthin’ wrong wi’ the whisky this time, an’ I
never got no fight out o’ it to remember.” He
cleared the mouth of a Hollands bottle with his
cuff, and shook his head. “There ain’t no good
’nless yer hev the rememb’rin’ ter chew on. You
lay up yer fights agin yer on’y got the remem-
b’rin'.” He came to his feet unsteadily, and his
face looked dirtier and more withered in the gay
sunlight. “ Stoppin’ in fur breakfas’ ?” he asked.
I wasn t. But I waited till the bar opened.
Then I took the Packman in, gave him a drink,
and set his feet towards his claim. Hagan told
me that the Packman was breaking up.
“ Good day. he said as I left him on the bridge.
“ I s’pose 1 11 hev ter make it four after this, or go
ter Mosely. There’s suthin’ wrong wi’ Hagan’s
stuff. I never got no fun out o’ it. There warn’t
a chap there’d tight me.”
“ Hadn't you better ring it off altogether ?” I
sugge-ted.
The Packman looked at me, and wiped his nose
with the back of his hand.
“ You young fellers,” he said, “ you don’t know
what life is. You’re tim’rus. You won’t hev no
fights ter chaw on when yer gits old. Good day.”
G. B. LANCASIER.
